a indigo planter by maori 1878 -lrb- note : some word in this book have a macron over a vowel a macron be a punctuation mark -lrb- - -rrb- and be represent herein as -lrb- = a -rrb- -lrb- = e -rrb- or -lrb- = o -rrb- -rrb- preface i go home in 1875 for a few month after some twelve year ' residence in indium what first suggest the writing of such a book as this be the amazing ignorance of ordinary indian life betray by people at home the question ask I about indium and we daily life there show in many case such a utter want of knowledge that i think surely there be room here for a chatty familiar unpretentious book for friend at home give a account of we every-day life in indium we labor and amusement we toil and relaxation and a few picture of we ordinary daily surroundings in the far far east such then be the design of my book i want to picture to my reader planter life in the mofussil or country district of indium to tell they of we hunting shooting fishing and other amusement to describe we work we play and matter-of-fact incident in we daily life to describe the native as they appear to we in we intimate every-day dealings with they to illustrate they manners custom disposition observance and saying so far as these bear on we own social life i be no politician no learn ethnologist no sage theorist i simply try to describe what i have see and hope to enlist the attention and interest of my reader in my reminiscence of sport and labor in the village and jungle on the far off frontier of nepaul i have try to express my meaning as far as possible without anglo-indian and hindustani word where these have be use as at time they could not but be i have give a synonymous word or phrase in english so that all my friend at home may know my meaning i know that my friend will be lenient to my fault and even the sternest critic if he look for it may find some pleasure and profit in my page jas inglis contents chapter i province of behar -- boundary -- province of behar -- boundary -- general description -- district of chumparun -- mooteeharree -- the town and lake -- native house -- the planter ' club -- legoulie among the many beautiful and fertile province of indium none can i think much excel that of behar for richness of soil diversity of race beauty of scenery and the energy and intelligence of its inhabitant stretch from the nepaul hill to the far distant plain of gya with the gunduch bogmuttee and other noble stream water its rich bosom and swell with they tribute the stately gange it include every variety of soil and climate and its various race with they strange costume creed and custom might afford material to fill volume the northern part of this splendid province follow the nepaulese boundary from the district of goruchpore on the north to that of purneah on the south in the forest and jungle along this boundary line live many strange tribe whose custom and even they name and language be all but unknown to the english public strange wild animal dispute with these aborigine the possession of the gloomy jungle solitude great tree of wondrous dimension and strange foliage rear they stately head to heaven and be mat and entwine together by creeper of huge size and tenacious hold to the south and east vast billow of golden grain roll in successive undulation to the mighty gange the sacred stream of the hindoo innumerable village nestle amid grove of plantain and feathery rustling bamboo send up they wreath of pale gray smoke into the still warm air at frequent interval the steely blue of some lovely lake where thousand of water-fowl disport themselves reflect from its polished surface the sheen of the noonday sun great mass of mango wood shew a sombre outline at interval and here and there the tower chimney of a indigo factory pierce the sky government road and embankment intersect the face of the country in all direction and vast sheet of the indigo plant refresh the eye with they plain of live green form a grateful contrast to the hard dry sun-baked surface of the stubble field where the rice crop have rustle in the breeze of the past season in one of the loveliest and most fertile district of this vast province namely chumparun i begin my experience as a indigo planter chumparun with its subdistrict of bettiah lie to the north of tirhoot and be bound all along its northern extent by the nepaul hill and forest when i join my appointment as assistant on one of the large indigo concern there there be not more than about thirty european resident altogether in the district the chief town mooteeharree consist of a long _ bazaar _ or market street beautifully situate on the bank of a lovely lake some two mile in length from the main street with its quaint little shop sheltered from the sun by makeshift verandah of tattered sack weather-stained shingle or rot bamboo mat various little lane and alley diverge lead one into a collection of tumble-down and ruinous hut set up apparently by chance and present the most incongruous appearance that could possibly be conceive one or two _ pucca _ house that be house of brick and masonry shew where some wealthy bunneah -lrb- trader -rrb- or usurious banker live but the majority of the house be of the usual mud and bamboo order there be a small thatched hut where the meal be cook and where the owner and he family could sleep during the rain another smaller hut at right angle to this give shelter to the family goat or if they be rich enough to keep one the cow all round the village in indium there be generally large patch of common where the village cow have free rights of pasture and all who can keep either a cow or a couple of goat the milk from which form a welcome addition to they usual scanty fare in this second hut also be store as much fuel consist of dry cow-dung straw maize-stalk leave etc as can be collect and a ragged fence of bamboo or _ rahur _ -lrb- 1 -rrb- stalk enclose the two unprotected side thus form inside a small court quadrangle or square this court be the native 's _ sanctum sanctorum _ it be keep scrupulously clean be sweep and garnish religiously every day in this the woman prepare the rice for the day 's consumption here they cut up and clean they vegetable or they fish when the adjacent lake have be drag by the village fisherman here the produce of they little garden capsicum indian corn onion or potato -- perchance turmeric ginger or other root or spice -- be dry and make ready for store in the earthen sun-baked repository for the reception of such produce appertaining to each household here the child play and be wash and tend here the maiden comb out she long black hair or decorate she bronzed visage with streak of red paint down the nose and a little antimony on the eyelid or myrtle juice on the finger and toe nail here too the matron or the withered old crone of a grandmother spin she cotton thread or in the old scriptural hand-mill grind the corn for the family flour and meal and the father and the young man -lrb- when the sun be high and hot in the heaven -rrb- take they noonday _ siesta _ or the day 's labor over cower round the smoking dung fire of a cold winter night and discuss the price rule in the bazaar the rise of rent or the last village scandal in the middle of the town and surround by a spacious fenced-in compound which slope gently to the lake stand the planter ' club a large low roofed bungalow with a roomy wide verandah in front here we meet when business or pleasure bring we to ` the station ' here be hold we annual ball or a occasional public dinner party to the north of the club stand a long range of barrack-looking building which be the opium godown where the opium be collect and store during the season face this again and at the extremity of the lake be the district jail where all the rascal of the surrounding country be confine its high wall tip at interval by a red puggree and flash bayonet wherever a jail sepoy keep he ` lonely watch ' near it sheltered in a grove of shady tree be the court house where the collector and magistrate daily dispense justice or where the native _ moonsiff _ disentangle knotty point of law here too come the session judge once a month or so to try criminal case and mete out justice to the law-breaker we have thus a small european element in we ` station ' consist of we magistrate and collector whose large and handsome house be build on the bank of another and yet lovelier lake which join the town lake by a narrow stream or strait at its southern end a opium agent a district superintendent of police and last but not least a doctor these form the official population of we little ` station ' there be also a nice little church but no resident pastor and behind the town lay a quiet churchyard rich in the dust of many a pioneer who far from home and friend have here be gather to he silent rest about twelve mile to the north and near the nepaul boundary be the small military station of legoulie here there be always a native cavalry regiment the officer of which be frequent and welcome guest at the factory in the district and be always glad to see they indigo friend at they mess in cantonment at rettiah still further to the north be a rich rajah 's palace where a resident european manager dwelt and have for he sole society a assistant magistrate who transact the executive and judicial work of the subdistrict these with some twenty-five or thirty indigo manager and assistant compose the whole european population of chumparun never be there a more united community we be all like brother each know all the rest the assistant frequently visit each other and the manager be kind and considerate to they subordinate hunting party be common cricket and hockey match be frequent and in the cold weather which be we slackest season fun frolic and sport be the order of the day we have a annual race meet when all the crack horse of the district meet in keen rivalry to test they pace and endurance during this high carnival we live for the most part under canvass and have friend from far and near to share we hospitality in a future chapter i must describe we racing meet -lrb- 1 -rrb- the _ rahur _ be a kind of pea grow not unlike we english broom in appearance it be sow with the maize crop during the rain and garner in the cold weather it produce a small pea which be largely use by the native and form the nutritive article of diet know as _ dhall _ chapter ii my first charge -- how we get we land -- we home farm -- system of farming -- collection of rent -- the planter 's duty my first charge be a small outwork of the large factory seeraha it be call puttihee there be no bungalow that be there be no regular house for the assistant but a little one-roomed hut build on the top of the indigo vat serve I for a residence it have neither door nor window and the rain use to beat through the room while the eaves be inhabit by countless swarm of bat who in the evening flash backwards and forwards in ghostly rapid flight and be a most intolerable nuisance to give some idea of the duty of a indigo assistant i must explain the system on which we get we land and how we grow we crop water of course be a _ sine qua non _ the first object in select a site for a factory be to have water in plenty contiguous to the propose building consequently puttihee be build on the bank of a very pretty lake shape like a horseshoe and cover with water lily and broad-leaved green aquatic plant the lake be keep by the native proprietor as a fish preserve and literally teem with fish of all sort shape and size i have not be long at puttihee before i have erect a staging lead out into deep water and many a happy hour i have spend there with my three or four rod out pull in the finny inhabitant have get water and a site the next thing be to get land on which to grow you crop by purchase by get a long lease or otherwise you become possess of several hundred acre of the land immediately surround the factory of course some factory will have more and some less as circumstance happen this land however be peculiarly factory property it be in fact a sort of home farm and go by the name of _ zeraat _ it be plough by factory bullock work by factory coolie and be altogether apart and separate from the ordinary land hold by the ryot and work by they -lrb- a ryot mean a cultivator -rrb- in most factory the zeraat be farm in the most thorough manner many now use the light howard 's plough and apply quantity of manure the field extend in vast unbroken plain all round the factory the land be work and pulverise and replough and harrow and clean till not a lump the size of a pigeon 's egg be to be see if necessary it be carefully weed several time before the crop be sow and in fact a fine clean stretch of zeraat in tirhoot or chumparun will compare most favourably with any field in the highest farming district of england or scotland the ploughing and other farm labor be do by bullock a staff of these vary of course with the amount of land under cultivation be keep at each factory for they support a certain amount of sugar-cane be plant and in the cold weather carrot be sown and _ gennara _ a kind of millet and maize both maize and gennara have broad green leaf and long juicy succulent stalk they grow to a good height and when cut up and mix with chop straw and carrot form a most excellent feed for cattle besides the bullock each factory keep up a staff of generally excellent horse for the use of the assistant or manager on which he ride over he cultivation and look generally after the farm some of the native subordinate also have pony or cabool horse or country-bred and for the feed of these animal some few acre of oats be sow every cold season in most factory too when any particular bit of the zeraat get exhaust by the constant repetition of indigo crop a rest be give it by take a crop of oil seed or oats off the land the oil seed usually sow be mustard or rape the oil be useful in the factory for oil the screw or the machinery and for other purpose the factory road through the zeraat be keep in most perfect order many of they be metal the ditch be clean once a year all thistle and weed by the side of the road and ditch be ruthlessly cut down the edge of all the field be neatly trim and cut useless tree and clump of jungle be cut down and in fact the zeraat round a factory shew a perfect picture of orderly thrift careful management and neat scientific and elaborate farming have get the zeraat the next thing be to extend the cultivation outside the land in indium be not as with we at home parcel out into large farm there be wealthy proprietor raja baboo and so on who hold vast tract of land either by grant or purchase or hereditary succession but the tenant be literally the child of the soil wherever a village nestle among its plantain or mango grove the land be parcel out among the villager a large proprietor do not reckon up he farm as a landlord at home would do but he count he village in a village with a thousand acre belong to it there might be 100 or even 200 tenant farm the land each petty villager would have he acre or half acre or four or five or ten or twenty acre as the case might be he hold this by a ` tenant right ' and can not be dispossess as long as he pay he rent regularly he can sell he tenant right and the purchaser on pay the rent become the _ bona fide _ possessor of the land to all intent and purpose if the average rent of the village land be let I say one rupee eight anna a acre the rent roll of the 1000 acre would be 1500 rupee out of this the government land revenue come certain deduction have to be make -- some ryot may be defaulter the village temple or the village brahmin may have to get something the road-cess have to be pay and so on take everything into account you arrive at a pretty fair view of what the rental be if the proprietor of the village want a loan of money or if you offer to pay he the rent by half-yearly or quarterly instalment you take all the risk of collect in turn from each ryot individually he be often only too glad to accept you offer and give you a lease of the village for whatever term may be agree on you step in as virtually the landlord and the ryot have to pay they rent to you in many case by careful management by remeasure land settle doubtful boundary and generally work up the estate you can much increase the rental and actually make a profit on you bargain with the landlord this department of indigo work be call zemindaree have then get the village in lease you summon in all you tenant shew they they rent account arrange with they for the punctual payment of they and get they to agree to cultivate a certain percentage of they land in indigo for you this percentage vary very considerably in some place it be one acre in five in some one in twenty it all depend on local circumstance you select the land you give the seed but the ryot have to prepare the field for sowing he have to plough weed and reap the crop and deliver it at the factory for the indigo he get so much per acre the price be as near as possible the average price of a acre of ordinary produce : take the average out-turn and price of say ten year it use formerly to be much less but the ryot nowadays get nearly double for he indigo what he get some ten or fifteen year ago and this although price have not rise for the manufactured article and the price of labor store machinery live stock etc have more than double in some part the ryot get pay so much per bundle of plant deliver at the vat but generally in behar at least in north behar he be pay so much per acre or _ beegah _ i use the word acre as be more easily understand by people at home than beegah the beegah vary in different district but be generally about two-third of a acre when he rent account then come to be make out the ryot get credit for the price of he indigo grow and deliver and this very often suffice not only to clear he entire rent but to leave a margin in hard cash for he to take home before the beginning of the indigo season however he come into the factory and take a cash advance on account of the indigo to be grow this be often a great help to he enable he to get he seed for he other land perhaps plough or to buy a cart or clothes for the family or to replace a bullock that may have die or to help to give a marriage portion to a son or daughter that he want to get marry you will thus see that we have cultivation to look after in all the village round about the factory which we can get in lease the ryot in return for he cash advance agree to cultivate so much indigo at a certain price for which he get credit in he rent such shortly be we indigo system in some village the ryot will estimate for we without we have the lease at all and without take advance he grow the indigo as he would grow any other crop as a pure speculation if he have a good crop he can get the price in hard cash from the factory and a great deal be grow in this way in both purneah and bhaugulpore this be call _ kooskee _ as against the system of advance which be call _ tuccaree _ the planter then have to be constantly over he village look out for good land give up bad field and take in new one he must watch what crop grow best in certain place he must see that he do not take land where water may lodge and on the other hand avoid those that do not retain they moisture he must attend also to the state of the other crop generally all over he cultivation as the punctual payment of rent depend largely on the state of the crop he must have he eye open to everything go on be able to tell the probable rent-roll of every village for mile around know whether the ryot be lazy and discontented or be industrious and hard-working up in the early morning before the hot blaze sun have climb on high he be off on he trusty nag through he zeraat with he greyhound and terrier pant behind he as he near a village the farm-servant in charge of that particular bit of cultivation come out with a low salaam to report progress or complain that so-and-so be not work up he field as he ought to do over all the land he go see where re-ploughing be necessary order harrowing here weed there or roll somewhere else he see where the ditch need deepen where the road want levelling or widen where a new bridge will be necessary where land must be throw up and new one take in he know nearly all he ryot and have a kind word for every one he pass ask after they crop they bullock or they land rouse up the indolent give a cheerful nod to the industrious order this one to be bring in to settle he account or that one to make greater haste with the preparation of he land that he may not lose he moisture in fact he have he hand full till the mount sun warn he to go back to breakfast and so with a rattle burst after a jackal or fox he get back to he bungalow to bathe dress and break he fast with fowl cutlet and curry and rice wash down with a wholesome tumbler of bass chapter iii how to get we crop -- the ` dangurs ' -- farm servant and they duty -- kassee rai -- hoe -- plough -- ` oustennie ' -- coolie at work -- sowing -- difficulty the plant have to contend with -- weed have now get we land water and building -- which latter i will describe further on -- the next thing be to set to work to get we crop manufacture be finish and the crop all cut by the beginning or middle of October when the annual rain be over it be of importance to have the land dig up as early as possible that the rich moisture on which the successful cultivation of the crop mainly depend may be secure before the hot west wind and strong sun of early spring lick it up attach to every factory be a small settlement of labourer belong to a tribe of aborigine call _ dangurs _ these originally i believe come from chota nagpoor which seem to have be they primal home they be a cheerful industrious race have a distinct language of they own and only intermarry with each other long ago when there be no post carriage to the hill and but few road the dangurs be largely employ as dale runner or postmen some few of they settle with they family on land near the foot of the hill in purneah and gradually other make they way northwards until now there be scarcely a factory in behar that have not its dangur tolum or village the man be tractable merry-hearted and faithful the woman betray none of the exaggerated modesty which be characteristic of hindoo woman generally they never turn aside and hide they face as you pass but look up to you with a merry smile on they countenance and exchange greeting with the utmost frankness in a future chapter i may speak at greater length of the dangurs at present it suffice to say that they form a sort of appanage to the factory and be in fact treat as part of the permanent staff each dangur when he marry get some grass and bamboo from the factory to build a house and a small plot of ground to serve as a garden for which he pay a very small rent or in many instance nothing at all in return he be always on the spot ready for any factory work that may be go on for which he have he daily wage some factory pay by the month but the general custom be to charge for hoe by piece-work and during manufacture when the work be constant there be pay a monthly wage in the close foggy morning of October and November long before the sun be up the dangur be hard at work in the zeraat turn up the soil with they _ kodaly _ -lrb- a kind of cut hoe -rrb- and you can often hear they merry voice rise through the mist as they crack joke with each other to enliven they work or troll one of they quaint native ditty they be preside over by a ` mate ' generally one of the oldest man and first settler in the village if he have have a large family he son look up to he and he son-in-law obey he order with the utmost fealty the ` mate ' settle all dispute present all grievance to the _ sahib _ and all order be give through he the indigo stubble which have be leave in the ground be perhaps about a foot high and as they cut it out they wife and child come to gather up the stick for fuel and this of course also help to clean the land by eleven o'clock when the sluggish mist have be dissipate by the ray of the scorching sun the day 's labor be nearly conclude you will then see the swarthy dangur with he favorite child on he shoulder wend he way back to he hut follow by he comely wife carry he hoe and a tribe of little one bring up the rear each carry bundle of the indigo stubble which the industrious father have dig up during the early hour of morning in the afternoon out come the _ hengha _ which be simply a heavy flat log of wood with a v shape cut or groove all along under its flat surface to each end of the hengha a pair of bullock be yoke and two man stand on the log and hold on by the bullock ' tail it be slowly drag over the field wherever the hoeing have be go on the lump and clod be catch in the groove on the under surface and drag along and break up and pulverize and the whole surface of the field thus get harrow down and form a homogeneous mass of light friable soil cover the weed and dirt to let they rot expose the least surface for the wind and heat to act on and thus keep the moisture in the soil now be a busy time for the planter up early in the cold raw fog he be over he zeraat long before dawn and round by he outlying village to see the ryot at work in they field to each eighty or a hundred acre a man be attach call a _ tokedar _ he duty be to rouse out the ryot see the hoe and plough at work get the weed do and be responsible for the state of the cultivation generally he will probably have two village under he if the village with its land be very extensive of course there will be a tokedar for it alone but frequently a tokedar may have two or more village under he charge in the village the head man -- generally the most influential man in the community -- also act with the tokedar help he to get plough bullock and coolie when these be wanted and under he the village _ chowkeydar _ or watchman see that stray cattle do not get into the field that the road bridge and fence be not damage and so on over the tokedar again be zillahdar a ` zillah ' be a small district there may be eight or ten village and three or four tokedar under a zillahdar the zillahdar look out for good land to change for bad one where this be necessary and where no objection be make by the farmer see that the tokedar do they work properly report rain blight locust and other visitation that might injure the crop watch all that go on in he zillah and make he report to the planter whenever anything of importance happen in he particular part of the cultivation over all again come the jemadar -- the head man over the whole cultivation -- the planter 's right-hand man he be generally a old experienced and trust servant he know all the land for mile round and the peculiar soil and product of all the village far and near he can tell what land grow the best tobacco what land be free from inundation what free from drought the temper of the inhabitant of each village and the history of each farm where be the best plough the best bullock and the best farming in what village you get most coolie for weed where you can get the best cart the best straw and the best of everything at the most favourable rate he come up each night when the day 's work be do and get he order for the morrow you be often glad to take he advice on sowing reap and other operation of the farm he know where the plant will ripen earliest and where the leaf will be thickest and to he you look for satisfaction if any screw get loose in the outside farm-work he generally accompany you in you morning ride show you you new land consult with you about throw up exhaust field and be generally a sort of farm-bailiff or confidential land-steward where he be a honest intelligent and loyal man he take half the care and work off you shoulder such man be however rare and if not very closely look after they be apt to abuse they position and often harass the ryot needlessly look more to the feathering of they own nest than the advancement of you interest the only jemadar i feel i could thoroughly trust be my first one at parewah a old rajpoot call kassee rai he be a fine ruddy-faced white-haired old man as independent and straightforward a old farmer as you could meet anywhere and i never have reason to regret take he advice on any matter i never find he out in a lie or in a dishonest or underhand action though over seventy year of age he be upright as a dart he could not keep up with I when we go out ride over the field but he would be out the whole day over the land and be always the first at he work in the morning and the last to leave off at night the ryot all love he and would do anything for he and when poor old kassee die the third year he have be under I i feel as if a old friend have go i never speak a angry word to he and i never have a fault to find with he when the hoeing have be finish in zeraat and zillah and all the upturned soil batten down by the _ hengha _ the next thing be to commence the plough you ploughman be mostly low caste man -- doosadhs churnar moosahurs gwallah _ et hoc genus omne _ the indian plough so like a big misshapen wooden pickaxe have often be describe it however turn up the light soft soil very well consider its pretension and those make in the factory workshop be generally heavier and sharper than the ordinary village plough we bullock too be strong and well feed the ploughing in the zeraat be generally good the ploughing be immediately follow up by the _ hengha _ which again triturate and break up the clod roll the stick leave and grass root together bring the refuse and dirt to the surface and again level the soil and prevent the wind from take away the moisture the land now look fine and fresh and level but very dirty a host of coolie be put on the field with small stick in they hand all the dangur woman and child be there with man woman and child of all the poorest class from the village round whom the attraction of wages or the exertion of headmen tokedar and zillahdar have bring together to earn they daily bread with the stick they beat and break up every clod leave not one behind the size of a walnut they collect all the refuse weed and dirt which be heap up and burn on the field and so they go on till the zeraat look as clean as a nobleman 's garden and you would think that surely this must satisfy the fastidious eye of the planter but no we work be not half begin yet it be rather a strange sight to see some four or five hundred coolie squat in a long irregular line chattering laugh shout or squabbling a dense cloud of dust rise over they and through the dim obscurity one hear the ceaseless sound of the thwack thwack as they stick rattle on the ground white dust lie thick on each swarthy skin they face be like face in a pantomime there be the flash eye and the grin row of white tooth all else be cloud in thick layer of dust with black spot and stencilling show here and there like a picture in sepia and chalk as they near the end of the field they redouble they thwack shuffle along like land-crab and while the mate peon and tokedar shout at they to encourage they they raise a roar loud enough to wake the dead the dust rise in denser cloud the noise be deafen a regular mad hurry-scurry a wild boisterous scramble ensue and amid much chaffing noise and laughter they scramble off again to begin another length of land and so the day 's work go on the planter have to count he coolie several time a-day or they would cheat he some come in the morning get count and they name put on the roll and then go off till paytime come round some come for a hour or two and send a relative in the evening when the pice be be pay out to get the wage of work they have not do all be pay in pice -- little copper bit of coin average about sixty-four to the rupee however you soon come to know the coolie by sight and after some experience be rarely ` take in ' but many young beginner get ` do ' most thoroughly till they become accustomed to the trick of the artless and unsophisticated coolie the type of feature along a line of coolie be as a rule a very forbid and degrade one they be mostly of the very poorest class many of they be plainly half silly or wholly idiotic not a few be deaf and dumb other be cripple or deform and number be leprous and scrofulous number of they be afflict in some district with goitre cause probably by bad drinking water all have a pinched withered wan look that tell of hard work and insufficient fare it be a pleasure to turn to the end of the line where the dangur woman and boy and girl generally take they place here be the loudest laughter and the sauciest face the child be merry chubby fat thing with well-distended stomach and pleasant look a merry smile ripple over they broad fat cheek as they slyly glance up at you the woman -- with huge earring in they ear and a perfect load of heavy brass ring on they arm -- chatter away make believe to be shy and show off a thousand coquettish air they very toe be bedizen with brass ring and long festoon of red white and blue bead hang pendent round they neck in the evening the line be re-formed before the bungalow a huge bag of copper coin be produce the old lallah or writer with spectacles on nose squat down in the middle of the assemble coolie and as each name be call the mate count out the pice and make it over to the coolie who forthwith hurry off to get he little purchase make at the village bunneah 's shop and so on a poor supper of parched pea or boil rice with no other relish but a pinch of salt the poor coolie crawl to bed only to dream of more hard work and scanty fare on the morrow poor thing a village coolie have a hard time of it during the hot month if rice be cheap and plentiful he can jog along pretty comfortably but when the cold night come on and he cower in he wretched hut hungry half naked cold and wearied he be of all object most pitiable it be however a fact little creditable to he more prosperous fellow-countryman that he get better pay for he labor in connection with factory work than he do in many case for task force on he by the lead ryot of the village in connection with they own field -lrb- illustration : coolie 's hut -rrb- this first cleaning of the field -- or as it be call _ oustennie _ -- be finish the land be all again re-ploughed re-harrowed and then once more re-cleaned by the coolie till not a weed or spot of dirt remain and till the whole surface be uniformly soft friable moist and clean we have now some breathing time and as this be the most enjoyable season of the year when the day be cool and roar wood fire at night remind we of home we hunt visit race dance and generally enjoy ourselves should heavy rain fall as it sometimes do about christmas and early in February the whole cultivation get beat down and cake over in such a case amusement must for a time be throw aside till all the land have be again re-ploughed of course we be never wholly idle there be always rent to collect matter to adjust in connexion with we village and tenantry law-suit to recover bad debt to enforce contract or protect manorial or other rights -- but generally speak when the land have be prepare we have a slack season or breathing time for a month or so arrangement have be make for the supply of seed which generally come from about the neighborhood of cawnpore as February draw near we make preparation for begin we sowing February be the usual month but it depend on the moisture and sometimes sowing may go on up till may and June in purneah and bhaugulpore where the cultivation be much rougher than in tirhoot the sowing be do broadcast and in bengal the sowing be often do upon the soft mud which be leave on the bank of the river at the retire of the annual flood in tirhoot however where the high farming i have be try to describe be practice the sowing be do by means of drill drill be get out overhaul and put in thorough repair bag of seed be send out to the village advance for bullock be give to the ryot and on a certain day when all seem favourable -- no sign of rain or high wind -- the drill be set at work and day and night the work go on till all the cultivation have be sow as the drill go along the hengha follow close behind cover the seed in the furrow and once again it be put over till the field be all level shine and clean wait for the first appearance of the young soft shoot these after some seven nine or perhaps fifteen day accord to the weather begin to appear in long line of delicate pale yellowish green this be a most anxious time should rain fall the whole surface of the earth get cake and hard and the delicate plant burn out or be chafe against the hard surface crust it withers and die if the wind get into the east it bring a peculiar blight which settle round the leaf and collar of the stem of the young plant choke it and sweep off mile and mile of it if hot west wind blow the plant get black discolor burn up and dead a south wind often bring caterpillar -- at least this pest often make its appearance when the wind be southerly but as often as not caterpillar find they way to the young plant in the most mysterious manner -- no one know whence they come daily nay almost hourly report come in from all part of the zillah : now you hear of ` lahee ' blight on some field now it be ` ihirka ' scorching or ` pilooa ' caterpillar in some place the seed may have be bad or cover with too much earth and the plant come up straggle and thin if there be abundant moisture this must be re-sow in fact there be never-ending anxiety and work at this season but when the plant have get into ten or fifteen leaf and be a inch or two high the most critical time be over and one begin to think about the next operation namely weed the coolie be again in requisition each come arm with a _ coorpee _ -- this be a small metal spatula broad-pointed with which they dig out the weed with amazing deftness sometimes they may inadvertently take out a single stem of indigo with the weed : the eye of the mate or tokedar espy this at once and the careless coolie be treat to a volley of hindoo billingsgate in which all he relation be abused to the seventh generation by the time the first weeding be finish the plant will be over a foot high and if necessary a second weeding be then give after the second weeding and if any rain have fall in the interim the plant will be fully two foot high it be now a noble-looking expanse of beautiful green wave foliage as the wind ruffle its myriad of leave the sparkle of the sunbeam on the undulating mass produce the most wonderful combination of light and shade feathery spray of a delicate pale green curl gracefully all over the field it be like a ocean of vegetation with billow of rich color chase each other and blending in harmonious hue the whole field look a perfect oasis of beauty amid the surround dull brown tint of the season it be now time to give the plant a light touch of the plough this ease the soil about the root let in air and light tend to clean the undergrowth of weed and give it a great impetus the operation be call _ bedaheunee _ by the beginning of June the tiny red flower be peep from its leafy sheath the lower leaf be turn yellowish and crisp and it be almost time to begin the grandest and most important operation of the season the manufacture of the dye from the plant to this you have be look forward during the cold raw foggy day of November when the plough be hard at work -- during the hot fierce wind of march and the still sultry breathless early day of June when the air be so still and oppressive that you could scarcely breathe these sultry day be the lull before the storm -- the pause before the moisture-laden cloud of the monsoon roll over the land ` rugged and brown ' and the wild rattle of thunder and the lurid glare of quiver never-ceasing lightning herald in the annual rain the manufacture however deserve a chapter to itself -lrb- illustration : indigo beat vat -rrb- chapter iv manufacture of indigo -- load the vat -- beating -- boil strain and press -- scene in the factory -- fluctuation of produce -- chemistry of indigo indigo be manufacture solely from the leaf when arrangement have be make for cut and cart the plant from the field the vat and machinery be all make ready and a day be appoint to begin ` mahye ' or manufacture the apparatus consist of first a strong serviceable pump for pump up water into the vat : this be now mostly do by machinery but many small factory still use the old persian wheel which may be shortly describe as simply a endless chain of bucket work on a revolving wheel or drum the machine be work by bullock and as the bucket ascend full from the well they be empty during they revolution into a small trough at the top and the water be convey into a huge masonry reservoir or tank situate high up above the vat which form a splendid open air bath for the planter when he feel inclined for a swim many of these tank call _ kajhana _ be capable of contain 40000 cubic foot of water or more below and in a line with this reservoir be the steep vat each capable of contain about 2000 cubic foot of water when full of course the vat vary in size but what be call a _ pucca _ vat be of the above capacity when the fresh green plant be bring in the cart with they load be range in line opposite these load vat the loading coolie ` bojhunneas ' -- so call from ' _ bojh _ ' a bundle -- jump into the vat and receive the plant from the cart-men stack it up in perpendicular layer till the vat be full : a horizontal layer be put on top to make the surface look even bamboo batten be then place over the plant and these be press down and hold in they place by horizontal beam work in upright post the upright have hole at interval of six inch a iron pin be put in one of the hole a lever be put under this pin and the beam press down till the next hole be reach and a fresh pin insert which keep the beam down in its place when sufficient pressure have be apply the sluice in the reservoir be open and the water run by a channel into the vat till it be full vat after vat be thus fill till all be finish and the plant be allow to steep from ten to thirteen or fourteen hour accord to the state of the weather the temperature of the water and other condition and circumstance which have all to be carefully note at first a greenish yellow tinge appear in the water gradually deepen to a intense blue as the fermentation go on froth form on the surface of the vat the water swell up bubble of gas arise to the surface and the whole range of vat present a frothing bubble sweltering appearance indicative of the chemical action go on in the interior if a torch be apply to the surface of a vat the accumulate gas ignite with a loud report and a blue lambent flame travel with amazing rapidity over the effervescent liquid in very hot weather i have see the water swell up over the mid wall of the vat till the whole range would be one uniform surface of frothing liquid and on apply a light the report have be as loud as that of a small cannon and the flame have leap from vat to vat like the flit will-o ' - the-wisp on the surface of some miasmatic marsh when fermentation have proceed sufficiently the temperature of the vat lower somewhat and the water which have be globular and convex on the surface and at the side now become distinctly convex and recede a very little this be a sign that the plant have be steep long enough and that it be now time to open the vat a pin be knock out from the bottom and the pent-up liquor rush out in a golden yellow stream tint with blue and green into the beating vat which lie parallel to but at a lower level than the load vat of course as the vat be load at different hour and the steep vary with circumstance they must be ready to open also at different interval there be two man specially engage to look after the open the time of load each one be carefully note the time it will take to steep be guess at and a hour for open write down when this hour arrive the _ gunta parree _ or time-keeper look at the vat and if it appear ready he get the pinman to knock out the pin and let the steeped liquor run into the beating vat where there be many vat this go on all night and by the morning the beating vat be all full of steeped liquor and ready to be beat the beating now be mostly do by machinery but the old style be very different a gang of coolie -lrb- generally dangurs -rrb- be put into the vat have long stick with a disc at the end with which standing in two row they throw up the liquor into the air the quantity force up by the one coolie encounter in mid air that send up by the man standing immediately opposite to he and the two jet meeting and mix confusedly together tumble down in broken frothy mass into the vat begin with a slow steady stroke the coolie gradually increase the pace shout out a hoarse wild song at interval till what with the swish and splash of the fall water the measure beat of the _ furrovah _ or beat rod and the yell and cry with which they excite each other the noise be almost deafen the water which at first be of a yellowish green be now begin to assume a intense blue tint this be the result of the oxygenation go on as the blue deepen the exertion of the coolie increase till with every muscle strain head throw back chest expand he long black hair drip with white foam and he bronzed naked body glisten with blue liquor he yell and shout and twist and contort he body till he look like a true ` blue devil ' to see eight or ten vat full of yell howling blue creature the water splash high in mid air the foam fleck the wall and the measure beat of the _ furrovah _ rise weird-like into the morning air be almost enough to shake the nerve of a stranger but it be music in the planter 's ear and he can scarce refrain from yell out in sympathy with he coolie and share in they frantic excitement indeed it be often necessary to encourage they if a vat prove obstinate and the color refuse to come -- a event which occasionally do happen it be very hard work beating and when this constant violent exercise be keep up for about three hour -lrb- which be the time generally take -rrb- the coolie be pretty well exhaust and require a rest -lrb- illustration : indigo beater at work in the vat -rrb- during the beat two process be go on simultaneously one be chemical -- oxygenation -- turn the yellowish green dye into a deep intense blue : the other be mechanical -- a separation of the particle of dye from the water in which it be hold in solution the beating seem to do this cause the dye to granulate in larger particle when the vat have be beat the coolie remove the froth and scum from the surface of the water and then leave the contents to settle the feculum or dye or _ mall _ as it be technically call now settle at the bottom of the vat in a soft pulpy sediment and the waste liquor leave on the top be let off through graduate hole in the front pin after pin be gradually remove and the clear sherry-coloured waste allow to run out till the last hole in the series be reach and nothing but dye remains in the vat by this time the coolie have have a rest and food and now they return to the work and either lift up the _ mall _ in earthen jar and take it to the mall tank or -- as be now more commonly do -- they run it along a channel to the tank and then wash out and clean the vat to be ready for the renew beating on the morrow when all the _ mall _ have be collect in the mall tank it be next pump up into the strain room it be here strain through successive layer of wire gauze and cloth till free from dirt sand and impurity it be run into the large iron boiler to be subject to the next process this be the boil this operation usually take two or three hour after which it be run off along narrow channel till it reach the straining-table it be a very important part of the manufacture and have to be carefully do the straining-table be a oblong shallow wooden frame in the shape of a trough but all compose of open woodwork it be cover by a large straining-sheet on which the mall settle while the waste water trickle through and be carry away by a drain when the mall have stand on the table all night it be next morning lift up by scoop and bucket and put into the press these be square box of iron or wood with perforated side and bottom and a removeable perforated lid the inside of the box be line with press cloth and when fill these cloth be carefully fold over the _ mall _ which be now of the consistence of starch and a heavy beam work on two upright three-inch screw be let down on the lid of the press a long lever be now put on the screw and the nut work slowly round the pressure be enormous and all the water remain in the _ mall _ be press through the cloth and perforation in the press-box till nothing but the pure indigo remain behind the press be now open and a square slab of dark moist indigo about three or three and a half inch thick be carry off on the bottom of the press -lrb- the top and side have be remove -rrb- and carefully place on the cut frame this frame correspond in size to the bottom of the press and be groove in line somewhat after the manner of a chess-board a stiff iron rod with a brass wire attach be put through the groove under the slab the wire be bring over the slab and the rod be pull smartly through bring the wire with it cut the indigo much in the same way as you would cut a bar of soap when all the slab have be cut into bar the wire and rod be next put into the groove at right angle to the bar and again pull through thus divide the bar into cubical cake each cake be then stamp with the factory mark and number and all be note down in the book they be then take to the dry house this be a large airy building with strong shelf of bamboo reach to the roof and have narrow passage between the tier of shelf on these shelf or _ mychan _ as they be call the cake be range to dry the dry take two or three month and the cake be turn and move at frequent interval till thoroughly ready for pack all the little piece and corner and chip be carefully put by on separate shelf and packed separately even the sweepings and refuse from the sheet and floor be all carefully collect mixed with water boil separately and make into cake which be call ` washing ' during the dry a thick mould form on the cake this be carefully brush off before packing and mixed with sweepings and tiny chip be all ground up in a hand-mill packed in separate chest and sell as dust in October when _ mahye _ be over and the preparation of the land go on again the packing begin the cake each of separate date be carefully scrutinise and place in order of quality the finest quality be pack first in layer in mango-wood box the box be first weigh empty re-weighed when full and the difference give the nett weight of the indigo the tare gross and nett weight be print legibly on the chest along with the factory mark and number of the chest and when all be ready they be send down to the broker in calcutta for sale such shortly be the system of manufacture during _ mahye _ the factory be a busy scene long before break of day the ryot and coolie be busy cut the plant leave it in green little heap for the cartman to load in the early morning the cart be see converge to the factory on every road crawl along like huge green beetle here a cavalcade of twenty or thirty cart there in cluster of two or three when they reach the factory the loader have several vat ready for the reception of the plant while other be take out the already steep plant of yesterday stagger under its weight as drip with water they toss it on the vast accumulate heap of refuse material down in the vat below the beating coolie be plash and shout and yell or the revolving wheel -lrb- where machinery be use -rrb- be scatter cloud of spray and foam in the blinding sunshine the fireman strip to the waist be feed the furnace with the dry stem of last year 's crop which form we only fuel the smoke hover in volume over the boiling-house the pinman be busy sort they pin roll hemp round they to make they fit the hole more exactly inside the boiling-house dimly discernible through the cloud of stifle steam the boilerman be see with long rod stir slowly the boiling mass of bubble blue the clank of the lever resound through the pressing-house or the hoarse guttural ` hah hah ' as the huge lever be strain and pull at by the press-house coolie the straining-table be be clean by the table ` mate ' and he coolie while the washerman stamp on he sheet and press-cloth to extract all the color from they and the cake-house boy run to and fro between the cutting-table and the cake-house with batch of cake on they head bear on board like a baker take he hot roll from the oven or like a busy swarm of ant take the spoil of the granary to they forest haunt everywhere there be a confused jumble of sound the plash of water the clank of machinery the creaking of wheel the roaring of the furnace mingle with the shout cry and yell of the excited coolie the vituperation of the driver as some terrify or obstinate bullock plunge madly about the objurgation of the ` mate ' as some lazy fellow ease he stroke in the beating vat the crack of whip as the bullock tear round the circle where the persian wheel creak and rumble in the damp dilapidated wheel-house the-dripping bucket revolve clumsily on the drum the arrive and depart cart the clang of the anvil as the blacksmith and he man hammer away at some huge screw which have be bent the hurry crowd of cartman and loader with they burden of fresh green plant or drip refuse -- form such a medley of sight and sound as i have never see equal in any other industry the planter have to be here there and everywhere he send cart to this village or to that accord as the crop ripen coolie must be count and pay daily the stubble must be plough to give the plant a start for the second growth whenever the weather will admit of it report have to be send to the agent and owner the boiling must be narrowly watch as also the beating and the strain he have a large staff of native assistant but if he _ mahye _ be to be successful he eye must be over all it be a anxious time but the constant work be grateful and when the produce be good and everything work smoothly it be perhaps the most enjoyable time of the whole year be it nothing to see the crop on which so much care have be expend which you have watch day by day through all the vicissitude of the season through drought and flood and blight be it nothing to see it safely harvest and you shelf fill day by day with fine sound cake the representative of wealth that will fill you pocket with commission and build up you name as a careful and painstaking planter ` what be you produce ' be now the first query at this season when planter meet calculation be make daily nay hourly to see how much be be get per beegah or how much per vat the press be calculate to weigh so much some day you will get a press a vat some day it will mount up to two press a vat and at other time it will recede to half a press a vat or even less cold wet weather reduce the produce warm sunny weather will send it up again short stunt plant from poor land will often reduce you average per acre to be again send up as fresh hardy leafy plant come in from some favorite village where you have new and fertile land or where the plant from the rich zeraat laden with broad strong leaf be tumble into the load vat so far as i know there seem to be no law of produce it be the most erratic and incomprehensible thing about plant one day you press be full to strain next day half of they lie empty no doubt the state of the weather the quality of you plant the temperature of the water the length of time steeping and other thing have a influence but i know of no planter who can entirely and satisfactorily account for the sudden and incomprehensible fluctuation and variation which undoubtedly take place in the produce or yield of the plant it be a matter of more interest to the planter than to the general public but all i can say be that if the circumstance attendant on any sudden change in the yield power of the plant be more accurately note if the chemical condition of the water the air and the raw material itself more especially in reference to the soil on which it grow the time it take in transit from the field to the vat and other point which will at once suggest themselves to a practical planter be more carefully methodically and scientifically observe some coherent theory result in plain practical result might be evolve planter should attend more to this i believe the chemical history of indigo have yet to be write the whole manufacture so far as chemistry be concern be yet crude and ill-digested i know that by careful experiment and close scientific investigation and observation the preparation of indigo could be much improve so far as the mechanical appliance for the manufacture go the last ten year have witness amazing and rapid improvement what be now want be that what have be do for the mere mechanical appliance should be do for the proper understanding of the chemical change and condition in the constitution of the plant and in the have be work in jessore purneah kishnaghur and other place this increase vary accord to circumstance have in some instance reach the amazing extent of 30 to 47 per cent and so far from be attend with a deterioration of quality the dye produce be say to be finer than that obtain under the old crude process describe in the above chapter this show what a waste must have be go on and what may yet be do by properly organise scientific investigation i firmly believe that with a intelligent application of the principle of chemistry and agricultural science not only to the manufacture but to growth cultivation nature of the soil application of manure and other such department of the business quite a revolution will set in and a new era in the history of this great industry will be inaugurate another breed of large strong-limbed big-boned dog call rampore hound they be a cross breed from the original upcountry dog and the persian greyhound some call they the indian greyhound they seem to be breed principally in the rampore-bareilly district but one or more be generally to be find in every planter 's pack they be fast and strong enough but i have often find they bad at tackle and they be too fond of they keeper ever to make a affectionate faithful dog to the european another somewhat similar breed be the _ tazus _ this although not so large a dog as the rhamporee be a much pluckier animal and when well train will tackle a jackal with the utmost determination he have a wrinkled almost hairless skin but a very uncertain temper and he be not very amenable to discipline _ tazus _ be simply the persian word for a greyhound and refer to no particular breed the common name for a dog be _ kutta _ pronounce _ cootta _ but the tazus have certainly be a importation from the north-west be difficult to tame but fleet and plucky hunting well with a english pack my neighbor anthony at meerpore have some very fine english greyhound and bulldog and many a rattle burst have we have agreeable break to the irksome monotony of we lonely life chapter vi fishing in indium -- hereditary trade -- the boatman and fisherman of indium -- they village -- net -- mode of fishing -- curiosity relate thereto -- catch a alligator with a hook -- exciting capture-crocodile -- shoot a alligator -- death of the man-eater not only in the wild jungle on the undulating plain and among the withered brown stubble do animal life abound in indium but the river lake and creek teem with fish of every conceivable size shape and color the variety be legion from the huge black porpoise tumble through the turgid stream of the gange to the bright sparkling silvery shoal of delicate _ chillooah _ or _ poteeah _ which one see dart in and out among the rice stubble in every paddy field during the rain here a huge _ bhowarree _ -lrb- pike -rrb- or ravenous _ coira _ come to the surface with a splash there a _ raho _ the indian salmon with its round sucker-like mouth rise slowly to the surface suck in a fly and disappear as slowly as it rise or a _ pachgutchea _ a long sharp-nosed fish dart rapidly by a shoal of mullet with they head out of the water swim athwart the stream and far down in the cool depths of the tank or lake a thousand different variety disport themselves among the mazy labyrinth of the broad-leaved weed during the middle and about the end of the rain be the best time for fishing the whole country be then a perfect network of stream every rice field be a shallow lake with countless thousand of tiny fish dart here and there among the rice stalk every ditch teem with fish and every hollow in every field be a well stock aquarium round the edge of every lake or tank in the early morning or when the fierce heat of the day begin to get temper by the approach shades of evening one see number of boy and man of the poorer class each with a couple of rough bamboo rod stick in the ground in front of he watch he primitive float with the greatest eagerness and whipping out at interval some luckless fish of about three or four ounce in weight with a tremendous haul fit for the capture of a forty-pounder they get a coarse sort of hook in the bazaar rig up a roughly-twisted line tie on a small piece of hollow reed for a float and with a lively earth-worm for a bait they can generally manage in a very short time to secure enough fish for a meal with a short light rod a good silk line and a english hook attach to fine gut i have enjoy many a good hour 's sport at parewah i use to have a cane chair send down to the bank of the stream a _ punkah _ or hand fan plenty of cool drink and two coolie boy in attendance to remove the fish renew bait and keep the punkah in constant swing there i use to sit enjoy my cigar and pull in little fish at the rate sometimes of a couple a minute i remember hook a turtle once and a terrible job it be to land he my light rod bent like a willow but the tackle be good and after ten minute ' hard work i get the turtle to the side where my boy soon secure he he weigh thirteen pound sometimes you get among a colony of freshwater crab they be little brown brute and strip you hook of the bait as fast as you fling they in there be nothing for it in such a case but to shift you station many of the bottom fish -- the _ ghuraus _ the _ saourie _ the _ barnee _ -lrb- eel -rrb- and other make no effort to escape the hook you see they rest at the bottom and drop the bait at they very nose on the whole the hand fishing be uninteresting but it serve to wile away a odd hour when hunting and shooting be hardly practicable particular occupation in indium be restricted to particular caste all trade be hereditary for example a _ tatmah _ or weaver be always a weaver he can not become a blacksmith or carpenter he have no choice he must follow the hereditary trade the peculiar system of land-tenure in indium which secure as far as possible a bit of land for every one tend to perpetuate this hereditary selection of trade by enable every cultivator to be so far independent of he handicraft thus restrict competition there may be twenty _ lohar _ or blacksmith in a village but they do not all follow they call they till they land and be _ de facto _ petty farmer they know the rudiment of they handicraft but the actual blacksmith 's work be do by the hereditary smith of the village whose son in turn will succeed he when he die or if he leave no son he fellow caste man will put in a successor nearly every villager during the rain may be find on the bank of the stream or lake angling in a amateur sort of way but the fisherman of behar _ par excellence _ be the _ mull -lrb- = a -rrb- h _ they be also call _ gouhree beeu _ or _ muchooah _ in bengal they be call _ nikaree _ and in some part _ baeharee _ from the persian word for a boat in the same way _ muchooah _ be derive from _ much _ a fish and _ mullah _ mean boatman strictly speak rather than fisherman all boatman and fisherman belong to this caste and they village can be recognize at once by the instrument of they call lie all around perch high on some bank overlook the stream or lake you see innumerable festoon of net hang out to dry on tall bamboo pole or hang like lace curtain of very coarse texture from the roof and eaves of the hut haul up on the beach be a whole fleet of boat of different size from the small _ dugout _ which will hold only one man to the huge _ dinghy _ in which the big net and a dozen man can be stow with ease great heap of shell of the freshwater mussel show the source of great supplies of bait while overhead a great hover army of kite and vulture be constantly circle round eagerly watch for the slightest scrap of offal from the net when the rain have fairly set in and the fisherman have get they rice field all plant out they be at liberty to follow they hereditary avocation a day be fix for a drag and the big net be overhaul and get in readiness the head _ mullah _ a wary grizzled old veteran give the order the big drag-net be bundle into the boat which be quickly push off into the stream and at a certain distance from shore the net be cast from the boat be weight at the lower end it rapidly sink and buoy on the upper side with piece of cork it make a perpendicular wall in the water several long bamboo pole be now run through the rope along the upper side of the net to prevent the net be drag under water altogether by the weight of the fish in a great haul the little boat a crowd of which be in attendance now dart out surround the net on all side and the boatman beat they oar on the side of the boat create such a clatter as to frighten the fish into the circumference of the big net this be now be drag slowly to shore by strong and willing arm the woman and child watch eagerly on the bank at length the glittering haul be pull up high and dry on the beach the fish be divide among the man the woman fill they basket and away they hie to the nearest _ bazaar _ or if it be not _ bazaar _ or market day they hawk the fish through the nearest village like we fish-wife at home there be another common mode of fishing adopt in narrow lake and small stream which be let out to the fisherman by the zemindar or landholder a barricade make of light reed all mat together by string be stick into the stream and a portion of the water be fence in generally in a circular form the reed fence be quite flexible be gradually move in narrow the circle as the circle narrow the agitation inside be indescribable fish jump in all direction -- a move mass of glitter scale and fin the larger one try to leap the barrier and be catch by the attendant _ mulla _ who pounce on they with swift dexterity eagle and kite dart and swoop down bear off a captive fish in they talon the reed fence be double back on itself and gradually push on till the whole of the fish inside be jam together in a move mass the weed and dirt be then remove and the fish put into basket and carry off to market other again use circular casting net which they throw with very great dexterity gather the net into a bunch they rest it on the shoulder then with a circular sweep round the head they fling it far out be load it sink down rapidly in the water a string be attach to the center of the net and the fisherman haul it in with whatever prey he may be lucky enough to secure as the water recede during October after the rain have end each runlet and purling stream become a scene of slaughter on a most reckless and improvident scale the innumerable shoal of spawn and small fish that have be feed in the rice field warn by some instinct seek the lake and main stream as they try to get they way back however they find at each outlet in each ditch and field a deadly wicker trap in the shape of a square basket with a v-shaped opening lead into it through which the stream make its way after enter this basket there be no egress except through the narrow opening and they be trap thus in countless thousand other of the native in mere wantonness put a shelf of reed or rush in the bed of the stream with a upward slope as the water rush along the little fish be leave high and dry on this shelf or screen and the water run off below in this way scarcely a fish escape and as million be too small to be eat it be a most serious waste the attention of government have be direct to the subject and step may be take to stop such a reckless and wholesale destruction of a valuable food supply in some part of purneah and bhaugulpore i have see a most ingenious method adopt by the _ mulla _ a gang of four or five enter the stream and travel slowly downward stir up the mud at the bottom with they foot the fish ascend the stream to escape the mud get entangled in the weed the fisherman feel they with they foot amongst the weed and immediately pounce on they with they hand each man have a _ gilum _ or earthen pot attach by a string to he waist and float behind he in the water i have see four man fill they earthen pot in less than a hour by this ingenious but primitive mode of fishing some of they can use they foot almost as well for grasp purpose as they hand another mode of capture be by a small net a flat piece of net be spread over a hoop to which four or five piece of bamboo be attach rise up and meeting in the center so as to form a sort of miniature skeleton tent-like frame over the net the hoop with the net stretch tight across be then press down flat on the bottom of the tank or stream if any fish be beneath they effort to escape agitate the net the motion be communicate to the fisherman by a string from the center of the net which be roll round the fisherman 's thumb when the jerking of he thumb announce a captive fish he put down he left hand and secure he victim the _ bantur _ _ nepaulee _ and other jungle tribe also often use the bow and arrow as a means of secure fish seat on the branch of some overhang tree while he keen eye scan the depths below he watch for a large fish and as it pass he let fly he arrow with unerring aim and impale the luckless victim some tribe fish at night by torchlight spear the fish who be attract by the light in nepaul the bark of the _ hill sirre _ be often use to poison a stream or piece of water pound up and throw in it seem to have some uncommon effect on the fish after water have be treat in this way the fish seemingly quite stupefied rise to the surface on which they float in great number and allow themselves to be catch the strangest part of it be that they be perfectly innocuous as food notwithstanding this treatment fish form a very favorite article of diet with both mussulman and hindoo many of the latter take a vow to touch no flesh of any kind they be call _ kunthee _ or _ boghut _ but a _ boghut _ be more of a ascetic than a _ kunthee _ however the _ kunthee _ be glad of a fish dinner when he can get it they be restricted to no particular sect or caste but all who have take the vow wear a peculiar necklace make generally of sandal-wood bead or _ neem _ bead round they throat hence the name from _ kunth _ mean the throat the right to fish in any particular piece of water be let out by the proprietor on whose land the water lie or through which it flow the let be generally do by auction yearly the fishing be call a _ shilkur _ from _ shal _ a net it be generally take by some rich _ bunneah _ -lrb- grain seller -rrb- or village banker who sub-let it in turn to the fisherman in some of the tank which be not so let and where the native proprietor preserve the fish first-class sport can be have a common native poach dodge be this : if some oil cake be throw into the water a few hour previous to you fishing or better still ball make of roasted linseed meal mix with bruise leaf of the ` sweet basil ' or _ toolsee _ plant the fish assemble in hundred round the spot and devour the bait greedily with a good eighteen-foot rod fish of from twelve to twenty pound be not uncommonly catch and will give good play too fishing in the plain of indium be however rather tame sport at the best of time you have hear of the famous _ mahseer _ -- some of they over eighty or a hundred pound weight we have none of these in behar but the huge porpoise give splendid rifle or carbine practice as he roll through the turgid stream they be difficult to hit but i have see several kill with ball and the oil extract from they body be a splendid dress for harness but the most exciting fishing i have ever see be -- what do you think -- alligator fishing yes the formidable scaly monster with he square snout and terrible jaw he ponderous body cover with armor and he serrated tail with which he could break the leg of a bullock or smash a outrigger as easily as a whale could smash a jolly-boat i must try to describe one day 's alligator fishing when i be down in bhaugulpore i go out frequently fishing in the various tank and stream near my factory my friend pat who be a keen sportsman and very fond of angle write to I one day when he and he brother willie be go out to the teljuga ask I to join they party the teljuga be the boundary stream between tirhoot and bhaugulpore and its sluggish muddy water teem with alligator -- the regular square-nosed _ mugger _ the terrible man-eater the _ nakar _ or long-nosed species may be see in countless number in any of the large stream stretch out on the bank bask in the noonday sun go down the koosee particularly you come across hundred sometimes lie on one bank as the boat near they they slide noiselessly and slowly into the stream a large excrescence form on the tip of the long snout like a huge sponge and this be often all that be see on the surface of the water as the huge brute swim about wait for he prey these _ nakar _ or long-nosed specimen never attack human being -- at least such case be very very rare -- but live almost entirely on fish i remember see one catch a paddy-bird on one occasion near the junction of the koosee with the gange my boat be fasten to the shore near a slimy creek that come ooze into the river from some dense jungle near i be wash
