snout and the ugly serrated ridgy back of a _ nakar _ be shoot like lightning at the hapless bird and right before we eye the poor paddy be crunch up as a rule however alligator confine themselves to a fish diet and be glad of any refuse or dead animal that may float they way but with the _ mugger _ the _ boach _ or square-nosed variety ` all be fish that come to he net ' he soul delight in young dog or live pork a fat duck come not amiss and impel by hunger he hesitate not to attack man once regale with the flavor of human flesh he take up he stand near some ferry or bathing ghaut where many hapless woman and child often fall victim to he unholy appetite before he career be cut short i remember shoot one ghastly old scaly villain in a tank near ryseree he have make this tank he home and with that fatalism which be so characteristic of the hindoo the usual ablutions and bathing go on as if no such monster exist several woman have be carry off however at short interval the villager ask I to try and rid they of they foe i take a ride down to the tank one Friday morning and find the bank a scene of great excitement a woman have be carry off some hour before as she be fill she water jar and the monster be now repose at the bottom of the tank digest he horrible meal the tank be cover with crimson water-lily in full bloom they broad brown and green leaf show off the crimson beauty of the open flower at the north corner some wild rise bush drop over the water cast a dense matted shade here be the haunt of the _ mugger _ he have excavate a huge gloomy-looking hole into which he retire when gorge with prey my first care be to cut away some of these bush and then find he be not at home we drive some bamboo stake through the bank to prevent he get into he _ manu _ which be what the native term the den or hole i then sit down under a _ goolar _ tree to wait for he appearance the _ goolar _ be a species of fig and the leaf be much relish by cattle and goat gradually the village boy and young man go off to they ploughing or grass cutting for the cow ' evening meal a woman come down occasionally to fill she waterpot in evident fear and tremble a swarm of _ mina _ -lrb- the indian starling -rrb- hop and twitter round my foot the cooing of a pair of amatory pigeon overhead nearly lull I to slumber a flock of green parrot come swiftly circle overhead make for the fig-tree at the south end of the tank a occasional _ raho _ lazily rise among the water-lily and disappear with a indolent flap of he tail the brilliant kingfisher resplendent in crimson and emerald sit on the withered branch of a prostrate mango-tree close by plume he feather and doubtless meditate on the vanity of life suddenly close by the massive post which mark the center of every hindoo tank a huge scaly snout slowly and almost imperceptibly rise to the surface then a broad flat forbid forehead top by two gray fishy eye with warty-looking callosity for eyebrow just then a eager urchin who have be squat by I for hour point to the brute it be enough down sink the loathsome creature and we have to resume we attitude of expectation and patient wait another hour pass slowly it be the middle of the afternoon and very hot i have send my _ tokedar _ off for a ` peg ' to the factory and be begin to get very drowsy when right in the same spot the repulsive head again rise slowly to the surface i have my trusty no 12 to my shoulder on the instant glance carefully along the barrel but just then only the eye of the brute be invisible a moment of intense excitement follow and then embolden by the extreme stillness he show he whole head above the surface i pull the trigger and a meade shell crash through the monster 's skull scatter he brain in the water and actually send one splinter of the skull to the opposite edge of the tank where my little hindoo boy pick it up and bring it to I there be a mighty agitation in the water the water-lily rock to and fro and the broad leave glitter with the water drop throw on they then all be still hear the report of my gun the native come flock to the spot and tell they they enemy be slay i depart leave instruction to let I know when the body come to the surface it do so three day later get some _ chumar _ and _ dome _ -lrb- two of the lowest caste as none of the higher caste will touch a dead body under pain of lose caste -rrb- we haul the putrid carcase to shore and on cut it open find the glass armlet and brass ornament of no less than five woman and the silver ornament of three child all in a lump in the brute 's stomach its skull be completely smash and shatter to piece by my shot its tooth be crust with tartar and worn almost to the very stump it measure nineteen foot but during this digression my friend pat and willie have be wait on the bank of the ` teljuga ' i reach they tent late at night find they both in high spirit after a good day 's execution among the duck and teal and preparation be make for catch a alligator next day up early in the morning we beat some grass close by the stream and rouse out a enormous boar that give we a three mile spin and a good fight after pat have give he first spear after breakfast we get we tackle ready this be a large iron hook with a strong shank to which be attach a stout iron ring to this ring a long thick rope be fasten and i notice for several yard the strand be all loose and detach and only knot at interval i ask pat the reason of this curious arrangement and be tell that if we be lucky enough to secure a _ mugger _ the loose strand would entangle themselves amongst he formidable tooth whereas be the rope in one strand only he might bite it through the knotting at interval be to give greater strength to the line we now get we bait ready on this occasion it be a live tame duck pass the bend of the hook round its neck and the shank under its right wing we tie the hook in this position with thread we then make a small raft of the soft pith of the plantain-tree tie the duck to the raft and commit it to the stream hold the rope as clear of the water as we could the poor quack duck float slowly down the muddy current make a occasional vain effort to get free we see at a distance a ugly snout rise to the surface for a instant and then noiselessly disappear ` there be one ' say pat in a whisper ` be sure and not strike too soon ' say willie ` look out there you lazy rascal ' this in hindostanee to the groom and servant who be with we again the black mass rise to the surface but this time nearer to the fate duck as if aware of its peril it now struggle and quack most vociferously nearer and nearer each time the black snout rise and then each time silently disappear beneath the turgid muddy stream now it appear again this time there be two and there be another at a distance attract by the quacking of the duck we on the bank cower down and go as noiselessly as we can sometimes the rope dip on the water and the huge snout and stare eye immediately disappear at length it rise within a few yard of the duck then there be a mighty rush two huge jaw open and shut with a snap like factory shears and amid a whirl of foam and water and surge mud the poor duck and the hideous reptile disappear and but for the eddy swirl and dense volume of mud that rise from the bottom nothing give evidence of the tragedy that have be enact the other two disappointed monster swim to and fro still further disturb the muddy current ` give he lot of time to swallow ' yell pat now fairly mad with excitement the groom and grass-cutter howl and dance willie and i dig each other in the rib and all generally act in a excited and insane way pat now put the rope over he shoulder we all take hold and with a ` one two three ' we make a simultaneous rush from the bank and as the rope suddenly tighten with a pull and strain that nearly jerk we all on we back we feel that we have hook the monster and we excitement reach its culminate point what a commotion now in the black depths of the muddy stream the water lash by he powerful tail surge and dash in eddy whirl he rise and dart backwards and forwards snap he horrible jaw move he head from side to side he eye glare with fury we hold stoutly on to the rope although we wrist be strain and we arm ache at length he begin to feel we steady pull and inch by inch struggle demoniacally he near the bank when once he reach it however the unite effort of twice we number would fail to bring he farther bleeding and foam at the mouth he horrid tooth glisten amid the frothy blood-flecked foam he plant he strong curved fore-leg against the shelve bank and tug and strain at the rope with devilish force and fury it be no use -- the rope have be test and answer bravely to the strain and now with a long boar spear pat cautiously descend the bank and give he a deadly thrust under the fore arm with a last fiendish glare of hate and defiance he spring forward we haul in the rope pat nimbly jump back and a pistol shot through the eye settle the monster for ever this be the first alligator i ever see hooked he measure sixteen and a half foot exactly but word can give no idea of half the excitement that attend the capture chapter vii native superstition -- charming a bewitch woman -- exorcise ghost from a field -- witchcraft -- the witchfinder or ` ojah ' -- influence of fear -- snake bite -- how to cure they -- how to discover a thief -- ghost and they habit -- the ` haddick ' or native bone-setter -- cruelty to animal by native the native as a rule and especially the lower class be excessively superstitious they be afraid to go out after nightfall believe that then the spirit of the dead walk abroad it be almost impossible to get a coolie or even a fairly intelligent servant to go a message at night unless you give he another man for company a belief in witch be quite prevalent and there be scarcely a village in behar that do not contain some withered old crone repute and firmly believe to be a witch other either young or old be believe to have the evil eye and as in scotland some century ago there be also witch-finder and sorcerer who will sell charm cast nativity give divination or ward off the evil effort of wizard and witch by powerful spell when a wealthy man have a child bear the brahmin cast the nativity of the infant on some auspicious day they fix on the name and settle the date for the baptismal ceremony i remember a man come to I on one occasion from the village of kuppoorpuckree he rush up to where i be sit in the verandah throw himself at my foot with tear stream down he cheek and amid loud cry for pity and help tell I that he wife have just be bewitch get he somewhat soothe and pacify i learn that a reputed witch live next door to he house that she and the man 's wife have quarrel in the morning about some capsicum which the witch be try to steal from he garden that in the evening as he wife be wash herself inside the _ angana _ or little courtyard appertain to he house she be seize with cramp and shiver fit and be now in a rage fever that the witch have be also bathing at the time and that the water from she body have splash over this man 's fence and part of it have come in contact with he wife 's body -- hence undoubtedly this strange possession he wish I to send peon at once and have the witch seize beat and expel from the village it would have be no use my try to persuade he that no witchcraft exist so i give he a good dose of quinine for he wife which she be to take as soon as the fit subside next i get my old _ moonshee _ or native writer to write some persian character on a piece of paper i then give he this paper mutter a bit of english rhyme at the time and tell he this be a powerful spell i tell he to take three hair from he wife 's head and a pare from she thumb and big toe nail and at the rising of the moon to burn they outside the wall of he hut the poor fellow take the quinine and the paper with the deepest reverence make I a most lowly _ salaam _ or obeisance and depart with a light heart he carry out my instruction to the letter the quinine act like a charm on the feverish woman and i find myself quite a famous witch-doctor there be a nice flat little field close to the water at parewah in which i think i could get a good crop of oats during the cold weather i send for the ` dangur ' mate and ask they to have it dig up next day they hum and haw and hesitate as i think in rather a strange manner but depart in the evening back they come to tell I that the dangurs would _ not _ dig up the field ` why ' i ask ` well you see sahib ' say old teerbouan who be the patriarch and chief spokesman of the village ` this field have be use for year as a burning ghaut ' -lrb- ie a place where the body of dead hindoo be bury -rrb- ` well ' say i ` well sahib my man say that if they disturb this land the bhoot -lrb- ghost -rrb- of all those who have be burn there will haunt the village at night and they hope you will not persist in ask they to dig up the land ' ` very well bring down the man with they digging-hoe and i will see ' accordingly next morning i go down on my pony find the dangurs all assemble but no digging go on i call they together tell they that it be a very reasonable fear they have but that i would cast such a spell on the land as would settle the ghost of the depart for ever i then get a branch of a _ bael _ -lrb- 1 -rrb- tree that grow close by dip it in the stream and walk backwards round the ground wave the drip branch round my head repeat at the same time the first gibberish that come into my recollection my incantation or spell be as follow a old scotch rhyme i have often repeat when a child at school -- ` eenerty feenerty fickerty feg ell dell domun 's egg irky birky story rock a tan toose jock black fish white troot gibbie gaw ye be oot ' it have the desire effect no sooner be my charm utter than after a few encouraging word to the man tell they that there be now no fear that my charm be powerful enough to lay all the spirit in the country and that i would take all the responsibility they set to work with a will and have the whole field dig up by the evening i have see many such case a blight attack the melon or cucumber bed a fierce wind rise during the night and shake half the mango off the tree the youngest child be attack with teethe convulsion the plough-bullock be accidentally lame or the favorite cow refuse to give milk in every case it be some ` dyne ' or witch that have be at work with she damnable spell and charm i remember a case in which a poor little child have bad convulsion the ` ojah ' or witch-finder in this case a fat greasy oleaginous knave be send for full of importance and blow like a porpoise he come and cause the child to be bring to he under a tree near the village i be pass at the time and stop out of curiosity he spread a tattered cloth in front of he and mutter some unintelligible gibberish unceasingly make strange pass with he arm he put down a number of article on he cloth -- which be villainously tattered and greasy -- a unripe plantain a handful of rice of parched pea a thigh bone two wooden cup some ball & c & c all of which he keep constantly lift and move about keep up the pass and mutter all the time the child be a sickly-looking pine sort of creature rock about in evident pain and moaning and fret just as sick child do gradually its attention get fix on the strange antic go on the ojah keep mutter away quicker and quicker constantly shift the bone and cup and other article on the cloth he body be suffuse with perspiration but in about half a hour the child have go off to sleep and attend by some dozen old woman and the anxious father be bear off in triumph to the house another time one of mr d 's female servant get bite by a scorpion the poor woman be in great agony with she arm swell up when a ojah be call in set she before he he begin he incantation in the usual manner but make frequent pass over she body and over the bitten place a gentle perspiration begin to break out on she skin and in a very short time the ojah have throw she into a deep mesmeric sleep after about a hour she awake perfectly free from pain in this case no doubt the ojah be a mesmerist the influence of fear on the ordinary native be most wonderful i have know dozen of instance in which native have be bring home at night for treatment in case of snake-bite they have arrive at the factory in a complete state of coma with closed eye the pupil turn back in the head the whole body rigid and cold the lip pale white and the tongue firmly lock between the tooth i do not believe in recovery from a really poisonous bite where the venom have be truly inject i invariably ask first how long it be since the infliction of the bite i would then examine the mark and as a rule would find they very slight when the patient have be bring some distance i know at once that it be a case of pure fright the native wrap themselves up in they cloth or blanket at night and lie down on the floor of they hut turn about or get up for water or tobacco or perhaps to put fuel on the fire they unluckily tread on a snake or during sleep they roll over on one the snake give they a nip and scuttle off they have not see what sort of snake it be but they imagination conjure up the very worst after the first outcry when the whole house be alarm the man sit down firmly possess by the idea that he be mortally bite gradually he fear work the effect a real poisonous bite would produce he eye get dull he pulse grow feeble he extremity cold and numb and unless forcibly rouse by the bystander he will actually succumb to pure fright not to the snake-bite at all my chief care when a case of this sort be bring I be to assume a cheery demeanor laugh to scorn the fear of the relative and tell they he would be all right in a few hour if they attend to my direction this not uncommonly work by sympathetic influence on the patient himself i believe so long as all round he think he be go to die and expect no other result the same effect be produce on he own mind as soon as hope spring up in the breast of all around he he spirit also catch the contagion as a rule he would now make a effort to articulate i would then administer a good dose of sal volatile brandy eau-de-luce or other strong stimulant cut into the suppose bite and apply strong nitric acid to the wound this generally make he wince and i would hail it as a token of certain recovery by this time some confidence would return and the suppose die man would soon walk back sound and whole among he companion after profuse expression of gratitude to he preserver i have treat dozen of case in this way successfully and only see two death one be a young woman my chowkeydar 's daughter the other be a old man who be already dead when they lift he out of the basket in which they have sling he i do not wish to be misunderstand i believe that in all these case of recovery it be pure fright work on the imagination and not snake-bite at all my opinion be share by most planter that there be no cure yet know for a cobra bite or for that of any other poisonous snake where the poison have once be fairly inject and allow to mix with the blood -lrb- 2 -rrb- there be another curious instance of the effect of fear on the native mind in the common method take by a ojah or brahmin to discover a suspect thief when a theft occur the ojah be send for and the suspect party be bring together after various _ muntra _ ie charm or incantation have be mutter the ojah who have meanwhile narrowly scrutinize each countenance give each of the suspect individual a small quantity of dry rice to chew if the thief be present he superstitious fear be at work and he conscience accuse he he see some terrible retribution for he in all these _ muntra _ and he heart become like water within he he tongue get dry he salivary gland refuse to act the innocent munch away at they rice contentedly but the guilty wretch feel as if he have ash in he mouth at a give signal all spit out they rice and he whose rice come out chew indeed but dry as summer dust be adjudge the thief this ordeal be call _ chowl chipao _ and be rarely unsuccessful i have know several case in my own experience in which a thief have be thus discover the _ bhoot _ or ghost be popularly suppose to have favorite haunt generally in some specially select tree the _ neem _ tree be suppose to be the most patronise the most intelligent native share this belief with the poorest and most ignorant they fancy the ghost throw stone at they cast evil influence over they lure they into quicksand and play other devilish trick and cantrip some road be quite shun and desert at night for no other reason than that a ghost be suppose to haunt the place the most tempting bribe would not make a native walk alone over that road after sunset besides the witchfinder another important village functionary who rely much on muntra and charm be the _ huddick _ or cow doctor he be the only veterinary surgeon of the native when he cow or bullock dislocate or break a limb or fall ill the huddick pass he hand over the affected part and mutter he _ muntra _ which have most probably descend to he from he father usually know a little of the anatomical structure of the animal he may be able to reduce a dislocation or roughly to set a fracture but if the ailment be internal a draught of mustard oil or some pounded spice and turmeric or neem leaf administer along with the _ muntra _ be suppose to be all that human skill and science can do the native be cruel to animal half-starved bullock be shamefully overworked when blow fail to make the ill-starred brute move they give a twist and wrench to the tail which must cause the animal exquisite torture and unless the hapless beast be utterly exhaust this generally induce it to make a further effort ploughman very often deliberately make a raw open sore one on each rump of the plough-bullock they goad the poor wretch on this raw sore with a sharp-pointed stick when he lag or when they think he need stir up pony too be always work far too young and they miserable leg get frightfully twisted and bent the petty shopkeeper seller of brass pot grain spice and other bazaar wares who attend the various bazaar or weekly and bi-weekly market transport they goods by means of these pony the pack of merchandise be sling on rough pack-saddle make of coarse sack shamble along with knee bent together sore on every joint and frequently a eye knock out the poor pony 's back get cruelly gall when the bazaar be reach he be hobble as tightly as possible the coarse rope cut into the flesh and he be then turn adrift to contemplate starvation on the burnt-up grass great open sore form on the back on which a plaster of moist clay or cowdung and pound leaf be roughly put the wretched creature get worn to a skeleton a little common care and cleanliness would put he right with a little kindly consideration from he brutal master but what do the _ kulwar _ or _ bunneah _ care he be too lazy this unfeeling cruelty and callous indifference to the suffering of the lower animal be a cry evil and every magistrate european and educate native might do much to ease they burden tremendous number of bullock and pony die from sheer neglect and ill treatment every year it be now become so serious a trouble that in many village plough-bullock be too few in number for the area of land under cultivation the tillage suffer the crop deteriorate this react on price the ryot sink lower and lower and get more into the grasp of the rapacious money-lender in many village i have see whole tract of land relapse into _ purtee _ or untilled waste simply from want of bullock to draw the plough severe epidemic like foot and mouth disease and pleuro occasionally sweep off great number but i repeat that annually the life of hundred of valuable animal be sacrifice by sheer sloth dirt inattention and brutal cruelty in some part of indium cattle poisoning for the sake of the hide be extensively practice the _ chumar _ that be the shoemaker furrier tanner and worker in leather and skin generally frequently combine together in place and wilfully poison cattle and buffalo there be actually a section in the penal code take cognisance of the crime the hindoo will not touch a dead carcase so that when a bullock mysteriously sicken and die the _ chumar _ haul away the body and appropriate the skin some luckless witch be blame for the misfortune when the rascally chumar themselves be all the while the real culprit the police however be pretty successful in detect this crime and it be not now of such frequent occurrence -lrb- 3 -rrb- highly as the pious hindoo venerate the sacred bull of shira he treatment of he mild patient beast of burden be a foul blot on he character be you to shoot a cow or be a mussulman to wind a stray bullock which might have trespass and be trample down he opium or he tobacco crop and ruin he field the hindoo would rise _ en masse _ to revenge the insult offer to they religion yet they scruple not to goad they bullock beat they half starve they and let they gaping wound fester and become corrupt when the poor brute become old and unable to work and he worn-out tooth unfit to graze he be ruthlessly turn out to die in a ditch and be tear to piece by jackal kite and vulture the higher class and well-to-do farmer show much consideration for high-priced well-conditioned animal but when they get old or unwell and demand redouble care and attention they be too often neglect till from sheer want of ordinary care they rot and die -lrb- 1 -rrb- the _ bael _ or wood-apple be a sacred wood with hindoo it be enjoin in the shastra that the body of the dead should be consume in a fire feed by log of bael-tree but where it be not procurable in sufficient quantity the native compound with they conscience by light the funeral pyre with a branch from the bael-tree it be a fine yellow-coloured pretty durable wood and make excellent furniture a very fine sherbet can be make from the fruit which act as a excellent corrective and stomachic -lrb- 2 -rrb- death from actual snake bite be sadly numerous but it appear from return furnish to the indian government that european enjoy a very happy exemption during the last forty year it would seem that only two european have be kill by snake bite at least only two well substantiate case the poorer class be the most frequent victim they universal habit of walk about unshod and sleep on the ground penetrate into the grass or jungle in pursuit of they daily avocation no doubt conduce much to the frequency of such accident a good plan to keep snake out of the bungalow be to leave a space all round the room of about four inch between the wall and the edge of the mat have this wash over about once a week with a strong solution of carbolic acid and water the smell may be unpleasant for a short time but it prove equally so to the snake and i have prove by experience that it keep they out of the room mat should also be all firmly fasten down to the floor with bamboo batten and furniture should be often move and keep raise a little from the ground and the space below carefully sweep every day at night a light should always be keep burning in occupied bedroom and on no account should one get out of bed in the dark or walk about the room at night without slipper or shoe -lrb- 3 -rrb- somewhat analogous to this be the custom which use to be a common one in some part of behar _ koombar _ and _ granns _ that be tile-makers and thatcher when trade be dull or rain impend would scatter pea and grain in the interstice of the tile on the house of the well-to-do the pigeon and crow in they effort to get at the pea would loosen and perhaps overturn a few of the tile the granns would be send for to replace these would condemn the whole roof as leaky and the tile as old and unfit for use and would provide a job for himself and the tile-maker the nefarious profit of which they would share together cultivator of thatching-grass have be know deliberately and wantonly to set fire to village simply to raise the price of thatch and be better everything be emphatically ` on the square ' no _ pull _ and _ roping _ here no false entry no dodging of any kind fine gallant english gentleman meet each other in fair and honest emulation capture by the native -- the ` batan ' or buffalo herd -- pig charge -- they courage and ferocity -- destruction of game -- a close season for game the sport _ par excellence _ of indium be pig-sticking call it hog-hunting if you will i prefer the honest old-fashioned name with a good horse under one a fair country with not too many pitfall and ` lot of pig ' this sport become the most exciting that can be practice some prefer tiger shooting from elephant other like to stalk the lordly ibex on the steep himalayan slope but anyone who have ever enjoy a rattle after a pig over a good country will recall the fierce delight the eager thrill the wild mad excitement that flush he whole frame as he meet the infuriate charge of a good thirty-inch fighting boar and drive he trusty spear well home lay low the gallant gray tusker the indomitable unconquerable grisly boar the subject be well worn and though the theme be a noble one there be but few i fancy who have not read the record of some gallant fight where the highest skill the finest ride the most undaunted pluck and the cool keen daring of a practiced hand be not _ always _ successful against the headlong rush and furious charge of a bengal boar at bay a record of planter life in indium however such as this aim at be would be incomplete without some reference to the gallant tusker and so at the risk of tire my reader i must try to describe a pig-sticking party there be two distinct kind of boar in indium the black and the gray they disposition be very different the gray be fiercer and more pugnacious he be a vicious and implacable foe when rouse and always shew better fight than the black variety the great difference however be in the shape of the skull that of the black fellow be high over the frontal bone and not very long in proportion to height while the skull of the gray boar be never very high but be long and recede in proportion to height the black boar grow to a enormous size and the gray one be generally speak smaller make animal than the black the young of the two also differ in at least one important particular those of the gray pig be always bear striped but the young of the black variety be bear of that color and be not striped but a uniform black color throughout the two kind of pig sometimes interbreed but cross be not common and from the color size shape of the head and general behavior one can easily tell at a glance what kind of pig get up before he spear whether it be the heavy sluggish black boar or the veritable fiery vicious fighting gray tusker many story be tell of they enormous size and a ` forty-inch tusker ' be the established standard for a goliath among boar the best fighting boar however range from twenty-eight to thirty-two inch in height and i make bold to say that very few of the present generation of sportsman have ever see a veritable wild boar over thirty-eight inch high g who have have perhaps as much jungle experience as any man of he age in indium a careful observer and a finish sportsman tell I that the biggest _ boar _ he ever see be only thirty-eight inch high while the biggest _ pig _ he ever kill be a barren sow with three-inch tusk stick out of she gum she measure thirty-nine-and-a-half inch and fight like a demon i have shoot pig -- in heavy jungle where spear be impracticable -- over thirty-six inch high but the biggest pig i ever stick to my own spear be only twenty-eight inch and i do not think any pig have be kill in chumparun within the last ten or a dozen year at any rate over thirty-eight inch in some part of indium where pig be numerous and the jungle dense the native adopt a very ingenious mode of hunting i have frequently see it practice by the cowherd on the koosee _ derah _ ie the flat swampy jungle on the bank of the koosee when the annual flood have subside leave behind a thick deposit of mud wrack and brushwood the long thick grass soon shoot up to a amazing height and vast herd of cattle and tame buffalo come down to the jungle from the interior of the country where natural pasture be scarce they be attend by the owner and he assistant all generally belong to the _ guallum _ or cowherd caste although of course there be other caste employ the owner of the herd get leave to graze he cattle in the jungle by pay a certain fixed sum per head he fix on a high dry ridge of land where he run up a few grass hut for himself and man and there he erect line of grass and bamboo screen behind which he cattle take shelter at night from the cold south-east wind there be also a few hut of exceedingly frail construction for himself and he people this small colony in the midst of the universal jungle cover the country for mile round be call a _ batan _ at earliest dawn the buffalo be milk and then with they attendant herdsman they wend they way to the jungle where they spend the day and return again to the batan at night when they be again milk the milk be make into _ ghee _ or clarify butter and large quantity be send down to the town by country boat when we want to get up a hunt we generally send to the nearest _ batan _ for _ khubber _ ie news information the _ batanea _ or proprietor of the establishment be well post up every herdsman as he come in at night tell what animal he have see through the day and thus at the _ batan _ you hear where tiger and pig and deer be to be meet with where a unlucky cow have be kill in what ravine be the thickest jungle where the path be free from clay or quicksand what ford be safest and in short you get complete information on every point connect with the jungle and its wild inhabitant to these man the mysterious jungle reveal its most hidden secret surround by he herd of buffalo the _ guallum _ venture into the darkest recess and the most tangled thicket they have strange wild call by which they give each other notice of the approach of danger and when two or three of they meet each arm with he heavy iron-shod or brass-bound _ lathee _ or quarter staff they will not budge a inch out of they way for buffalo or boar nay they have be know to face the terrible tiger himself and fairly beat he away from the quiver carcase of some unlucky member of they herd they have generally some favorite buffalo on whose broad back they perch themselves as it browse through the jungle and from this elevated seat they survey the rest of the herd and note the incident of jungle life when they wish a little excitement or a change from they milk and rice diet there be hundred of pig around they have a broad sharp spear-head to which be attach a stout cord often make of twisted hide or hair into the socket of the spear be thrust a bamboo pole or shaft tough pliant and flexible the cord be wind round the spear and shaft and the loose end be then fasten to the middle of the pole have thus prepared he weapon the herdsman mount he buffalo and guide it slowly warily and cautiously to the haunt of the pig these be of course quite accustomed to see the buffalo grazing round they on all side and take no notice until the _ guallum _ be within striking distance when he have get close up to the pig he fancy he throw he spear with all he force the pig naturally bound off the shaft come out of the socket leave the spearhead sticking in the wound the rope uncoil of itself but be firmly fasten to the bamboo it bring up the pig at each bush and tear and lacerate the wound until either the spearhead come out or the wretched pig drop down dead from exhaustion and loss of blood the _ guallum _ follow upon he buffalo and frequently finish the pig with a few stroke of he _ lathee _ in any case he get he pork and it certainly be a ingenious and bold way of procure it wild pig be very destructive to crop during the night they revel in the cultivate field contiguous to the jungle and they destroy more by root up than by actually eat it be common for the ryot to dig a shallow pit and ensconce himself inside with he matchlock beside he he head be on a level with the ground he can discern any animal that come between he and the sky-line when a pig come in sight he wait till he be within sure distance and then put either a bullet or a charge of slug into he the pig be perhaps the most stubborn and courageous animal in indium even when pierce with several spear and bleed from numerous wound he preserve a sullen silence he disdain to utter a cry of fear and pain but maintain a bold front to the last and die with he face to the foe defiant and unconquered when hard press he scorn to continue he flight but wheeling round he make a determine charge very frequently to the utter discomfiture of he pursuer i have see many a fine horse fearfully cut by a charge pig and a determine boar over and over again break through a line of elephant and make good he escape there be no animal in all the vast jungle that the elephant dread more than a lusty boar i have see elephant that would stand the repeat charge of a wounded tiger turn tail and take to ignominious flight before the onset of a angry boar he thick short neck ponderous body and wedge-like head be admirably fit for crash through the thick jungle he inhabit and when he have make up he mind to charge very few animal can withstand he furious rush instance be quite common of he have make good he charge against a line of elephant cut and rip more than one severely he have be know to encounter successfully even the kingly tiger himself can it be wonder then that we consider he a ` foeman worthy of we steel ' to be a good pig-sticker be a recommendation that win acceptance everywhere in indium in a district like chumparun where nearly every planter be a ardent sportsman a good rider and spend nearly half he time on horseback it be a grow evil and threaten the total extinction of sport in some district i can remember when nearly every tank be good for a few brace of mallard duck or teal where never a feather be now to be seldom so that sport never interfere with the interest of honest hard work but meeting each other as we do and engage in exciting sport like pig-sticking cement we friendship keep we in health and encourage all the hardy tendency of we nature it whet we appetite it rouse all those robust virtue that have make englishman the man they be it send we back to work with lighter heart and renew energy bhaugulpore where there be still vast tract of forest jungle the haunt of the spotted deer nilghau leopard wolf and other wild animal along the bank of the koosee a rapid mountain river that roll its flood through numerous channel to join the gange there be immense tract of uncultivated land cover with tall elephant grass and give cover to tiger hog deer pig wild buffalo and even a occasional rhinoceros to say nothing of smaller game and wildfowl which be very plentiful the sal forest in north bhaugulpore generally keep to the high ridge which be compose of a light sandy soil very friable and not very fertile except for oil and indigo seed which grow most luxuriantly wherever the forest land have be clear in the shallow valley which lie between the ridge rice be chiefly cultivate and give large return the sal forest have be sadly thin by unscientific and indiscriminate cutting and very few fine tree now remain the earth be teem with insect chief amongst which be the dreaded and destructive white ant the high pointed nest of these destructive insect form of hardened mud be the commonest object one meet with in these forest solitude at interval beneath some wide spreading peepul or bhur tree one come on a rude forest shrine daub all over with red paint and with gaudy festoon of imitation flower cut from the pith of the plantain tree hang on every surround bough these shrine be sacred to _ chumpa buttee _ the hindoo diana protectress of herd deer buffalo huntsman and herdsman she be the recognize jungle goddess and be hold in great veneration by all the wild tribe and half-civilized denizen of the gloomy sal jungle the general color of the forest be a dingy green save when a deeper shade here and there show where the mighty bhur uprear its towering height or where the crimson flower of the _ seemul _ or cotton tree and the bronze-coloured foliage of the _ sunpul _ -lrb- a tree very like the ornamental beech in shrubbery at home -rrb- impart a more varied color to the generally pervade dark green of the universal sal the variety of tree be of course almost innumerable but the sal be so out of all proportion more numerous than any other kind that the forest well deserve they recognize name the sal be a fine hard wood of very slow growth the leaf be broad and glistening and in spring be beautifully tip with a reddish bronze which gradually tone down into the dingy green which be the prevail tint the _ sheshum _ or _ sissod _ a tree with bright green leaf much resemble the birch the wood of which be invaluable for cart wheel and such-like work be occasionally meet with there be the _ kormbhe _ a very tough wood with a red stringy bark of which the jungle man make a kind of touchwood for they matchlock and the _ parass _ whose peculiarity be that at time it burst into a wondrous wealth of bright crimson blossom without a leaf be on the tree the _ parass _ tree in full bloom be gorgeous after the blossom fall the dark-green leaf come out and be not much different in color from the sal then there be the _ mhowa _ with its lovely white blossom from which a strong spirit be distil and on which the deer pig and wild bear love to feast the peculiar sickly smell of the _ mhowa _ when in flower pervade the atmosphere for a great distance round and remind one forcibly of the peculiar sweet sickly smell of a brewery the hill _ sirre _ be a tall feathery-looking tree of most elegant shape towering above the other forest tree and the native strip it of its bark which they use to poison stream it seem to have some narcotic or poisonous principle easily soluble in water for when put in any quantity in a stream or piece of water it cause all the fish to become apparently paralyzed and rise to the surface where they float about quite stupified and helpless and become a easy prey to the poach ` banturs ' and ` moosahur ' who adopt this wretched mode of fishing along the bank of the stream vegetation get very luxurious and among the thick undergrowth be find some lovely fern broad-leaved plant and flower of every hue all alike nearly scentless here be no odorous breath of violet or honeysuckle no delicate perfume of primrose or sweetbriar only a musty dank earthy smell which get more and more pronounced as the mist rise along with the deadly vapor of the night sleep in these forest be very unhealthy there be a most fatal miasma all through the year less during the hot month but very bad during and immediately after the annual rain and in September and October nearly every soul in the jungly tract be smite with fever the vapor only rise to a certain height above the ground and at the elevation of ten foot or so i believe one could sleep in the jungle with impunity but it be dangerous at all time to sleep in the forest unless at a considerable elevation the absence of all those delicious smell which make a walk through the woodland at home so delightful be conspicuous in the sal forest and another of the most noticeable feature be the extreme silence the oppressive stillness that reign you know how full of melody be a english wood when thrush blackbird mavi linnet and a thousand warbler flit from tree to tree how the choir ring out its full anthem of sweetest sound till every bush and tree seem a center of sweet strain soft low liquid trill and full ripe gush of melody and song but it be not thus in a indian forest there be actually few bird as you brush through the long grass and trample the tangled undergrowth put aside the sprawl branch or dodge under the pliant arm of the creeper you may flush a black or gray partridge raise a covey of quail or startle a quiet family party of peafowl but there be no sweet singer flit about to make the vault arcade of the forest echo to they music the hornbill dart with a succession of long bound flight from one tall tree to another the large woodpecker tap a hollow tree close by he gorgeous plumage glisten like a mimic rainbow in the sun a flight of green parrot sweep scream above you head the _ golden oriole _ or mango bird the _ koel _ with here and there a red-tufted _ bulbul _ make a faint attempt at a chirrup but as a rule the deep silence be unbroken save by the melancholy hoot of some blinking owl and the soft monotonous coo of the ringdove or the green pigeon the exquisite honey-sucker as delicately form as the petal of a fairy flower flit noiselessly about from blossom to blossom the native call it the ` muddpenah ' or drinker of honey there be innumerable butterfly of graceful shape and gorgeous color what few bird there be have beautiful plumage there be a faint rustle of leave a faint far hum of insect life but it feel so silent so unlike the wood at home you be oppress by the solemn stillness and feel almost nervous as you push warily along for at any moment a leopard wolf or hyena may get up before you or you may disturb the siesta of a sounder of pig or a herd of deer up in those forest on the border of nepaul which be call the _ morung _ there be a great many variety of parrot all of they very beautiful there be first the common green parrot with a red beak and a circle of salmon-coloured feather round its neck they be very noisy and destructive and flock together to the field where they do great damage to the crop the _ lutkun sooga _ be a exquisitely-coloured bird about the size of a sparrow the _ ghur -lrb- = a -rrb- l _ a large red and green parrot with a crimson beak the _ tota _ a yellowish-green color and the male with a breast as red as blood they call it the _ amereet bhelum _ another lovely little parrot the _ taeteea sooga _ have a green body red head and black throat but the most showy and brilliant of all the tribe be the _ putsoogee _ the body be a rich living green red wing yellow beak and black throat there be a tuft of vivid red as a topknot and the tail be a brilliant blue the under feather of the tail be a pure snowy white at time the silence be break by a loud metallic bell-like cry very like the yodel you hear in the alp you hear it rise sharp and distinct ` looralei ' and as suddenly cease this be the cry of the _ kookoor gh -lrb- = e -rrb- t _ a bird not unlike a small pheasant with a reddish-brown back and a fawn-coloured breast the _ sherra _ be another green parrot a little larger than the _ putsoogee _ but not so beautifully color there be generally a green slime-covered sluggish stream in all these forest its channel choke with rot leaf and decay vegetable matter the water should never be drunk until it have be boil and filter at interval the stream open out and form a clear rush-fringed pool and the tree recede on either bank leave a lovely grassy glade where the deer and nilghau come to drink on the glassy bosom of the pool in the center fine duck mallard and teal can frequently be find and the rush round the margin be to a certainty good for a couple of brace of snipe sometimes on a withered branch overhang the stream you can see perch the _ ahur _ or great black fish-hawk it have a grate discordant cry which it utter at interval as it sit plume its black feather above the pool the dark ibis and the ubiquitous paddy-bird be of course also find here and where the land be low and marshy and the stream crawl along through several channel you be sure to come across a couple of red-headed _ sarus _ serpent bird a crane and a solitary heron the _ moosahernee _ be a black and white bird i fancy a sort of ibis and be good eat the _ dokahur _ be another fine big bird black body and white wing and as its name -lrb- derive from _ dokha _ a shell -rrb- imply it be the shell-gatherer or snail-eater and give good shooting when you have determine to beat the forest you first get you coolie and villager assemble and send they some mile or two mile ahead under charge of some of the head man to beat the jungle towards you while you look out for a likely spot shady conceal and cool where you wait with you gun till the game be drive up to you the whole arrangement be generally make of course under you own supervision by you _ shekarry _ or gamekeeper as i suppose you might call he he be generally a thin wiry silent man well verse in all the lore of the wood acquaint with the name appearance and habit of every bird and beast in the forest he know they haunt and when they be to be find at home he will track a wounded deer like a bloodhound and can tell the sign and almost impalpable evidence of a animal 's whereabouts the knowledge of which go to make up the genuine hunter when all be still around and only the distant shout of the beater fall faintly at interval on the ear he keen hearing detect the light patter of hoof or paw on the crisp withered leave he hawk-like glance can pick out from the deepest shade the sleek coat or hide of the leopard or the deer and even before the animal have come in sight he sens tell he whether it be young or old whether it be alarmed or walk in blind confidence in fact i have know a good shekarry tell you exactly what animal be come whether bear leopard fox deer pig or monkey the best shekarry i ever have be a nepaulee call ` mehrman singh ' he have the regular tartar physiognomy of the nepaulese small oblique twinkling eye high cheekbone flattish nose and scanty moustache he be a tall wiry man with a remarkably light springy step a bold erect carriage and be altogether a fine manly independent fellow he have none of the fawn obsequiousness which be so common to the hindoo but be a merry laughing fellow with a keen love of sport and a great appreciation of humor he gun be fearfully and wonderfully make it be a long heavy flint gun with a tremendously heavy barrel and the stock all splice and splinter tie in place with bit of string i would rather not have be in the immediate vicinity of the weapon when he fire it and yet he contrived to do some good shooting with it he be wonderfully patient in stalk a animal or wait for its near approach as he never venture on a long shot and do not understand we objection to pot-shooting he shot be compose of jagged little bit of iron chip from a old _ kunthee _ or cooking-pot and he powder be truly unique be like lump of charcoal about the size of small raisin a shekarry fill about four or five finger ' depth of this into he gun then a handful of old iron and with a little touch of english powder prick in with a pin as priming he be ready for execution on any game that may come within reach of a safe pot-shot when the gun go off there be a mighty splutter a roar like that of a small cannon and the slug go hurtle through the bush carry away twig and leave and not unfrequently smash up the game so that it be almost useless for the table the _ bantur _ who principally inhabit these jungle be mostly of nepaulese origin they be a sturdy independent people and the woman have fair skin and be very pretty unchastity be very rare and the infidelity of a wife be almost unknown if it be find out mutilation and often death be the penalty exact from the unfortunate woman they wear one long loose flow garment much like the skirt of a gown this be tightly twist round the body above the bosom leave the neck and arm quite bare they be fond of ornament -- nose ear toe and arm and even ancle be load with silver ring and circlet some decorate they nose and the middle parting of the hair with a greasy-looking red pigment while nearly every grown-up woman have she arm neck and low down on the collar bone most artistically tattoo in a variety of close elaborate pattern the woman all work in the clearing sowing and weeding and reap the rice barley and other crop they do most of the digging where that be necessary the man confine themselves to plough and wood-cutting at the latter employment they be most expert they use the axe in the most masterly manner but they mode of cut be fearfully wasteful they always leave some three foot of the best part of the wood in the ground very rarely cut a tree close down to the root many of they be good charcoal-burner and indeed they principal occupation be supply the adjacent village with charcoal and firewood they use small narrow-edged axis for fell but for lop they invariably use the nepaulese national weapon -- the _ kookree _ this be a heavy curved knife with a broad blade the edge very sharp and the back thick and heavy in use it they slash right and leave with a quick downward stroke draw the blade quickly toward they as they strike they be wonderfully dexterous with the _ kookree _ and will clear away brush and underwood almost as quickly as a man can walk they pack they charcoal rice or other commodity in long narrow basket which they sling on a pole carry on they shoulder as we see the chinese do in the well know picture on tea-chest they be all hindoo in religion but be very fond of rice-whiskey although not so abstemious in this respect as the hindoo of the plain they be a much finer race both physically and morally as a rule they be truthful honest brave and independent they be always glad to see you laugh out merrily at you as you pass and be wonderfully hospitable it would be a nice point for sir wilfrid lawson to reconcile the use of rice-whiskey with this marked superiority in all moral virtue in the whiskey-drinking as against the totally-abstaining hindoo to return to mehrman singh he face be seam with smallpox mark and he have seven or eight black patch on it the first time i see he cause by the splintering of he flint when he let off he antediluvian gun when he see my breechloader the first he have ever behold he admiration be unbounded he tell I he have come on a leopard asleep in the forest one day and be very fond of give feast to each other they dinner-party i assure you be very often ` great affair ' they be not mean in they arrangement and the want of the inner man be very amply provide for they the acacia squat down in a circle with half-a-dozen of these sylvan cup around the attendant fill one with rice another with _ dhall _ a third with goat 's - flesh a fourth with _ turkaree _ or vegetable a fifth with chutnee pickle or some kind of preserve curd ghee a little oil perhaps sugar plantain and other fruit be not want and the whole be wash down with copious draught of fiery rice-whiskey or where it can be procure with palm-toddy not unfrequently dancing boy or girl be in attendance and the horrid din of tom-tom cymbal a squeak fiddle or a twanging sitar rattle castanets and ear-piercing song from the dusky _ prima donna _ make night hideous until the gray dawn peep over the dark forest line early in January 1875 my camp be at a place in the sal jungle call lohurneah i have be collect rent and look after my seed cultivation and pat and we sport district engineer have join I we determine to have a beat for deer mehrman singh have report numerous herd in the vicinity of we camp during the night we have be disturb by the reveller at such a feast in the village as i have be describe we have fill cartridge see to we gun and make every preparation for the beat and early in the morning the coolie and idler of the forest village all round be range in circle about we camp swallow a hasty breakfast we mount we pony and follow by we ragged escort make off for the forest on the way we meet a crowd of bantur with bundle of stake and great coil of strong heavy net send the coolie on ahead under charge of several headman and peon we plunge into the gloom of the forest leave we pony and groom outside when we come to a likely-looking spot the bantur begin operation by fix up the net on the stake and between tree till a line of strong net extend across the forest for several hundred yard we then go ahead leave the net behind we and each take up he station about 200 yard in front the man with the net then hide themselves behind tree and crouch in the underwood with we kookry we cut down several branch stick they in the ground in front and ensconce ourselves in this artificial shelter behind we and between we and the net be a narrow cart track lead through the forest and the reason of we take this position be give I by pat who be a old hand at jungle shooting when deer be be drive they be intensely suspicious and of course frighten they know every spot in the jungle and be acquaint with all the path track and open place in the forest when they be near a open glade or a road they slacken they pace and go slowly and warily forward a old buck generally lead when he have carefully reconnoitr and examine the suspect place in front and find it clear to all appearance they again put on the pace and clear the open ground at they greatest speed the best chance of a shot be when a path be in front of _ they _ and behind _ you _ as then they be go slowly at first when i use to go out after they i often get a open glade or road in _ front _ of I but experience soon tell I that pat 's plan be the best as this be a beat not so much for real sport as to show I how the villager manage these affair we be all under pat 's direction and he could not have choose better ground i be on the extreme left behind a clump of young tree with the sluggish muddy stream on my leave we engineer to my right be about one hundred yard off and pat himself on the extreme right at about the same distance from h behind we be the road and in the rear the long line of net with they conceal watcher the net be so set up on the stake that when a animal bound along and touch the net it fall over he and ere he can extricate himself from its mesh the vigilant banturs rush out and despatch he with spear and club we wait a long time hear nothing of the beater and watch the red and black ant hurry to and fro huge green-bellied spider oscillate backwards and forwards in they strong systematically weave web a small mungoose keep peep out at I from the turn round to look behind and expose the beautifully curve neck full to my aim i fire and have the satisfaction of see the fine buck topple over seemingly hard hit a shot on my right and two shot in rapid succession further on shewed I that pat and h be also at work and then the whole forest seem alive with frightened madly-plunging pig deer and other animal i fire at and wound a enormous boar that come rush past and now the cry of the coolie in front as they come troop on mingle with the shout of the man at the net where the work of death evidently be go on it be most exciting while it last but after all i do not think it be honest sport the only apology i could make to myself be that the deer and pig be far too numerous and do immense damage to the crop and if not thin out they would soon have make the grow of any crop whatever a impossibility the monkey be a sacred animal be never molest by the native and the damage he do in a night to a crop of wheat or barley be wend they way back from the field where they have be revel all night you can shoot number of they i use to go about twenty or thirty yard into the jungle and walk slowly along keep that distance from a good retriever will sometimes flush they but the best way be to go along the edge of the jungle in the early morn as i have describe the peachick about seven or eight month old be deliciously tender manager _ the generic name for all deer be in hindustanus hurin but the nepaulese call it cheeter the male spotted deer they call kubra the female kubree these spotted deer keep almost exclusively to beautifully spotted skin flash through the dark green foliage he antler lay back over he withers he look the very embodiment of grace and swiftness he be very timid and not easily stalk in march and April when a strong west wind be blow it rustle the myriad of leaf that dry as tinder encumber the earth this perpetual rustle prevent the deer from hear the footstep of a approach foe they generally betake themselves then to some patch of grass or long-crop outside the jungle altogether and if you want they in those month it be in such place and not inside the forest at all that you must search like all the deer tribe they be very curious and a bit of rag tie to a tree or a cloth put over a bush will not unfrequently entice they within range old shekarry will tell you that as long as the deer go on feeding and flap they ear you may continue you approach as soon as they throw up the head and keep the ear still they suspicion have be arouse and if you want venison you must be as still as a rock till you game be again lull into security as soon as the ear begin flap again you may continue you stalk but at the slightest noise the noble buck will be off like a flash of lightning you should never go out in the forest with white clothes as you be then a conspicuous mark for all the prying eye that be invisible to you the best color be dun brown dark gray or dark green when you see a deer have become suspicious and no cover be near stand perfectly erect and rigid and do not leave you leg apart the ` forked-parsnip ' formation of the ` human form divine ' be detect at a glance but there be just a chance that if you leg be draw together and you remain perfectly motionless you may be mistake for the stump of a tree or at the best some less dangerous enemy than man as we ride and to give a series of picture of what be go on there if i occasionally touch on what may to indian reader seem well-worn ground they will forgive I the leopard then as a rule keep to the wooded part of indium in the long grassy jungle border the koosee he be not generally meet with he be essentially a predatory animal always on the outlook for a meal round the village nestle amid they sal forest he be continually on the prowl look out for a goat a calf or unwary dog he appearance and habit be well know he generally select for he lair a retired spot surround by dense jungle the one we be after now have he home in a matted jungle grow out of a pool of water which have collect in a long hollow form the receptacle of the surface drainage from the adjacent slope this hollow stretch for mile towards the creek which we have be beat up and the locality have moisture and other concur element in its favor the vegetation have attain a luxuriance rarely see in the dry upland where the west wind lick up the moisture and the soil be arid and unpromise the mat intertwine branch of the creeper have form a almost impervious screen and on the basis thus form amid the branch and creeper the leopard have form they lair beneath be a still stagnant pool above be the leafy foliage the track lead down to a well-worn path climb like a cat as the leopard can do they find no difficulty in gain a footing on the mass of vegetation they generally select some retired spot like this and be very seldom see in the daytime with the approach of night however they begin they wander in quest of prey in a beat such as we be have ` all be fish that come to the net ' and leopard if they be in the jungle have to yield to the advance of the beater like the other denizen of the forest experience tell you that the leopard be daring and ferocious old experienced hand warn you that unless you can make sure of you shot it be unwise to fire at a leopard approach it be better to wait till he have get past you or at all event be ` broadside on ' if you only wind he as he be approach he will almost to a certainty make straight at you but if you shoot he as he be go past he will madden by pain and anger go straight forward and you escape he charge he be more courageous than a tiger and a very dangerous customer at close quarter up in one of the forest in oudh a friend of mine be out one day after leopard with a companion who belong to the forest department my friend 's companion fire at a leopard as it be approach he and wound it severely nothing daunted and recognize whence its hurt have come it charge directly down on the conceal sportsman and before he could half realize the position spring on he catch he left arm in its tooth and begin maul he with its claw he presence of mind do not desert he notice close by the stump of a sal tree that have be eat by white ant till the harder part of the wood alone remain stand up hard and sharp like so many spike of steel and know that the leopard be already badly wound and in all probability struggle for he life he manage to drag the struggle animal up to the stump jam he left arm yet further into the open mouth of the wound beast and be a strong man by pure physical force dash the leopard 's brain out on the jagged edge of the stump it be a splendid instance of presence of mind he be horribly maul of course in fact i believe he lose he arm but he save he life it show the danger of only wound a leopard especially if he be come towards you always wait till he have pass you station if it be practicable if you _ must _ shoot take what care you can that the shot be a sure one in some of the hill station and indeed in the village on the plain it be very common for a leopard to make he appearance in the house or verandah of a evening one be shoot in bhaugulpore station by the genial and respected chaplain on a Sunday morning two or three year ago as we go along h tell we a humorous story of a assistant in the public work come to he rescue with a stout _ lathee _ between they they succeed in kill the wound animal but not before it have leave its mark on a very sensitive portion of he frame the moral be if you go after leopard be sure you kill he at once they seldom attack a strong well-grown animal calf however goat and dog be frequently carry off by they the young of deer and pig too fall victim and when nothing else can be have peafowl have be know to furnish they a meal in my factory in oudh i have a small graceful four-horned antelope it be carry off by a leopard from the garden in broad daylight and in face of a gang of coolie the most commonly practice mode of leopard shooting be to tie a goat up to a tree you have a _ mychan _ erect that be a platform elevate on tree above the ground here you take you seat attract by the bleating of the goat the prowl leopard approach he intended victim if you be on the watch you can generally detect he approach they steal on with extreme caution be intensely to be the same brute i have already describe we mode of beat the jungle be close and there be a great growth of young tree i be again on the right and near the edge of the forest beyond be a glade before we pat be close to a mighty bhur tree whose branch send down shoot from the parent stem have plant round it a colony of vigorous support it be a magnificent tree with dense shade all be solemn
