and bye mehrman singh and the rest of the beater come up and the joy of the villager be gratify these be doubtless the two leopard we have hear so much about for which i have sit up and watch it be amusing to see some villager whose pet goat or value calf have be carry off now come up strike the dead body of the leopard and abuse it in the most unmeasured term such a crowd round as there be such a noise and such excitement while wait for the horse to be bring and while the excited mob of beater and coolie carry off the dead animal to the camp to be skin we amuse ourselves by try we rifle at a huge tree that grow on the further side of the rice swamp we find the effect of the ` express ' bullet to be tremendous it splintered up and burst the bark and body of the tree into fragment its effect on a animal be even more wonderful on look afterwards at the leopard which have be shoot we find that my bullet have touch the base of the shoulder near the collar-bone it have go downward through the neck under the collar-bone and strike the shoulder there it have splintered up and make a frightful wound scatter its fragment all over the chest and cut and lacerate everything in its way for big game the ` express ' be simply invaluable for all-round shooting perhaps a no 12 smooth-bore be the best it should be snap action with rebound lock you should have facility and instrument for load cartridge a good cartridge belt be a good thing for carry they but go where you will now where there be game to be kill a no 12 b l will enable you to participate in whatever shooting be go such a one as i have describe would satisfy all the wish of any young man who perhaps can only afford one gun as we ride slowly along we learn many curious fact of jungle and native life from the follower and by notice little incident happen before we eye pat who be so well verse in jungle life and its tradition tell we of a curious moveable feast which the native of these part hold annually generally in march or April which be call the _ sirwah purrub _ it seem to be somewhat like the old carnival of the middle age i have read that in sardinium and italy and switzerland something similar take place the _ sirwah purrub _ be a sort of festival hold in honor of the native diana -- the _ chumpa buttee _ before refer to on the appoint day all the male in the forest village without exception go a-hunting old spear be furbish up miraculous gun of even yet more ancient lineage than mehrman singh 's dangerous flintpiece be bring out from dusty hiding-place battle-axis bow and arrow hatchet club and weapon of all sort be look up and the motley crowd hie to the forest the one party beat up the game to the other some go fishing other try to secure a quail or partridge but it be a point of honor that something must be slay if game be not plentiful they will even go to another village and slay a goat which rather than return empty-handed they will bear in triumph home the woman meet the return hunter and if there have be a fortunate beat there be a great feast in the village during the evening and far on into the night the net be use and in this way they generally have some game to divide in the village on they return from the hunt ordinarily they seethe the flesh and pour the whole contents of the cooking-pot into a mess of boil rice with the addition of a little salt this be to they very palatable fare they be very good cook with very simple appliance with a little mustard oil or clarify butter a few vegetable or a cut-up fish they can be very successful the food however be generally smoke from the cow-dung fire if you be much out in these village this smoke constantly hang about cling to you clothes and flavor you food but the native seem to like it amazingly in the cold morning of December or January it hang about like the peat smoke in a highland village round every house be great stack and pile of cow-dung cake before every house be a huge pile of ash and the villager cower round this as the evening fall or before the sun have dissipate the mist of the morning during the day the village dog burrow in the ash hover in a dense cloud about the roof and eaves and along the lower branch of the tree in filmy layer the smoke almost choke one to ride through it i have see a native sit till half-choked in a dense column of this smoke he be too lazy to shift he position the fume of pungent smoke half smother he tear run from he eye he splutter and cough and abuse the smoke and its grandfather and maternal uncle and all its other known relative but he prefer semi-suffocation to the trouble of budge a inch sometimes the energy of these people be surprising to go to a fair or feast or on a pilgrimage they will walk mile upon mile subsist on parched pea or rice and carry heavy burden in company they sing and carol blithely enough when alone they be very taciturn man and woman walking together the man first with he _ lathee _ or staff the woman behind carry child or bundle and often look fagged and tired enough take vegetable or rice or other commodity to the bazaar the carrier often sling he burden to the two end of a pole wear over the shoulder much as chinaman do but they generally make they load into one bundle which they carry on the head or which they sling if not large and bulky over they back roll up in one of they cloth during the rice-planting season they toil in mud and water from earliest morn till late into twilight bend and stoop all the day they lower extremity up to the knee sometimes in water and the scorching sun beating on they back they certainly show they patient plod industry for it be downright honest hard work the young rice be take from the nursery patch where it have be sow thick some time previously when the rice-field be ready -- a sloppy muddy embanked little quagmire -- the ryot get he bundle of young rice-plant and shove in two or three at a time with he finger and thumb these afterwards form the tuft of rice its growth be very rapid sometimes in case of flood the rice actually grow with the rise of the water always keep its tip above the stream if wholly submerge for any length of time it die there be over a hundred variety some be only suit for very deep marshy soil other such as the _ s -lrb- = a -rrb- tee _ or sixty-days rice can be grow on comparatively high land and ripen early if rain be scanty the _ s -lrb- = a -rrb- tee _ and other rice crop have to be weed it be cut with a jagged-edged sort of reaping-hook call a _ hussooa _ the cut bundle be carry from the field by woman girl and lad they could not take cart in many instance into the swamp at such time you see every little dyke or embankment with a crowd of bustling villager each with a heavy bundle of grain on he head hurry to and fro like a stream of busy ant the woman with clothes tuck up above the knee plod and plash through the water they go at a half run a kind of fast trot and hardly a word be speak -- garner the rice crop be too important a operation to dawdle and gossip over each hurry off with he burden to the little family threshing-floor dump down he load give a weary grunt straighten he back give a yawn then off again to the field for another load it be no use leave a bundle on the field where food be so eagerly look for by such a dense population where there be hungry mouth and empty stomach in every village a bundle of rice would be go by the morning as in greece where every man have to watch he vineyard at night so here the _ kureehan _ or threshing-floor each have its watchman at night for the protection of the grow crop the villager club together and appoint a watchman or _ chowkeydar _ whom they pay by give he a small percentage on the yield or a small fractional proportion of the area he have to guard with its standing crop may be make over to he as a recompense they thresh out the rice when it have mature a little on the threshing-floor four to six bullock be tie in a line to a post in the center and round this they slowly pace in a circle they be not muzzle and the poor brute seem rather to enjoy the unwonted luxury of feed while they work when there be a good wind the grain be winnow it be lift either in bamboo scoop or in the two hand the wind blow the chaff or _ bhoosa _ on to a heap and the fine fresh rice remain behind the grain merchant now do a good business rice must be sell to pay the rent the money-lender and other clamour creditor the _ bunniah _ will take repayment in kind they put on the interest and cheat in the weighment and measurement so much have to be give to the weigh-man as a perquisite if seed have be borrow it have now to be return at a ruinous rate of interest some seed must be save for next year and a average _ poor _ ryot the cultivator of but a little hold very soon see the result of he harvest melt away leave little for wife and little one to live on he never get free of the money-lender he will have to go out and work hard for other as well as get up he own little land no chance of a new bullock this year and the old one be get wear out and thin the wife must dispense with she promise ornament or dress for the poor ryot it be a miserable hand-to-mouth existence when crop be poor as a rule he be never out of debt he live on the scantiest fare hunger often pinch he he know none of the luxury of life notwithstanding all the majority be patient frugal industrious and to the full extent of they scanty means even charitable and benevolent with the average ryot a little business go a great way there be some irreconcileable discontented worthless fellow in every village all more or less count a lie as rather a good thing to be expert in they lie naturally simply and instinctively : but with all he fault and they be doubtless many i confess to a great liking for the average hindoo ryot at time however they apathy and laziness be amazing they be very childish pet and easily rouse in a quarrel however they generally confine themselves to vituperation and abuse and seldom come to blow as a instance of they fatalism or apathetic indolence i can remember a village on the estate i be manage take fire it be quite close to the factory i have my pony saddle at once and gallop off for the burning village it be a long straggle one with a good masonry well in the center shadow by a mighty _ peepul _ tree the wind be blow the fire right along and if no obstruction be offer would sweep off every hut in the place the only soul who be try to do a thing be a young brahmin watchman belong to the factory he have succeed in remove some brass jar of he own and be save some grain one woman be rock to and fro beat she breast and cry there sit the rest of the apathetic villager in group not lift a finger not stir a step but calmly look on while the devour element be lick up hut after hut and destroy they little all in a few minute some of my servant syce and factory man have arrive i tie up the pony order my man to pull down a couple of hut in the center and try to infuse some energy into the villager not a bit of it they would _ not _ stir they would not even draw a bucket of water however my man get earthen pot i dig up fresh earth and throw it on the two dismantle hut drag away as much of the thatch and _ debris _ as we could the fire lick we face and actually get a footing on the first house beyond the frail opening we have try to make but we persevere and ultimately stay the fire and save about two third of the village i never see such a instance of complete apathy some of the inhabitant even have not untied the cattle in the shed they seem quite prostrated however as we work on and they begin to see that all be not yet lose they begin to buckle to yet even then they principal object be to save they brass pot and cooking utensil thing that could not possibly burn and which they might have leave alone with perfect safety a hindoo village be as inflammable as touchwood the house be generally build of grass wall connect with thin batten of bamboo the roof be bamboo and thatch thatch fence surround all the little courtyard leave refuse cowdung fuel and wood be pile up round every hut at each door be a open air fire which smoulder all day a stray puff of wind make a inquisitive visit round the corner and before one can half realize the catastrophe the village be on fire then each only think of he own goods there be no combined effort to stay the flame in the hot west wind of march April and may these fire be of very frequent occurrence in bhaugulpore i have see from my verandah three village on fire at one and the same time in some part of oudh among the sal forest village after village be burn down annually and i have see the same catastrophe visit the same village several time in the course of one year these fire arise from pure carelessness sheer apathy and laziness sanitary precaution too be very insufficient practically there be none huge unsightly water-hole fill during the rain with the drainage of all the dung-heap and mound of offal and filth that abound in the village swelter under the hot summer sun they get cover with a rank green scum and if they inky depths be stir the foulest and most fearful odour issue forth in these filthy pool the villager often perform they ablutions they do not scruple to drink the putrid water which be no doubt a hotbed and regular nursery for fever and choleraic and other disorder many home reader be but little acquaint with the indian village system and i shall village -- village functionary -- the barber -- bathing habit -- the village well -- the school -- the child -- the village bazaar -- the landowner and he dwelling -- the ` putwarrie ' or village accountant -- the blacksmith -- the ` punchayiet ' or village jury system -- we legal system in indium -- remark on the administration of justice a typical village in behar be a heterogeneous collection of thatched hut apparently set down at random -- as indeed it be for every one erect he hut wherever whim or caprice lead he or wherever he can get a piece of vacant land grove of feathery bamboo and broad-leaved plumy-looking plantain almost conceal the hut and building several small orchard of mango surround the village the road lead to and from it be merely well-worn cattle track -- in the rain a perfect quagmire and in the hot weather dusty and confine between straggle hedge of aloe or prickly pear these hedge be festoon with mass of clinging luxuriant creeper among which sometimes struggle up a custard apple a avocado pear or a wild plum-tree the latter be a prickly straggle tree call the _ bhyre _ the wood be very hard and be often use for make plough the fruit be a little hard yellow crisp fruit with a big stone inside and very sweet when it be ripe the village urchin throw stick up among the branch and feast on the golden shower on many of the bank border the road thatch grass or rather strong upright wave grass with a beautiful feathery plume be plant this be use to make the wall of the house and these be then plaster outside and in with clay and cowdung the tall hedge of dense grass keep what little breeze there may be away from the traveller the road be something like a irish ` boreen ' want only its beauty and freshness on a hot day the atmosphere in one of these village road be stifle and load with dust these house with they grass wall and thatched roof be call _ kutcha _ as oppose to more pretentious structure of burnt brick with maybe a tiled sloping or flat plaster roof which be call _ pucca pucca _ literally mean ` ripe ' as oppose to _ cutcha _ ` unripe ' but the rich oriental tongue have adapt it to almost every kind of secondary meaning thus a man who be true upright respect a man to be depend on be call a _ pucca _ man it be a word in constant use among anglo-indian a _ pucca _ road be one which be bridge and metal if you make a engagement with a friend and he want to impress you with its importance he will ask you now be that _ pucca _ ' and so on other house in the village be compose of unburnt brick cement with mud or maybe compose of mud wall and thatched roof these be a compound sort of erection be call _ cutcha pucca _ in the _ cutcha _ house live the poorer caste the _ chumar _ or worker in leather the _ moosahm doosadh _ or _ gwallah _ the _ dorne _ or scavenger feeder on offal have to live apart in a _ tolah _ which might be call a small suburb by themselves the _ dorne _ drag from the village any animal that happen to die they generally pursue the handicraft of basket making or mat making and the _ dorne tolah _ can always be know by the pig and fowl prowl about in search of food and the _ dorne _ and he family splitting up bamboo and weave mat and basket at the door of they miserable habitation to the higher caste both pig arid fowl be unclean and a abomination _ moosahm doosadh _ and other poor caste such as _ dangur _ keep however a army of gaunt lean hungry-looking pig these may be see root and wallow in the marsh when the rice have be cut or forage among the mango grove to pick up any stray unripe fruit that may have escape the keen eye of the hungry and swarming child there be yet another small _ tolah _ or suburb call the _ kusbee tolah _ here live the miserable outcast who minister to the worst passion of we nature these degrade being be banish from the more respectable portion of the community but here as in we own highly civilised and favored land vice hover by the side of virtue and the hindoo village contain the same element of happiness and misery profligacy and probity purity and degradation as the fine home city that be a name in the mouth of man every village form a perfect little commonwealth it contain all the element of self-existence it be quite a little commune so far as social life be concern there be a hereditary blacksmith washerman potter barber and writer the _ dhobee _ or washerman can always be know by the propinquity of he donkey diminutive animal which he use to transport he bundle of unsavoury dirty clothes to the pool or tank where the linen be wash on great country road you may often see string of donkey laden with bag of grain which they transport from far-away village to the big bazaar but if you see a laden donkey near a village be sure the _ dhobee _ be not far off here as elsewhere the _ hajam _ or barber be a great gossip and generally a favorite he use no soap and have a most uncouth-looking razor yet he shave the head beard moustache and armpit of he customer with great deftness the lower class of native shave the hair of the head and of the armpit for the sake of cleanliness and for other obvious reason the higher class be very regular in they ablutions every morning be the water cold or warm the rajpoot and brahmin the respectable middle class and all in the village who lay any claim to social position have they _ goosal _ or bath some hie to the nearest tank or stream at all hour of the day at any ferry or landing stage you will see swarthy fine-looking fellow up to mid waist in the water scrub vigorously they bronze arm and neck and chest they clean they tooth with the end of a stick which they chew at one extremity till they loosen the fiber and with this improvise toothbrush and some wood ash for paste they make they look as white and clean as ivory there be generally a large masonry well in the middle of the village with a broad smooth _ pucca _ platform all round it it have be build by some former father of the hamlet to perpetuate he memory to fulfil a vow to the god perhaps simply from goodwill to he fellow townsman at all event there be generally one such in every village it be generally shadow by a huge _ bhur peepul _ or tamarind tree here may always be see the busiest sight in the village pretty young woman chatter laugh and talk and assume all sort of picturesque attitude as they fill they waterpot the village matron gossip and sometimes quarrel as they pull away at the windlass over the deep cool well on the platform be a group of fat brahmin nearly nude they lighter skin contrast well with the duskier hue of the lower class there be several group with damp drapery cling to they glisten skin they pour brass pot of cold water over they drip body they rub themselves briskly and gasp again as the cool element pour over head and shoulder they sit down while some young attendant or relation vigorously rub they down the back while sit they clean they foot thus amid much laughing and talk and quaint gesture and not a little expectoration they perform they ablutions not unfrequently the more wealthy anoint they body with mustard oil which at all event keep out cold and chill as they claim that it do though it be not fragrant round the well you get all the village news and scandal it be always throng in the morning and evening and only desert when the fierce heat of midday plunge the village into a lethargic silence unbroken save where the hum of the hand-mill or the thump of the husking-post tell where some busy damsel or matron be grind flour or husk rice in the cool shadow of she hut for the want of she lord and master education be now make rapid stride it be foster by government and many of the wealthier landowner or zemindar subscribe liberally for a schoolmaster in they village near the principal street then in a sort of lane shadow by a old mango-tree we come on the village school the little fellow have all discard they upper clothes on account of the heat and with much noise sway the body backwards and forwards and monotonously intone they grind away at the mill of learning and try to get a knowledge of book other dusky urchin figure away with lump of chalk on the floor or on flat piece of wood to serve as copy-book the din increase as the stranger pass : go into a english school the stranger would probably cause a momentary pause in the hum that be always hear in school the little hindoo scholar probably wish to impress you with a sense of he assiduity he raise he voice sway the body more briskly keep he one eye firmly fix on he task while with the other he throw a keen swift glance over you which embrace every detail of you costume and not improbably include a shrewd estimate of you disposition and character hindoo child never seem to I to be boy or girl they be preternaturally acute and observant you seldom see they play together they seem to be bear with the gift of tell a lie with most portentous gravity they wear a air of the most winning candor and guileless innocence when they be all the while plot some petty scheme against you they be certainly far more precocious than english child they realize the hard struggle for life far more quickly the poorer class can hardly be say to have any childhood as soon as they can toddle they be send to weed cut grass gather fuel tend herd or do anything that will bring they in a small pittance and ease the burden of the struggle parent i think the child of the higher and middle class very pretty they have beautiful dark thoughtful eye and a most intelligent expression very young baby however be miserably nurse they hair be allow to get all tangled and mat into unsightly knot they face be seldom wash and they eye be paint with antimony about the lid and be often rheumy and run with water the use of the pocket handkerchief be sadly neglect there be generally one open space or long street in we village and in a hamlet of any importance there be weekly or bi-weekly a bazaar or market from early morning in all direction from solitary hut in the forest from struggle little croft in the rice land from fisherman 's dwelling perch on the bank of the river from lonely camp in the grass jungle where the herd and he family live with they cattle from all the petty thorpe about come the woman with they basket of vegetable they bundle of spin yarn they piece of weave cloth whatever they have to sell or barter there be a lad with a pair of wooden shoe which he have fashion as he be tend the village cow another with a grass mat or bamboo staff or some other strange outlandish-looking article which he hope to barter in the bazaar for something on which he heart be set the _ bunniah _ hurry up they totter overladen pony the rice merchant twist he patient bullock 's tail to make it move faster the cloth merchant with he bale under he arm and measure stick in hand walk briskly along here come a gang of charcoal-burner with they load of fuel sling on pole dangle from they shoulder a _ box wallah _ with he attendant coolie stagger under the weight of a huge box of manchester goods hurry by it be a busy sight in the bazaar what a cackle what a confused clatter of voice here also the woman be the chief contributor to the din of tongue there be no irate husband here or moody master to tell they to be still spread out on the ground be heap of different grain bag of flour basket of meal pulse or barley sweetmeat occupy the attention of nearly all the buyer all hindoo indulge in sweet which take the place of beer with we instead of a ` nobbler ' they offer you a ` lollipop ' trinket bead bracelet armlet and anklet of pewter there be in great bunch fruit vegetable stick of cane skin full of oil and sugar and treacle stand with fresh ` paun ' leave and pile of coarse look mass of tobacco be largely patronise it be like a hive of bee the dust hover over the move mass the smell be various none of they ` blest odour of sweet araby ' drug condiment spice shoe in fact everything that a rustic population can require be here the _ pice _ jingle as they change hand the haggle and chaffer be without parallel in any market at home here be a man apparently in the last madness of intense passion in fierce altercation with another who try he utmost to outbluster he furious declamation in a moment they be smile and to all appearance the best friend in the world the bargain have be conclude it be all about whether the one could give three _ brinjal _ or four for one pice it be a scene of indescribable bustle noise and confusion by evening however all will have be pack up again and only the faint outline of yet float cloud of dust and the hop cheeky crow pick up the scatter litter and half-naked listless indolent figure lie about or walk slowly to and from the yard with seemingly purposeless indecision in the outer verandah be a old _ palkee _ with evidence in the tarnished gilding and fray and tattered hanging that it once have some pretension to fashionable elegance the wall of the building however be sadly crack and numerous young _ peepul _ tree grow in the crevice they insidious root creep farther and farther into the fissure and expedite the work of decay which be everywhere apparent it be the residence of the zemindar the lord of the village the owner of the land adjoining probably he be descend from some noble house of ancient lineage he forefather possibly lead armed retainer against some rival in yonder far off village where the dim outline of a mud fort yet tell of the insecurity of the day of old now he be old and fat and lazy possibly he have be too often to the money-lender he land be mortgage to they full value though they respect and look up to they old zemindar the villager be get independent they be not so humble and pay less and less of feudal tribute than in the old day when the golden palanquin be new when the elephant have splendid housing when mace and javelin and matchlock-man follow in he train alas the elephant be sell long ago and be now the property of a wealthy _ bunniah _ who have amass money in the buying and selling of grain and oil the zemindar may be a man of progress and intelligence but many be of this break down and helpless type hold the land of the village by hereditary right by grant conquest or purchase he collect he rent from the village through a small staff of _ peon _ or un-official police the account be keep by another important village functionary -- the _ putwarrie _ or village accountant _ putwarry _ belong to the writer or _ kayasth _ caste they be probably as clever and at the same time as unscrupulous as any class in indium they manage the most complicated account between ryot and landlord with great skill they memory be wonderful but they can always forget conveniently where ryot be numerous the landlord 's want press and frequent call make on the tenantry for payment often make in various kind of grain and produce the rate and price of which be constantly change it be easy to imagine the complication and intricacy of a _ putwarrie 's _ account each ryot pretty accurately remember he own particular indebtedness but woe to he if he pay the _ putwarrie _ the value of a ` red cent ' without take a receipt certainly there may be a really honest _ putwarrie _ but i very much doubt it the name stand for chicanery and robbery on the one hand the landlord be constantly stir he up for money question he account and put he not unfrequently to actual bodily coercion the ryot on the other hand be constantly invent excuse get up delay and propound innumerable reason why he can not pay he will try to forge receipt he will get up false evidence that he have already pay and the wretched _ putwarrie _ need all he native and acquire sharpness to hold he own but all ryot be not alike and when the _ putwarrie _ get hold of some unwary and ignorant bumpkin whom he can plunder he _ do _ plunder he systematically all cowherd be popularly suppose to be cattle lifter and a _ putwarrie _ after he have get over the stage of infancy and have be indoctrinate into all the knavery that he elder can teach he be suppose to belong to the highest category of villain a popular proverb much use in behar say : -- ` unda poortee cowa maro iinnum I billar : bara burri I kayashh marige humesha mara gwar ' this be translate thus : ` when the shell be break kill the crow and the wild cat at its birth ' a _ kayasth _ writer or _ putwarrie _ may be allow to live till he be twelve year old at which time he be sure to have learn rascality then kill he but kill _ gwar _ or cowherd any time for they be invariably rascal there be a deal of grim bucolic humor in this and it very nearly hit the truth the _ putwarrie _ then be a important personage he have he _ cutcherry _ or office where he and he tribe -lrb- for there be always number of he fellow caste man who help he in he book and account -rrb- squat on they mat on the ground each possess the instrument of he call in the shape of a small brass ink-pot and a oblong box contain a knife pencil and several reed for pen each have a bundle of papers and document before he this be call he _ busta _ and contain all the papers he use there they sit and have fierce squabble with the tenantry there be always some noise about a putwarrie 's cutcherry he have generally some half dozen quarrel on hand but he trust to he pen and tongue and clever brain he be essentially a man of peace hate physical contest delight in a keen argument and a encounter with a plot calculate brain another proverb say that the putwarrie have as much chance of become a soldier as a sheep have of success in attack a wolf the _ lohar _ or blacksmith be very unlike he prototype at home here be no sound anvil no dusky shop with the spark from the heated iron light up its dim recess there be little to remind one of longfellow 's beautiful poem the _ lohar _ sit in the open air he hammer and other implement of trade be very primitive like all native handicraftsman he sit down at he work he bellows be make of two loose bag of sheepskin lift alternately by the attendant coolie as they lift they get inflate with air they be then sharply force down on they own fold and the contain air eject forcibly through a iron or clay nozzle into the very small heap of glowing charcoal which form the fire he principal work be make and sharpen the uncouth-looking ploughshare which look more like flat blunt chisel than anything else they also make and keep in repair the _ hussowah _ or serrated sickle with which the crop be cut they be slow at they task but many of they be ingenious worker in metal they be very imitative and i have see many english tool and even gun-lock make by a common native village blacksmith that could not be surpass in delicacy of finish by any english smith it be foreign to we idea of the brawny blacksmith to hear that he sit to he work but this be the invariable custom even carpenter and mason squat down to theirs cheap labor be but a arbitrary term and a country smith at home might do the work of ten or twelve man in indium but it be just as well to get a idea of exist difference on many of the factory there be very intelligent _ mistree _ which be the term for the master blacksmith these man get but twenty-four to thirty shilling a month and supply themselves with food and clothing be nevertheless competent to work all the machinery attend to the engine and do all the ironwork necessary for the factory they will superintend the staff of blacksmith and if the sewing-machine of the _ mem sahib _ the gun-lock of the _ luna sahib _ the lawn-mower english pump or other machine get out of order require any metal work the _ mistree _ be call in and be generally competent to put thing to rights -lrb- illustration : carpenter and blacksmith at work -rrb- as i have say every village be a self-contained little commune all trade necessary to supply the want of the villager be represent in it besides the profit from he actual call nearly every man except the daily labourer have a little bit of land which he farm so as to eke out he scanty income all possess a cow or two a few goat and probably a pair of plough-bullock when a dispute arise in the village should a person be suspect of theft should he cattle trespass on he neighbor 's grow crop should he libel some one against whom he have a grudge or proceed to stronger measure take the law into he own hand and assault he the aggrieved party complain to the head man of the village in every village the head man be the fountain of justice he hold he office sometimes by right of superior wealth or intelligence or hereditary succession not unfrequently by the unanimous wish of he fellow-villager on a complaint be make to he he summons both party and they witness the complainant be then allow to nominate two man to act as assessor or juryman on he behalf he nomination be liable to challenge by the opposite party the defendant next name two to act on he behalf and if these be agree to by both party these four with the head man form what be call a _ punchayiet _ or council of five in fact a jury they examine the witness and each party to the suit conduct he own case the whole village not unfrequently attend to hear what go on in a mere caste or private quarrel only the friend of the party will attend every case be try in public and all the inhabitant of the village can hear the proceedings if they wish respectable inhabitant can remark on the proceedings make suggestion and give a opinion public feeling be thus pretty accurately gauge and test and the _ punchayiet _ agree among themselves on the verdict to the honor of they character for fair play be it say that the decision of a _ punchayiet _ be generally correct and be very seldom appeal against we complicated system of law with its delay its technicality its uncertainty and above all its expense its stamp duty its court fee its bribe to native underling and the innumerable vexation attendant on the administration of justice in we revenue and criminal court be repugnant to the villager of hindostan they be very litigious and believe in we desire to give they justice and protection to life and property but we court be far too costly we machinery of justice be far too intricate and complicated for a people like the hindoo ` justice within the gate ' be what they want it be quite enough admission of the reality of we rule -- that we be the paramount power -- that they submit a case to we at all and all impediment in the way of they get cheap and speedy justice should be do away with a codification of exist law a sweeping away of one half the form and technicality that at present bewilder the applicant for justice and altogether a less legal and more equitable procedure have a due regard to efficiency and the conservation of imperial interest should be the aim of we indian ruler more especially should this be the case in rural district where large interest be concern where case involve delicate point of law we present court divest of they hungry crowd of middleman and retainer be right enough but i would like to see rural court for petty case establish preside over by lead native planter merchant and man of probity which would in a measure supplement the _ punchayiet _ system which would be easy of access cheap in they procedure and with all the impress of authority it be a question i merely glance at as it do not come within the scope of a book like this but it be well know to every planter and european who have come much in contact with the rural class of hindostan that there be a vast amount of smoulder disaffection of deep-rooted dislike to and contempt of we present cumbrous costly machinery of law and justice if a villager wish to level a withering sarcasm at the head of a plausible talkative fellow all promise and no performance ready with tongue but not with purse or service he call he a _ vakeel _ that be a lawyer if he have to cool he heel in you office or round the factory to get some little business do to neglect he work to get he rent or produce account investigate wherever there be worry trouble delay or difficulty about anything concern the relation between himself and the factory the deepest and keenest expression of discontent and disgust he versatile and acute imagination can suggest or he fluent tongue give utterance to be that this be ` adanlut lea mafich ' that be ` like a court of justice ' could there be a stronger commentary on we judicial institution the world be wake up now rapidly from the lethargic sleep of age man 's mind be keenly alive to what be pass communication be much improve the dissemination of news be rapid the old race of besotted ignorant tenant and grasp avaricious domineering tyrant of landlord be fast die out and there could be no difficulty in establish in such village or district court as i have indicate all educate respectable european with a stake in the country should be make justice of the peace with limited power to try petty case there be a vast material -- loyalty educate mind a honest desire to do justice independence and a genuine scorn of everything pettifogging and underhand -- that the indian government would do well to utilise the best friend of the baboo can not acquit he of a tendency to temporise a hankering after finesse a too fatal facility to fall under pecuniary temptation the educate gentleman planter of the present day be above suspicion and before shower title and honor on native gentleman elevate they to the bench and deluge the service with they it might be worth we ruler ' while to utilise or try to utilise the experience loyalty honor and integrity of those of we countryman who might be willing to place they service at the disposal of government ` indium for the indian ' be a very good cry it sound well but it will not do to push it to its logical issue unless indian can govern indium wisely and well in accordance with modern national idea they have no more right to indium than hottentot have to the cape or the black fellow to australia in my opinion hindoo would never govern hindustan half quarter nay one tithe as well as englishman make more of you englishman in indium then make not less of you baboo if you please but make more of you englishman keep they loyal and content treat they kindly and liberally one englishman contented loyal and industrious in a indian district be a greater pillar of strength to the indian government than ten dozen baboo or zemindar let they have as many title decoration university degree or certificate of loyalty from junior civilian as they may not india for the indian but indium for imperial britain say i chapter xiv a native village continue -- the watchman or ` chowkeydar ' -- the temple -- brahmin -- idol -- religion -- humility of the poorer class -- they low condition -- they apathy -- the police -- they extortion and knavery -- a instance of police rascality -- corruption of native official -- the hindoo unfit for self-government one more important functionary we have yet to notice the watchman or _ chowkeydar _ he be generally a _ doosadh _ or other low caste man and perambulate the village at night at interval utter a loud cry or a fierce howl which be catch up and echo by all the _ chowkeydar _ of the neighbouring village it be a weird strange sound cry after cry echo far away distance beyond distance till it fade into faintness at time it be not a unmusical cry but when he howl out close to you tent wake you from you first dreamless sleep you do not feel it to be so the _ chowkeydar _ have to see that no thief enter the village by night he protect the herd and property of the villager if a theft or crime occur he must at once report it to the nearest police station if you lose you way by night you shout out for the nearest _ chowkeydar _ and he be bind to pass you on to the next village these man get a small gratuity from government but the villager also pay they a small sum which they assess accord to individual mean the _ chowkeydar _ be generally a ragged swarthy fellow with long matted hair a huge iron-bound staff and always a blue _ puggra _ the blue be he official badge sometimes he have a brass badge and carry a sword a curved blunt weapon the handle of which be so small that scarcely a englishman 's hand would be find to fit it it be more for show than use and in thousand of case it have become so fix in the scabbard that it can not be draw -lrb- illustration : hindoo village temple -rrb- in the immediate vicinity of each village and often in the village itself be a small temple sacred to vishnu or shiva it be often perch high up on some bank overlook the lake or village tank generally there be some umbrageous old tree overshadow the sacred fane and seat near recline in the shade be several oleaginous old brahmin if the weather be hot they generally wear only the _ dhote _ or loin cloth make of fine linen or cotton and hang about the leg in not ungraceful fold the brahmin can be tell by he sacred thread worn round the neck over the shoulder he skin be much fairer than the majority of he fellow villager it be not unfrequently a pale golden olive and i have see they as fair as many european they be intelligent man with acute mind but lazy and self-indulgent frequently the village brahmin be simply a sensual voluptuary this be not the time or place to descant on they religion which with many gross practice contain not a little that be pure and beautiful the common idea at home that they be miserable pagan ` bow down to stock and stone ' be like many of the accept idea about indium very much exaggerate that the mass the crude uneducated hindoo place some faith in the idol and expect in some mysterious way that it will influence they fate for good or evil be not to be deny but the more intelligent native and most of the brahmin only look on the idol as a visible sign and symbol of the divinity they want a vehicle to carry they thought upward to god and the idol be a means to assist they thought heavenward as work of art they idol be not equal to the fine picture and other symbol of the greek or the roman catholic but they serve the same purpose where the village be very poor and no pious founder have perpetuate he memory or do honor to the god by erect a temple the native content themselves with a rough mud shrine which they visit at interval and daub with red paint they deposit flower pour libation of water or milk and in other way strive to shew that a religious impulse be stir within they so far as i have observe however the vast mass of the poor toiler in indium have little or no religion material want be too press they may have some dumb vague aspiration after a higher and a holier life but the fight for necessaries for food raiment and shelter be too incessant for they to indulge much in contemplation they have a dim idea of a future life but none of they can give you anything but a very unsatisfactory idea of they religion they observe certain form and ceremony because they father do and because the brahmin tell they of real vital practical religion as we know it they have little or no knowledge ask any common labourer or one of the low caste about immortality about salvation about the higher virtue about the yearning and wish that every immortal soul at period have and he will simply tell you ` khoda jane hum greel admus ' ie ` god know i be only a poor man ' there they take refuge always when you ask they anything puzzling if you be rate they for a fault ask they to perform a complicated task or inquire you way in a strange neighborhood the first answer you get will ten to one be ` hum greel admus ' it be say almost instinctively and no doubt in many case be the refuge of simple disinclination to think the matter out pure laziness suggest it it be too much trouble to frame a answer or give the desire information and the ` greel admus ' come naturally to the lip it be often deprecatory meaning ' i be ignorant and uninformed ' you must not expect too ` much from a poor rude uncultivated man like I ' it be often also a delicate mode of flattery which be truly oriental imply and often convey in a tone a look a gesture that though the speaker be ` greel ' poor humble despise it be only by contrast to you the questioner who be mighty exalted and powerful for downright fawn obsequiousness or delicate imply fine-strung subtle flattery i will back a hindoo sycophant against the courtier or place-hunter of every other nation it be very annoying at time if you be in a hurry and particularly want a direct answer to a plain question to hear the old old story ' i be a poor man ' but there be nothing for it but patience you must ask again plainly and kindly the poorer class be easily flurry they will always give what information they have if kindly speak to but you must not fluster they you must rouse they mind to think and let they fairly grasp the purport of you inquiry for they be very suspicious often ponder over you object carefully consider all the pro and con as to you motive inclination or you position many try to give a answer that they think would be please to you if they think you be weary and tired and you ask you distance from the place you may be wish to reach they will ridiculously underestimate the length of road a man may have all the cardinal virtue but if they think you do not like he and you ask he character they will paint he to you blacker than satan himself it be very hard to get the plain unvarnished truth from a hindoo many indeed be almost incapable of give a intelligent answer to any question that do not nearly concern they own private and purely personal interest they have a sordid grub vegetate life many of they indeed be but little above the brute creation they have no idea beyond the supply of the mere animal want of the moment the future never trouble they they live they hard unlovely life and experience no pleasure and no surprise they have few regret they mind be mere blank and life be one long continued struggle with nature for bare subsistence what wonder then that they be fatalist they do not speculate on the mystery of existence they be content to be to labor to suffer to die when they time come like a dog because it be _ kismet _ -- they fate many of they never strive to avert any impending calamity such for example as sickness a man sicken he wrap himself in stolid apathy he make no effort to shake of he malady he accept it with sullen despairing pathetic resignation as he fate he friend mourn in they dumb despairing way but they too accept the situation he have no one to rouse he if you ask he what be the matter he only wail out ` hum kya kurre ' what can i do i be unwell no attempt whatever to tell you of the origin of he illness no wish even for sympathy or assistance he accept the fact of he illness he struggle not with fate it be so ordain why fight against it amen so let it be i have often be sadden to see poor toiling tenant strike down in this way even if you give they medicine they often have not energy enough to take it you must see they take it before you eye it be _ you _ struggle not theirs _ you _ must rouse they by _ you _ will _ you _ energy must compel _ they _ to make a attempt to combat they weakness once you rouse a man and infuse some spirit into he he may resist he disease but it be a hard fight to get he to try what a meaning in that one word try to act to do the average poor suffering native hindoo know nothing of it of course they mood vary they have they ` high day and holiday ' feast procession and entertainment but on the whole the average ryot or small cultivator have a hard life in every village there be generally bit of uncultivated or jungle land on which the village herd have a right of pasture the cow be a sacred animal they only use she product milk and butter the urchin may be see in the morning drive long string of emaciated look animal to the village pasture which in the evening wend they weary way backwards through the choke dust have have but ` short common ' all the day on the parched and scanty herbage the police be too often a source of annoyance and become extortionate robber instead of the protector of the poor it seem to be inherent in the oriental mind to abuse authority i do not scruple to say that all the vast army of policeman court peon writer clerk messenger and underling of all sort about the court of justice in the service of government officer or in any way attach to the retinue of a government official one and all be undeniably shamelessly venal and corrupt they accept a bribe much more quickly than a attorney a fee or a hungry dog a shin of beef if a policeman only enter a village he expect a feast from the head man and will ask a present with unblushing effrontery as a perquisite of he office if a theft be report the inspector of the nearest police-station or _ thanna _ as it be call send one of he myrmidon or if the chance of bribe be good he may attend himself on arrival amble on he broken-kneed wall-eyed pony he seat himself in the verandah of the chief man of the village who forthwith with much inward trepidation make he appearance the policeman assume the air of a haughty conqueror receive homage from a conquer foe he assure the tremble wretch that ` act on information receive ' he must search he dwelling for the miss goods and that he woman 's apartment will have to be ransack and so annoy goad and insult the unfortunate man that he be too glad to purchase immunity from further insolence by make the policeman a small present perhaps a ` kid of the goat ' or something else the guardian of the peace be then regale with the best food in the house after which he be ` wreathe with smile ' if he see a chance of a farther bribe he take he departure say he will make he report to the _ thanna _ he repeat he procedure with some of the other respectable inhabitant and go back a good deal richer than he come to share the spoil with the _ thannadar _ or inspector another man may then be send and the same course be follow until all the force in the station have have they share the ryot be afraid to resist the police have tremendous power for annoying and do he harm a crowd of subservient scoundrel always hang round the station dependent relation or accomplice these harry the poor man who be unwise enough to resist the extortionate demand of the police they take he cattle to the pound foment strife between he and he neighbor get up frivolous and false charge against he harass he in a thousand way and if all else fail get he summon as a witness in some case you might think a witness a person to be treat with respect to be attend to to have every facility offer he for give he evidence at the least cost of time and trouble possible consistent with the demand of justice and the vindication of law and authority not so in indium with the witness in a police case when the force dislike he if he have not previously satisfy they leech-like rapacity he be torment tortured bully and kick ` from pillar to post ' till he life become a burden to he he have to leave all he avocation perhaps at the time when he affair require he constant supervision he have to trudge many a weary mile to attend the court the police get hold of he and keep he often in real durance he get no opportunity for cooking or eat he food he daily habit be upset and interfere with in every little vexatious way -lrb- and they be master of the art of petty torture -rrb- they so worry and goad he that the very threat of be summon as a witness in a police case be often enough to make the horrify well-to-do native give a handsome gratuity to be allow to sit quietly at home this be no exaggeration it be the every day practice of the police they exercise a real despotism they have set up a reign of terror the nature of the ryot be such that he will submit to a great deal to avoid have to leave he home and he work the police take full advantage of this feeling and be perfectly unscrupulous insatiably rapacious and league together in villany they make a golden harvest out of every case put into they hand they have make the name of justice stink in the nostril of the respectable and well-to-do middle class of indium the district superintendent be man of energy and probity but after all they be only mortal what with account inspection report form and innumerable writings they can not exercise a constant vigilance and personal supervision over every part of they district a district may comprise many hundred village thousand of inhabitant and league of intricate and densely peopled country the mere physical exertion of ride over he district would be too much for any man in about a week the subordinate police be all interested in keep up the present system of extortion and the inspector and sub-inspector who wink at malpractice come in for they share of the spoil there be little combination among the peasantry each selfishly try to save he own skin and they know that if any one individual be to complain or to dare to resist he would have to bear the brunt of the battle alone none of he neighbor would stir a finger to back he he be too timid and too much in awe of the official european and constitutionally too averse to resistance to do aught but suffer in silence no doubt he feel he wrong most keenly and a sullen feeling of hate and wrong be be garner up which may produce result disastrous for the peace and wellbeing of we empire in the east as a case in point i may mention one instance out of many which come under my own observation i have a _ moonshee _ or accountant in one of my outwork in purneah formerly when the police have come through the factory he have be in the habit of give they a present and some food under my strict order however that no policeman be to be allow near the place unless they come on business he have discontinue pay he black mail this be too glare a infringement of what they consider they vested rights to be pass over in silence example might spread my man must be make a example of i have a case in the court of the deputy magistrate some twenty mile or so from the factory the moonshee have be name as a witness to prove the writing of some papers file in the suit they get a citation for he to appear a mere summons for he attendance as a witness arm with this they appear at the factory two or three day before the date fix on for hear the cause i have just ridden in from purneah tired hot and dusty and be sit in the shade of the verandah with young d my assistant one policeman first come up present the summons which i take and he then state that it be a _ warrant _ for the production of my moonshee and that he must take he away at once i tell the man it be merely a summons require the attendance of the moonshee on a certain date to give evidence in the case he be very insolent in he manner it be customary when a hindoo of inferior rank appear before you that he remove he shoe and stand before you in a respectful attitude this man 's headdress be all disarrange which in itself be a sign of disrespect he speak loudly and insolently keep he shoe on and sit down squat on the grass before I my assistant be very indignant and want to speak to the man but rightly judge that the object be to enrage I and trap I into commit some overt act that would be afterwards construe against I i keep my temper speak very firmly but temperately tell he my moonshee be do some work of great importance that i could not spare he service then but that i would myself see that the summons be attend to the policeman become more boisterous and insolent i offer to give he a letter to the magistrate acknowledge the receipt of the summons and i ask he he own name which he refuse to give i ask he if he could read and he say he could not i then ask he if he could not read how could he know what be in the paper which he have bring and how he know my moonshee be the party mean he say a chowkeydar have tell he so i ask where be the chowkeydar and see from my coolness and determination that the game be up he shout out and from round the corner of the hut come another policeman and two village chowkeydar from a distance they have evidently be hide observe all that pass and mean to act as witness against I if i have be lead by the first scoundrel 's behavior to lose my temper the second man be not such a brute as the first and when i proceed to ask they name and all about they and tell they i mean to report they to they superintendent they become somewhat frightened and try to make excuse i tell they to be off the premise at once offer to take the summons and give a receipt for it but they now see that they have make a mistake in try to bully I and make off at once mark the sequel the day before the case be fix on for hearing i send off the moonshee who be a witness of my own and he evidence be necessary to my prove my case i supply he with travel expense and he start on he way to the court he have to pass the _ thanna _ or police-station the police be on the watch he be seize as he pass he be confine all that night and all the follow day for want of he evidence i lose my case and have thus achieve one part of they object to pay I off they let my moonshee go after insult and abuse and with threat of future vengeance should he ever dare to thwart or oppose they this be pretty ` hot ' you think but it be not all fear my complaint to the superintendent or to the authority might get they into trouble they lay a false charge against I that i have obstruct they in the discharge of they duty that i have shower abuse on they use threatening language and insult the majesty of the law by tear up and spit upon the respect summons of she majesty on this complaint i be accordingly summon into purneah the charge be a tissue of the most barefaced lie but i have to ride fifty-four mile in the burning sun ford several river and undergo much fatigue and discomfort my work be of course seriously interfere with i have to take in my assistant as witness and one or two of the servant who have be present i be put to immense trouble and no little expense to say nothing of the indignation which i naturally feel and all because i have set my face against a well know evil and be determine not to submit to impudent extortion of course the case break down they contradict themselves in almost every particular the second constable indeed admit that i have offer they a letter to the magistrate and have not move out of the verandah during the colloquy i be honourably acquit and have the satisfaction of see the lie rascal put into the dock by the indignant magistrate and prosecute summarily for get up a false charge and give false evidence it be a lesson to the police in those part and they do not dare to trouble I much afterwards but it be only one instance out of hundred i could give and which every planter have witness of the barefaced audacity the shameless extortion the unblushing lawlessness of the rural police of indium it be a gigantic evil but surely not irremediable by add more european officer to the force by educate the people and make they more intelligent independent and self-reliant much may be do to abate the evil but at present it be admittedly a foul ulcer on the administration of justice under we rule the menial who serve a summons get a decree of court to execute or be entrust with any order of a official nature expect to be bribe to do he duty if he do not get he fee he will throw such impediment in the way raise such obstacle and fashion such delay that he completely foil every effort to procure justice through a legal channel no wonder a native hate we english court we english official let it be plainly understand be above suspicion it need not my poor testimony to uphold they character for high honor loyal integrity and zealous eagerness to do ` justly and to walk uprightly ' they be unwearied in they effort to get at truth and govern wisely but we system of law be totally unsuited for oriental it be make a medium for chicanery and trickery of the most atrocious form most of the native underling be utterly venal and corrupt increase pay do not mean decrease of knavery cheating and lie and take bribe and abuse of authority be ingrain into they very soul and all the cut and dry formula of namby pamby philanthropist the inane maundering of stay-at-home sentimentalist the wise saw of self-opinionated theorist who know nothing of the hindoo as he really shew himself to we in daily and hourly contact with he will ever persuade I that native as oppose to english rule would be productive of aught but burn oppression and shameless venality or would end in anything but anarchy and chaos it sound very well in print and increase the circulation of a paper or two among the baboo to cry out that we task be to elevate the oppressed and ignorant million of the east to educate they into self-government to make they judge officer lawgiver governor over all the land to vacate we place and power and let the baboo and the bunneah to whom we have give the glory of western civilization rule in we place and guide the fortune of these toiling million who owe protection and peace to we foster rule it be a noble sentiment to resign wealth honor glory and power to give up a settled government to alter a policy that have weld the conflicting element of hindustan into one stable whole to throw up we title of conqueror and disintegrate a mighty empire for what a sprinkling of thinly-veneered half-educated native want a share of the loaf and fish in political scramble and a few inane people of the ` man and brother ' type cry out at home to let they have they way no give the hindoo education equal law protection to life and property develop the resource of the country foster all the virtue you can find in the native mind but till you can give he the energy the integrity the singleness of purpose the manly honorable straightforwardness of the anglo-saxon he scorn of meanness trickery and fraud he loyal single-heartedness to do right he contempt for oppression of the weak he self-dependence he probity but why go on when you make hindoo honest truthful god-fearing englishman you can let they govern themselves but as soon ` may the leopard change he spot ' as the hindoo he character he be wholly unfit for self-government utterly oppose to honest truthful stable government at all time bring strange change but the wisdom which have govern the country hitherto will surely be able to meet the new demand that may be make upon it in the immediate present or in the far distant future chapter xv jungle wild fruit -- curious method of catch quail brahmin play the wait game turn the table _ and _ the blacksmith -- remark on wrestle a peculiarity in the sombre sal jungle be the scarcity of wild fruit at home the wood be fill with berry and fruit-bearing bush who among my reader have not a lively recollection of bramble hunting nutting or merry expedition for blueberry wild strawberry raspberry and other wild fruit you might walk many a mile through the sal jungle without meeting fruit of any kind save the dry and tasteless wild fig or the sickly mhowa there be indeed very few jungle fruit that i have ever come across there be one acid sort of plum call the _ omra _ which make a good preserve but be not very nice to eat raw the _ gorkah _ be a small red berry very sweet and pleasant slightly acid not unlike a red currant in fact and with two small pip or stone the nepaulese call it _ bunchooree _ it grow on a small stunted-looking bush with few branch and a pointed leaf in form resemble the acacia leaf but not so large the _ glaphur _ be a brown round fruit the skin rather crisp and hard and of a dull earthy color not unlike that of a common boil potato the inside be a stringy spongy-looking mass with small seed embed in a gummy viscid substance the taste be exactly like a almond and it form a pleasant mouthful if one be thirsty travel one day along one of the glade i have mention as divide the strip of jungle i be surprise to see a man before I in a field of long stubble with a cloth spread over he head and two stick project in front at a obtuse angle to he body form horn-like projection on which the end of he cloth twist spirally be tie i think from he curious antic and movement that he must be mad but i soon discover that there be method in he madness he be catch quail the quail be often very numerous in the stubble field and the native adopt very ingenious device for they capture this be one i be now witness cover themselves with they cloth as i have describe the project end of the two stick represent the horn they simulate all the movement of a cow or bull they pretend to paw up the earth toss they make-believe horn turn round and pretend to scratch themselves and in fact identify themselves with the animal they be represent and it be irresistibly comic to watch a solitary performer go through this _ al fresco _ comedy i have laugh often at some cunning old herdsman or shekarry when they see you watch they they will redouble they effort and try to represent a old bull go through all he prank and practice and throw you into convulsion of laughter round two side of the field they have previously put fine net and at the apex they have a large cage with a decoy quail inside or perhaps a pair the quail be a run bird disincline for flight except at night in the day-time they prefer run to use they wing the idiotic look old cow as we will call the hunter have all he wit about he he proceed very slowly and warily he keen eye detect the covey of quail which way they be run he ruse generally succeed wonderfully he be no more like a cow than that respectable animal be like a cucumber but he paw and toss and move about pretend to eat to nibble here and switch he tail there and so maneuver as to keep the run quail away from the unprotected edge of the field when they get to the verge protect by the net they begin to take alarm they be probably not very certain about the peculiar look ` old cow ' behind they and run along the net they see the decoy quail evidently feed in great security and freedom the v shape mouth of the large basket cage look invitingly open the puzzling net be bar the way and the ` old cow ' be gradually close up behind as the hunter move along i should have tell you he rub two piece of dry hard stick gently up and down he thigh with one hand produce a peculiar crepitation a crackling sound not sufficient to startle the bird into flight but alarming they enough to make they get out of the way of the ` old cow ' one bolder than the other possibly the most timid of the covey irritated by the queer crackle sound now enter the basket the other follow like a flock of sheep and once in the puzzling shape of the entrance prevent they exit not unfrequently the hunter bag twenty or even thirty brace of quail in one field by this ridiculous look but ingenious method the small quail net be also sometimes use for the capture of hare the native stretch the net in the jungle much as they do the large net for deer describe in a former chapter form a line they then beat up the hare of which there be no stint my friend pat once make a novel haul he _ lobarkhanna _ or blacksmith 's shop be close to a patch of jungle and pat often notice number of quail run through the loose chink and crevice of the wall in the morning when anyone go into the place for the first time this be at a factory call rajpore pat come to the conclusion that as the blacksmith 's fire smoulder some time after work be discontinue at account of the warmth and shelter one night therefore he get some of he servant and with great caution and as much silence as possible they let down a quantity of net all round the lobarkhanna and in the morning they capture about twenty quail the quail be very pugnacious and as they be easily train to fight they be very common pet with the native who train and keep they to pit they against each other and bet what they can afford on the result a quail fight a battle between two trained ram a cock fight even a encounter between trained tame buffalo be very common spectacles in the village but the most popular sport be a good wrestling match the dweller in the presidency town and indeed in most of the large station seldom see a exhibition of this kind but away in the remote interior near the frontier it be very popular pastime and wrestling be a favorite with all class such manly sport be rather oppose to the commonly receive idea at home of the mild hindoo in nearly every village of behar however and all along the border of nepaul there be as a rule a bit of land attach to the residence of some head man or the common property of the commune set apart for the practice of athletic sport chief of which be the favorite _ khoosthee _ or wrestle there be generally some wary old veteran who have win he spur or laurel or belt or whatever you choose to call it in many a hard fight and well contest tussle for the championship of he little world he be ` up to every dodge ' and know every feint and guard every wile and tactic of the wrestling ground it be generally in some shady grove secluded and cool here of a evening when the labor of the day be over the most stalwart son of the hamlet meet to test each other skill and endurance in a friendly _ shake _ the old man put they through the preliminary practice show they every trick at he command and attend strictly to they training and various trial the ground be dig knee deep and form a soft good holding stand i have often look on at this evening practice and it would astonish a stranger who can not understand strength endurance and activity be attribute to a ` mere nigger ' to see the severe training these young lad impose upon themselves they leap into the air and suddenly assume a sit position then leap up again and squat down with a force that would seem to jerk every bone in they body out of its place this get up the muscle of the thigh some lie down at full length only touch the ground with the extreme tip of they toe they arm double up under they and sustain the full weight of the body on the extended palm of the hand they then sway themselves backwards and forwards to they full length never shift hand or toe till they be bath in perspiration they keep up a uniform steady backward and forward movement so as to develop the muscle of the arm chest and back they practice leap run and lift weight some standing at they full height brace up the muscle of the shoulder and upper arm and then leap up allow themselves to fall to earth on the tensely string muscle of the shoulder this severe exercise get the muscle into perfect form and few very few indeed of we untrained youth could cope in a dead lock or fierce struggle with a good village hindoo or mussulman in active training and have any knowledge of the trick of the wrestling school no hit be allow the hindoo system of wrestling be the perfection of science and skill mere dead weight of course will always tell in a close grip but the catch the hold the twist and dodge that be practiced allow for the fullest development of cultivate skill as against mere brute force the system be purely a scientific one the fundamental rule be ` catch where you can ' only you must not clutch the hair or strike with the fist the loin be tightly gird with a long waist-belt or _ kummerbund _ of cloth which pass repeatedly between the limb and round the loin sufficiently brace up and protect that part of the body in some match you be not allow to clutch this waist cloth or belt in some village it be allow the custom vary in various place but what be a fair grip and what be not be always make know before the competitor engage a twist or grip or dodge be know as a _ paench _ this literally mean a screw or twist but in wrestle phraseology mean any grip by which you can get such a advantage over you opponent as to defeat he for every paench there be a counter paench a throw be consider satisfactory when both shoulder of you opponent touch the ground simultaneously the old _ khalifa _ or trainer take a great interest in the progress of he _ chaila _ or pupil _ chailum _ really mean disciple or follower every khalifa have he favorite paench or grip which have stand he in good stead in he old battle day he teach these paench to he pupil so that when you get young fellow from different village to meet you see a really fine exhibition of wrestle skill there be little tripping as amongst we wrestler at home a dead-lock be uncommon the rival wrestler generally bind into the ring slapping they thigh and arm with a loud resounding slap they lift they leg high up from the ground with every step and scheme and maneuver sometimes for a long while to get the best corner they try to get the sun into they adversary eye they scan the appearance and every movement of they opponent the old wary fellow take it very coolly and if they can not get the desire side of the ground they keep hop about like a solemn old ostrich till the impetuosity or impatience of they foe lead he to attack they remind you for all the world of a pair of game cock they body be bent they head almost touch there be a deal of light play with the hand each try to get the other by the wrist or elbow or at the back of the head round the neck if one get the other by a finger even it be a great advantage as he would whip nimbly round and threaten to break the impound finger this would be consider quite fair one will often suddenly drop on he knee and try to reach the ankle of he adversary i have see a slippery customer stoop suddenly down grasp up a handful of dust and throw it into the eye of he opponent it be do with the quickness of thought but it be detect and on a appeal by the sufferer the knave be well thrash by the onlooker there be many professional who follow no other call wrestler be keep by raja and wealthy man who get up match frequently one village will challenge another like we village cricket club the villager often get up small subscription and purchase a silver armlet or bracelet the prize he who shall hold he own against all comer the ` champion 's belt ' scarcely call forth greater competition keener rivalry or better sport it be at once the most manly and most scientific sport in which the native indulge a disputed fall sometimes terminate in a general free fight when the backer of the respective man lay on the stick to each other with mutual hate and hearty lustiness it be not by any mean always the strongest who win the man who know the most paench who be agile active cool and careful will not unfrequently overthrow a antagonist twice he weight and strength all the wrestler in the country-side know each other 's qualification pretty accurately and at a general match get up by a zemindar or planter or by public subscription it be generally safe to let they handicap the man who be ready to compete for the prize we use generally to put down a few of the oldest professor and let they pit couple against each other the sport to the onlooker be most exciting between the man themselves as a rule the utmost good humor reign they strive hard to win but they accept a defeat with smile resignation it be only between rival village champion different caste man or worse still man of differ religion such as a hindoo and a mahommedan that there be any danger of a fight a disturbance be a rare exception but i have see a few wrestling match end in a regular general scrimmage with broken head and even fractured limb with good management however and a efficient body of man to guard against a breach of the peace this need never occur it rarely take much trouble to get up a match if you tell you head man that you would like to see one say on a Saturday afternoon they pass the word to the different village and at the appoint time all the finest young fellow and most of the male population lead by they head man with the old trainer in attendance be at the appoint place the competitor be admit within the enclosure and round it the row of spectator pack twenty deep although they be nigger and hodge and gile at home would not have a chance with they in a fair wrestling bout conduct accord to they own law and custom the entry be now all make place and pair be
