tribute -- old custom -- improvisatore and bard -- nautch -- dancing and music -- the dance of the dangurs -- juggler and itinerary showman -- ` bara roopes ' or actor and mimic -- they different style of act besides indigo plant proper there be another large branch of industry in north bhaugulpore and along the nepaul frontier there and in purneah which be the grow of indigo seed for the bengal planter the system of advance and the mode of cultivation be much the same as that follow in indigo plant proper the seed be sow in June or July be weed and tend all through the rain and cut in December the planter advance about four rupee a beegah to the ryot who cut he seed-plant and bring it into the factory threshing ground where it be beat out clean weigh and pack in bag when the seed have be thresh out and clean it be weigh and the ryot or cultivator get four rupee for every maund -- a maund be eighty pound avoirdupoi the previous advance be deduct the rent or loan account be adjust and the balance make over in cash other grow the seed on they own account without take advance and bring it to the factory for sale if price be rule high they may get much more than four rupee per maund for it and they adopt all kind of ingenious device to adulterate the seed and increase its weight they mix dust with it seed of weed even grain of wheat and mustard pea and other seed in buy seed therefore one have to be very careful to reject all that look bad or that may have be adulterate they will even get old useless seed the refuse stock of former year and mix this with leaf of the neem tree and some turmeric powder give it a gloss that make it look like fresh seed when you suspect that the seed have be tamper with in this manner you wet some of it and rub it on a piece of fresh clean linen so as to bring off the dye where the attempt have be flagrant you be sometimes tempt to take the law into you own hand and administer a little of the castigation which the cheating rascal so richly deserve in other case it be necessary to submit the seed to a microscopic examination if any old worn seed be detect you reject the sample unhesitatingly even when the seed appear quite good you subject it to yet another test take one or two hundred seed and put they on a damp piece of the pith of a plantain tree mix with a little earth set they in a warm place and in two day you will be able to tell what percentage have germinate and what be incapable of germination if the percentage be good the seed may be consider as fairly up to the sample and it be purchase there be native seed buyer who try to get as much into they hand as they can and rig the market there be also european buyer and there be a keen rivalry in all the bazaar the threshing-floor and seed-cleaning ground present a busy sight when several thousand maund of seed be be get ready for despatch by boat the dirty seed full of dust and other impurity be heap up in one corner the floor be in the shape of a large square nicely pave with cement as hard and clean as marble crowd of nearly nude coolie hurry to and fro with scoop of seed rest on they shoulder when they get in line at right angle to the direction in which the wind be blow they move slowly along let the seed descend on the heap below while the wind winnow it and carry the dust in dense cloud to leeward this be repeat over and over again till the seed be as clean as it can be make it be put through bamboo sieve so form that any seed larger than indigo can not pass through what remains in the sieve be put aside and afterwards clean sort and sell as food or if useless throw away or give to the fowl the man and boy dart backwards and forwards there be a steady drip drip of seed from the scoop dense cloud of dust and incessant noise and bustle peon or watchman be station all around to see that none be waste or steal some be fill sack full of the clean seed and haul they off to the weighman and he clerk two maund be put in every sack and when weigh the bag be haul up close to the _ godown _ or store-room here be a army of man with sailmaker 's needle and twine they sew up the bag which be then haul away to be mark with the factory brand cart be come and go carry bag to the boat which be lie at the river bank take in they cargo and the return cart bring back load of wood from the bank of the river in one corner under a shed sit the sahib chaffer with a party of _ paikar _ -lrb- seed merchant -rrb- who have bring seed for sale of course he decry the seed say it be bad will not hear of the price want and laugh to scorn all the fervent protestation that the seed be grow on they own ground and have never pass through any hand but they own if you be satisfied that the seed be good you secretly name you price to you head man who forthwith take up the work of depreciation you move off to some other department of the work the head man and the merchant sit down perhaps smoke a _ hookah _ each try to outwit the other but after a keen encounter of wit perhaps a bargain be make a pretty fair price be arrive at and away go the purchase seed to swell the heap at the other end of the yard it have to be carefully weigh first and the weighman get a little from the vendor as he perquisite which the factory take from he at the market rate you have buyer of you own out in the _ dehaat _ -lrb- district -rrb- and the parcel they have buy come in hour by hour with invoice detail all particulars of quantity quality and price the load from the seed depot and outwork come roll up in the afternoon and have all to be weigh check note down and examine every man 's hand be against you you can not trust you own servant for a paltry bribe they will try to pass a bad parcel of seed and even when you have you european assistant to help you it be hard work to avoid be over-reach in some shape or other you have to keep up a large staff of writer who make out invoice and account and keep the book you correspondence alone be enough work for one man and you have to tally bag count coolie see they pay they daily wage attend to lawsuit that may be go on and yet find time to superintend the operation of the farm and keep a eye to you rent and revenue from the village it be a busy a anxious time you have a vast responsibility on you shoulder and when one take into consideration the climate you have to contend with the home comfort and domestic joy you have to do without the constant tension of mind and irritation of body from dust heat insect lie bribery robber and villany of every description that meet you on all hand it must be allow that a planter at such a time have no easy life the time at which you despatch the seed be also the very time when you be prepare you land for spring sowing this require almost as much surveillance as the seed-buying and despatch you have not a moment you can call you own if you have subordinate you could trust who would be faithful and honest you could safely leave part of the work to they but from very sad experience i have find that trust to a native be trust to a very rotten stick they be certainly not all bad but there be just enough exception to prove the rule one peculiar custom prevail in this border district of north bhaugulpore which i have not observe elsewhere at the beginning of the financial year when the account of the past season have all be make up and arrange and the collection of the rent for the new year be begin the planter and zemindar hold what be call the _ pooneah _ it be customary for all cultivator and tenant to pay a proportion of they rent in advance the pooneah might therefore be call ` rent-day ' a similar day be set apart for the same purpose in tirhoot call _ tousee _ or collection but it be not attend by the same ceremonious observance and quaint custom as attach to the pooneah on the border land when every man 's account have be make up and check by the book the pooneah day be fix on invitation be send to all you neighbouring friend who look forward to each other 's annual pooneah as a great gala day in north bhaugulpore and purneah nearly all the planter and english-speaking population belong to old family who have be bear in the district and have settle and live there long before the day of quick communication with home they rule among they dependant be patriarchal everyone be know among the native who have see he since he birth live amongst they by some pet name the old man of the village remember he father and he father 's father the younger villager have have he point out to they on they visit to the factory as ` willie baba ' ` freddy baba ' or whatever he boyish name may have be with the addition of ` baba ' which be simply a pet name for a child these planter know every village for mile and mile they know most of the lead man in each village by name the villager know all about they discuss they affair with the utmost freedom and not a single thing ever so trivial happen in the planter 's home but it be know and comment on in all the village that lie within the _ ilaka _ -lrb- jurisdiction -rrb- of the factory the hospitality of these planter be unbounded they be most of they much like by all the native round i come a ` stranger amongst they ' and in one sense and not a flattering sense they try ` to take I in ' but only in one or two instance which i shall not specify here by nearly all i be welcome and kindly treat and i form some very lasting friendship among they old tradition of princely hospitality still linger among they they be clannish in the best sense of the word the kindness and attention give to aged or indigent relation be one of they best trait i be afraid the race be fast die out lavish expenditure and a too confide faith in they native dependant have often bring the usual result but many of my reader will associate with the name of purneah or bhaugulpore planter recollection of hospitality and unostentatious kindness and memory of glorious sport and warm-hearted friendship on the pooneah day then or the night before many of these friend would meet the day have long be know to all the village round and nothing could better shew the patriarchal semi-feudal style in which they rule over they village than the custom in connection with this anniversary some day before it requisition have be make on all the village in any way connect with the factory for various article of diet the herdsman have to send a tribute of milk curd and _ ghee _ or clarify butter cultivator of root crop or fruit send in sample of they produce in the shape of huge bundle of plantain immense jack-fruit or basket of sweet potato yam and other vegetable the _ koomhar _ or potter have to send in earthen pot and jar the _ mochee _ or worker in leather bring with he a sample of he work in the shape of a pair of shoe these be pounce on by you servant and _ omlah _ the omlah be the head man in the office it be a fine time for they wooden shoe umbrella brass pot fowl goat fruit in fact all the production of you country side be send or bring in it be the old feudal tribute of the middle age back again during the day the _ cutcherry _ or office be crowd with the more respectable villager pay in rent and settle account the noise and bustle be great but a immense quantity of work be get through the village putwarry and head man be all there with they voluminous account you rent-collector call a _ tehseeldar _ have be busy in the village with the tenant and putwarry collect rent for the great pooneah day there be a constant chink of money a busy hum a scratch of innumerable pen under every tree ` neath the shade of every hut busy group be squat round some acute accountant total be be tot up on all hand from greasy recess in the waistband a dirty bundle be slowly pull forth and the desire sum reluctantly count out from early morn till dewy eve this work go on and you judge you pooneah to have be a good or there be a constant go to and fro a hurry and bustling in the crowd a hum as of a distant fair pervade the place and by evening the total of the day 's collection be add up and while the sahib and he friend take they sherry and bitters the omlah and servant retire to wash and feast and prepare for the night 's festivity during the day at the house of the omlah culinary preparation on a vast scale have be go on the large supplies of grain rice flour fruit vegetable & c which be bring in as _ salamee _ or tribute supplement by addition from the sahib 's own store have be make into savoury mess curry and cake boil flesh and roast kid be all ready and the crowd have divest themselves of they head-dress and outer garment and clean they hand and foot by copious ablutions sit down in a wide circle the large leaf of the water-lily be now serve out to each man and perform the office of plate huge basket of _ chupatty _ a flat sort of ` griddle-cake ' be now bring round and each man get four or five dole out the cooking and attendance be all do by brahmin no inferior caste would answer as rajpoot and other high caste will only eat food that have be cook by a brahmin or one of they own class the brahmin attendant now come round with great _ dekchee _ or cooking-pots full of curried vegetable boil rice and similar dish a ladle-full be hand out to each man who receive it on he leaf the rice be serve out by the hand of the attendant the guest manipulate a huge ball of rice and curry mixed between the finger of the right hand pass this solemnly into they widely-gaping mouth with the head throw back to receive the mess like a adjutant-bird swallow a frog and then they masticate with much apparent enjoyment sugar treacle curd milk oil butter preserve and chutnee be serve out to the more wealthy and respectable the amount they can consume be wonderful see the enormous supplies you would think that even this great crowd could never get through they but by the time repletion have set in there be little or nothing leave and many of the inflated and distended old farmer could begin again and repeat ` another of the same ' with ease each person have he own _ lotah _ a brass drinking vessel and when all have eat they again wash they hand rinse out they mouth and don they gayest apparel the gentleman in the bungalow now get word that the evening 's festivity be about to commence light we cigar we sally out to the _ shamiana _ which have be erect on the ridge surround the deep tank which supply the factory during the manufacturing season with water the _ shamiana _ be a large canopy or wall-less tent it be festoon with flower and green plantain tree and evergreen have be plant all round it flare flambeau torch chinese lantern and oil lamp flicker and glare and make the interior almost as bright as day when we arrive we find we chair draw up in state one raise seat in the center be the place of honor and reserve for the manager of the factory when we be seat the _ malee _ or gardener advance with a wooden tray fill with sand in which be stick head of all the finest flower the garden can afford place in the most symmetrical pattern and really a pretty tasteful piece of workmanship two or three old brahmin principal among whom be ` hureehar jha ' a wicked old scoundrel now advance bearing gay garland of flower mutter a strange gibberish in omlah head by the dignified portly _ gornasta _ or confidential adviser dress in snowy turban and spotless white now come forward a large brass tray stand on the table in front of you they each halfpenny of we money and which recoup the putwarree for he outlay this have nothing to do with the legitimate revenue of the factory it never appear in the book it be quite a voluntary offering and i have never see it in any other district in the meantime the _ raj-bhat _ a wander class of hereditary minstrel or bard be sing you praise and those of you ancestor in ear-splitting strain some of they have really good voice all possess the gift of improvisation and be quick to seize on the salient point of the scene before they and weave they into they song sometimes in a very ingenious and humorous manner they be often employ by rich native to while away a long night with one of they treasured rhythmical tale or song one or two be keep in the retinue of every rajah or noble and they possess a mine of legendary information which would be invaluable to the collector of folk-lore and antiquarian literature at some of the pooneah the evening 's gaiety wind up with a _ nautch _ or dance by dance girl or boy i always think this a most sleep-inspiring exhibition it have be so often describe that i need not trouble my reader with it the woman be gaily dress in brocade and gauzy texture and glitter with spangle and tawdry ornament the musical accompaniment of clang zither asthmatic fiddle timber-toned drum clang cymbal and harsh metallic triangle be a sore affliction and when the dusky prima donna throw back she head extend she chest get up to she high note with she hand behind she ear and she poura-stained mouth and tooth wide expand like the jaw of a fangless wolf and the demoniac instrument and performer redouble they din the noise be something too dreadful to experience often the native woman sit mute and hushed seem to like it i have hear it say that the german eat ant finlander relish penny candle the nepaulese gourmandise on putrid fish i be fond of mouldy cheese and organ-grinder be a object of affection with some of we home community i _ know _ that the there be a very sudorific odour from the crowd all be comfortably seat on the ground the torch flare and send up volume of smoke to the ornamented roof of the canopy the light be reflect in the deep glassy bosom of the silent tank the combined sound and odour get oppressive and we be glad to get back to the bungalow to consume we ` peg ' and we ` weed ' in the congenial company of we friend in some factory the night close with a grand dance by all the inhabitant of the _ dangur tolum _ the man and woman range themselves in two semicircle stand opposite each other the tallest of both line at the one end diminish away at the other extremity to the child and little one who can scarcely toddle they have a wild plaintive song with swell cadence and abrupt stop they go through a extraordinary variety of evolution stamp with one foot and keep perfect time they sway they body revolve march and countermarch the man sometimes open they rank and the woman go through and _ vice versa _ they turn round like the wind convolution of a shell increase they pace as the song wax quick and shrill get excite and finish off with a resounding stamp of the foot and a guttural cry which seem to exhaust all the breath leave in they body the man then get some liquor and the woman a small money present if the sahib be very liberal he give they a pig on which to feast and the _ dangur _ go away very happy and contented they dance be not unlike the _ corroborry _ of the australian aborigine the two race be not unlike each other too in feature although i can not think that they be in any way connect next morning there be a jackal hunt or cricket or pony race or shooting match or sport of some kind while the rent collection still go on in the afternoon we have grand wrestling match amongst the native for small prize and generally witness some fine exhibition of athletic skill and endurance some wander juggler may have be attract by the rumor of the gather a tight-rope dancer a snake charmer a itinerant showman with a perform goat monkey or dancing bear may make he appearance before the admire crowd at time a party of mime or actor come round and a rare treat be not seldom afford by the _ bara roopee _ _ bara _ mean twelve and _ roop _ be a impersonation a character these ` twelve character ' make up in all sort of disguise they wardrobe be very limited yet the number of people they personate and they genuine act talent would astonish you with a project tooth and a few streak of clay they make up a withered tremble old hag afflict with palsy rheumatism and a hacking cough they make friend with you bearer and a old hat and coat transform they into a planter a missionary or a officer they whiten they face use false hair and moustache and while you be chat with you neighbor a strange sahib suddenly and mysteriously seat himself by you side you stare and look at you host who be generally in the secret but a stranger or new comer be often completely take in it be generally at night that they go through they personation and when they have dress for they part they generally choose a moment when you attention be attract by a cunning diversion on look up you be mimic the bengalee baboo or the merchant from cabool or afghanistan with he fruit and cloth a favorite _ roop _ with they be to paint one half of the face like a man everything be complete down to moustache the fold of the puggree the _ lathee _ or staff indeed to the slightest detail you would fancy you see a stalwart strapping hindoo before you he turn round and lo a bashful maiden she eye be stain with _ henna _ -lrb- myrtle juice -rrb- or antimony she long-hair neatly smooth down be tie into a knot at the back and glisten with the pearl-like ornament the taper arm be load with armlet and bracelet the very toe be bedeck with ring the bodice hide the taper waist and bud bosom the tiny ear be load with jewelled ear-ring the very nose be not forget but be ornament with a golden circle bearing on its circumference a pearl of great price the art the posture the mimicry be really admirable a good _ bara roopee _ be well worth see and amply earn the two or three rupee he get as he reward the pooneah seldom last more than the two day but it be quite unique in its feudal character and be one of the old-fashioned observance a relic of the time when the planter be really look upon as the father of he people and when a little sentiment and mutual affection mingle with the purely business relation of landlord and tenant i delighted my ryot by import some of we own country recreation and set the ploughman to compete against each other i stick a greasy bamboo firmly into the earth put a bag of copper coin at the top many try to climb it but when they come to the grease they come down ` by the run ' one fellow however fill he _ kummerbund _ with sand and after much exertion manage to secure the prize wheeling the barrow blindfold also give much amusement and we make some boy bend they forehead down to a stick and run round till they be giddy they ludicrous effort then to jump over some water-pot and run to a thorny bush raise tumultuous peal of laughter the poor boy generally smash the pot and end by tumble into the thorn chapter xvii the koosee jungle -- ferry -- jungle road -- the rhinoceros -- we go to visit a neighbor -- we lose we way and get belated -- elevate the people -- planter and factory chief of these -- character of the planter -- he claim to consideration from government in the vast grass jungle that border the bank of the koosee stretch in great plain without a undulation for mile on either side intersect by innumerable water-bed and dry up channel there be plenty of game of all sort it be a impetuous swiftly-flowing stream dash directly down from the mighty hill of nepaul so swift be its current and so erratic its course that it frequently burst its bank and career through the jungle form a new bed and carry away cattle and wild animal in its headlong rush the _ ghaut _ or ferry be constantly change and a long bamboo with a bit of white rag affix show where the boat and boatman be to be find in many instance the track be a mere cattle path and hundred of cross opening lead into the tall jungle grass be apt to bewilder and mislead the traveller during the dry season these jungle be the resort of great herd of cattle and tame buffalo which trample down the dry stalk and force they way into the innermost recess of the wilderness of grass which grow ten to twelve foot high if you once lose you path you may wander for mile until you weary horse be almost unable to stumble on in such a case the best way be to take it coolly and halloo till a herdsman or thatch-cutter come to you rescue the knowledge of the jungle display by these poor ignorant man be wonderful they know every gully and watercourse every ford and quicksand and they betray not the slightest sign of fear although they know that at any moment they may come across a herd of wild buffalo a savage rhinoceros or even a royal tiger the track of rhinoceros be often see but although i have frequently have these point out to I when out tiger shooting i only see two while i live in that district the first occasion be after a night of discomfort such as i have fortunately seldom experienced i have be away at a neighbouring factory in purneah some eighteen or twenty mile from my bungalow my companion have be my predecessor in the management and be suppose to be well acquaint with the country we have go over to one of the outwork across the river and i have receive charge of the place from he it be a lonely solitary spot the house be compose of grass wall plaster with mud and have not be use for some time f propose that we should ride over to see h to whom he would introduce I as he would be one of my nearest neighbor and would give we a comfortable dinner and bed which there be no chance of we procure where we be we plunge at once into the mazy labyrinth of the jungle and soon emerge on the high sandy downs stretch mile beyond mile along the southern bank of the ever-changing river have lose we way we get to the factory after dark but a friendly villager volunteer he service as guide and lead we safely to we destination after a cheerful evening with h we persuade he to accompany we back next day he take out he dog and we have a good course after a hare kill two jackal and send back the dog by the sweeper at burgamma the outwork we stop to _ tiffin _ on some cold fowl we have bring with we the old factory head man get we some milk egg and _ chupatty _ and about three in the afternoon we start for the head factory in a evil moment f propose that as we be near another outwork call _ fusseah _ we should diverge thither i could take over charge and we could thus save a ride on another day not know anything of the country i acquiesce and we reach fusseah in time to see the place and do all that be needful it be a miserable tumbledown little spot with four pair of vat it have formerly be a good working factory but the river have cut away most of its best land and completely wash away some of the village while the whole of the cultivation be fast relapsing into jungle ` debnarain singh ' the _ gomorsta _ or head man ask we to stay for the night as he say we could never get home before dark f however scout the idea and we resume we way the track for it could not be call a road lead we through one or two jungle village completely hide by the dense bamboo clump and long jungle grass you can not see a trace of habitation till you be fairly on the village and as the rice-field be border with long strip of tall grass the whole country present the appearance of a uniform jungle we get through the rice swamp the village and the grass in safety and as it be get dark emerge on the great plain of undulating ridgy sandbank that form the bed of the river during the annual flood we have we _ syce _ -lrb- groom -rrb- and two peon with we we have to ride over nearly two mile of sand before we could reach the _ ghat _ where we expect the ferry-boat and the main stream once cross we have only two mile further to reach the factory we be get both tired and hungry a heavy dew be fall and the night be raw and chill it be dark there be no moon to light we way and the star be obscure by the silently creep fog rise from the marshy hollow among the sand all at once f who be lead call out that we be off the path and before i could pull up my poor old tired horse be flounder in a quicksand up to the girth i throw myself off and try to wheel he round h be behind we and we cry to he to halt where he be i be sink at every movement up to the knee when the syce come to my rescue and take charge of the horse f 's syce run to extricate he master and horse the two peon keep call ` oh my father my father ' the horse snort and struggle desperately in the tenacious and treacherous quicksand but after a prolonged effort we all get safely out and rejoin h on the firm ridge we now halloo and shout for the boatman but beyond the swish of the rapid stream to we right or the plash of a fall bank as the swift current undermine it no sound answer we repeat call we be wet and weary but to go either backward or forward be out of the question we be off the path and the first step in any direction might lead we into another quicksand worse perhaps than that from which we have just extricate ourselves the horse be tremble in every limb the syce cower together and shiver with the cold we order the two peon to try and reach the ghat and see what have become of the boat while we await they return where we be the fog and darkness soon swallow they up and put the best face on we dismal circumstance that we could we light we pipe and extend we jaded limb on the damp sand for a time we could hear the shout of the peon as they halloo for the boatman and we listen anxiously for the response but there be none we could hear the purling swish of the rapid stream the crumble bank fall into the current with a distant splash occasionally a swift rush of wing overhead tell we of the arrowy flight of diver or teal far in the distance twinkle the gleam of a herdsman 's fire the faint tinkle of a distant bell or the subdue bark of a village dog for a moment alone break the silence at time the hideous chorus of a pack of jackal wake the echo of the night then at no great distance rise a hoarse booming cry swell on the night air and subside into a lengthen growl the syce start to they foot the horse snort with fear and as the roar be repeat follow closely by another to we left and seemingly nearer h exclaim ` by jove there be a couple of tiger ' sure enough so it be it be the first time i have hear the roar of the tiger in he own domain and i must confess that my sensation be not altogether pleasant we set about collect stick and what root of grass we could find but on the sand-flats everything be wet and it be so dark that we have to grope about on we hand and knee and pick up whatever we come across with great difficulty we manage to light a small fire and for about half-an-hour be nearly smother by try with inflated cheek to coax it into a blaze the tiger continue to call at interval but do not seem to be approach we it be a long weary wait we be cold wet hungry and tired f the cause of we misfortune have take off he saddle and with it for a pillow be now fast asleep h and i cower over the miserable sputter flame and long and wish for the morning it be a miserable night the hour seem interminable the dense volume of smoke from the water-sodden wood nearly choke we at last after some hour spend in this miserable manner we hear a faint halloo in the distance it be now past eleven at night we return the hail and bye-and-bye the peon return bring a boatman with they the lazy rascal at the ghat where we have propose cross have go home at nightfall leave they boat on the further bank we trusty peon have go five mile up the river through the thick jungle and bring a boat down with they from the next ghat to that where we be we now warily pick we way down to the edge of the bank the boat seem very fragile and the current look so swift and dangerous that we determine to go across first ourselves get the larger boat from the other side light a fire and then bring over the horse we embark accordingly leave the syce and horse behind we the peon and boatman pull the boat a long way up stream by a rope then shoot out we be carry swiftly down stream the dark shadow of the further bank seem at a great distance the boatman push vigorously at he bamboo pole the water rippled and gurgle and froth and eddy around half-a-dozen time we think we boat would topple over but at length we get safely across far below what we have propose as we landing place we find the boat all right and the boatman 's hut a mere collection of dry grass and a few old bamboo as it could be replace in a hour and the material lay all around we fire the hut which soon blaze up throw a weird lurid glow on bank and stream and disclose far on the other bank we weary nag and shiver syce look very bedraggled and forlorn indeed the leap and crackle of the flame and the genial warmth invigorate we a little and while i stay behind to feed the fire the other recross to bring the horse over with the previous fright however they long wait the blaze fire and be unaccustomed to boat at night the poor scared horse refuse to enter the boat the boat be flat-bottomed or broadly bulge with a bamboo platform strew with grass in the center as a rule they have no protect rail and even in the daytime when the current be strong and eddy numerous they be very dangerous for horse at all event the poor brute would not be lead on to the platform so there be nothing for it but to swim they across the boat be therefore tow a long way up the bank which on the farther side be nearly level with the current but where the hut have stand be steep and slushy and perhaps twenty foot high this be where the deepest water run and where the current be swiftest if the horse therefore miss the landing ghat or stage which be cut slope into the bank there be a danger of they be sweep away altogether and lose however we determine on make the attempt enter the water and hold the horse tightly by the head with a lead rope attach to be pay out in case of necessity the boat shoot out the horse paw the water enter deeper and deeper foot by foot into the swiftly rush silent stream so long as they be in they depth and have foot they be alright but when they reach the middle of the river the current rush with frightful velocity sweep they off they foot and boat and horse begin to go down stream the horse with lip apart show they tooth firmly set the lurid glare of the flame light up they strain eyeball the plashing of the water the dark rapid current flow noiselessly past the rock heave boat the dusky form of syce peon and boatman standing out clear in the ruddy fire-light against the utter blackness of the night compose a weird picture i can never forget the boat shot swiftly past the ghat and come with a thump against the bank it swing round into the stream again but the boatman have luckily manage to scramble ashore and he effort and mine unite haul on the mooring-rope suffice to bring she in to the bank the three struggle horse be yet in the current try bravely to stem the furious rush of the river the syce and my friend be hold hard to the tether-rope which be now at they full stretch it be a most critical moment have they let go the horse would have be sweep away to form a meal for the alligator they manage however to get in close to the bank and during which we think more than once the gallant nag would never emerge from the water they stagger up the bank drip tremble and utterly overcome with they exertion it be my first introduction to the treacherous koosee and i never again attempt to swim a horse across at night we lead the poor tired creature up to the fire heaping on fresh bundle of thatching-grass of which there be plenty lie about the syce then rub they down and shampoo they leg till they begin to take a little heart whinnying as we speak to they and caress they after rest for nearly a hour we replace the saddle and f who the narrow track we cross a rapid little stream a arm of the main river turn to the right progress a few hundred yard turn to the left and finally come to a dead stop have again lose we way we heap execration on the luckless peon 's head and i suggest that we should make for the main stream follow up the bank till we reach the next ghat where i know there be a cart-road lead to the factory otherwise we might wander all night in the jungle perhaps get into another quicksand or come to some other signal grief we accordingly turn round we could hear the swish of the river at no great distance and soon stumble over bush and burst through mat chump of grass drip with wet and utterly tired and dejected we reach the bank of the stream here we have no difficulty in follow the path the river be so swift that the only way boat be enable to get up stream to take down the inland produce be by have a few coolie or boatman to drag the boat up against the current by towing-line this be call _ goon _ the goon-rope be attach to the mast of the boat at the free end be a round bit of bamboo the towing-coolie place this against he shoulder and slowly and laboriously drag the boat up against the current we be now on this towing-path and after ride for nearly four mile we reach the ghat strike into the cart-road and without further misadventure reach the factory about four in the morning utterly fagged and worn out about eight in the morning my bearer wake I out of a deep sleep with the news that there be a _ gaerha _ that be a rhinoceros close to the factory we have some day previously hear it rumor that there be _ two _ rhinoceros in the _ battabarree _ jungle so i at once rouse my soundly-sleeping friend swallow a hasty morsel of toast and a cup of coffee we mount we pony send we gun on ahead and ride off for the village where the rhinoceros be report as we ride hurriedly along we could see native run in the same direction as ourselves and one of my man come up panting and breathless to confirm the news about the rhinoceros with the unwelcome addition that premnarain singh a young neighbouring zemindar have go in pursuit of it with he elephant and gun we hurry on and just then hear the distant report of a shot follow quickly by two more we try to take a short cut across country through some rice-field but we pony sink in the boggy ground and we have to retrace we way to the path by the time we get to the village we find a excited crowd of over a thousand native dancing and gesticulate round the prostrate carcase of the rhinoceros the baboo and he party have find the poor brute firmly imbed in a quicksand with organise effort they might have secure the prize alive and could have sell he in calcutta for at least a thousand rupee but they be too excited and blaze away three shot into the helpless beast ` many hand make light work ' so the crowd soon have the dead animal extricate roll he into the creek and float he down to the village where we find they already begin to hack and hew the flesh completely spoil the skin and properly complete the butchery we be terribly vex that we be too late but endeavor to stop the stupid destruction that be go on the body measure eleven foot three inch from the snout to the tail and stand six foot nine the horn be six and a half inch long and the girth a little over ten foot we put the best face on the matter congratulate the baboo with very bad grace and ask he to get the skin cut up properly cut in strip from the under part of the rib and along the belly the skin make magnificent riding-whip the boss on the shoulder and side be make into shield by the native elaborately ornament and much prize the horn however be the most coveted acquisition it be believe to have peculiar virtue and be popularly suppose by its mere presence in a house to mitigate the pain of maternity a rhinoceros horn be often hand down from generation to generation as a heirloom and when a birth be about to take place the anxious husband often get a loan of the precious treasure after which he have no fear for the safe issue of the labor the flesh of the rhinoceros be eat by all class it be one of the five animal that a brahmin be allow to eat by the _ shastras _ they be formerly much more common in these jungle but of late year very few have be kill when they take up they abode in a piece of jungle they be not easily dislodge they be fierce savage brute and do not scruple to attack a elephant when they be hard press by the hunter when they wish to leave a locality where they have be disturb they will make for some distant point and march on with dog and inflexible purpose some have be know to travel eighty mile in the twenty-four hour through thick jungle over river and through swamp and quicksand they sense of hearing be very acute and they be very easily rouse to fury one peculiarity often notice by sportsman be that they always go to the same spot when they want to obey the call of nature mound of they dung be sometimes see in the jungle and the track shew that the rhinoceros pay a daily visit to this one particular spot in nepaul and along the _ teraus _ or wooded slope of the frontier they be more numerous but ` jung bahadur ' the late ruler of nepaul would allow no one to shoot they but himself i remember the wail lament of a nepaul officer with whom i be out shooting when i happen to fire at and wind one of the protected beast it be in nepaul among a cluster of low woody hill with a brawling stream dash through the precipitous channel wear out of the rocky boulder-covered dell the rhinoceros be up the hill slightly above I and we be beat up for a tiger that we have see go ahead of the line in my eagerness to bag a ` rhino ' i quite forget the interdict and fire a express bullet into the shoulder of the animal as he stand broadside on stare stupidly at I he stagger and make as if he would charge down the hill the old ` major capt -lrb- = a -rrb- n ' as they call we sport host be shout out to I not to fire the _ mahout _ and beater be petrified with horror at my presumption i fancy they expect a immediate order for my decapitation or for my ear to be cut off at the very least but feel i might as well be ` in for a pound as for a penny ' i fire again and tumble the huge brute over with a bullet through the skull behind the ear the old officer be horror-stricken and would allow no one to go near the animal he would not even let I get down to measure it be terrify lest the affair should reach the ear of he formidable lord and ruler that he hurry we off from the scene of my transgression as quickly as he could the old major capt -lrb- = a -rrb- n be a curious character the government of nepaul be purely military all executive and judicial function be carry on by military officer after serve a certain time in the army they get reward for good service by be appoint to the executive charge of a district so far as i could make out they seem to farm the revenue much as be do in turkey they must send in so much to the treasury and anything over they keep for themselves they administration of justice be rough and ready fine corporal punishment and in the case of heinous crime mutilation and death be they penalty there be a tax of _ kind _ on all produce and licens to cut timber bring in a large revenue a protective tariff be levy on all goods or produce pass the frontier from british territory and no european be allow to travel in the country or to settle and trade there in the lower valley there be magnificent stretch of land suitable for indigo tea rice and other crop the stream be numerous moisture be plentiful the soil be fertile and the slope of the hill be cover with splendid timber a great quantity of which be cut and float down the gunduch bagmuttee koosee and other stream during the rainy season it be use principally for beam rafter and railway sleeper the people be jealous of intrusion and suspicious of stranger but as i be with a official they generally come out in great number to gaze as we pass through a village the country do not seem so thickly populate as in we territory and the cultivator have a more well-to-do look they possess vast number of cattle the house have conical roof and great quadrangular shed roof with a flat covering of thatch be erect all round the house for the protection of the cattle at night the tax must weigh heavily on the population the executive officer when he get charge of a district remove all the subordinate who have be act under he predecessor when i ask the old major if this would not interfere with the efficient administration of justice and the smooth working of he revenue and executive function he give a funny leer almost a wink and say it be much more satisfactory to have man of you own work under you the fact be that with he own man he could more securely wring from the ryot the uttermost farthing they could pay and be more certain of get he own share of the spoil with practically irresponsible power and only answerable directly to he immediate military superior a unscrupulous man may harry and harass a district pretty much as he choose we old major seem to be civil and lenient but in some district the exaction and extortion of the ruler have drive many of the hard-working nepaulese over the border into we territory we landholder or zemindar have vast area of untilled land be only too glad to encourage this immigration and give the exile whom they find hard-working industrious tenant long lease on easy term the new-comer be very independent and strenuously resist any encroachment on what they consider they rights if a attempt be make to raise they rent even equitably the land have increase in value they will resist the attempt ` tooth and nail ' and take every advantage the law afford to oppose it they be very fond of litigation and be mostly able to afford the expense of a lawsuit i generally find it answer better to call they together and reason quietly with they submit any point in dispute to a arbitration of party mutually select nearly all the river in nepaul be form principally from the melting of the snow on the higher range a vast body of water descend annually into the plain from the natural surface drainage of the country but the melting of the snow be the main source of the river system many of the hill stream and it be particularly observable at some season in the koosee have a regular daily rise and fall in the early morning you can often ford a branch of the river which by midday have become a swiftly-rolling torrent fill the channel from bank to bank the water be intensely cold and few or no fish be to be find in the mountain stream of nepaul when the nepaulese come down to the plain on business pleasure or pilgrimage they great treat be a mighty banquet of fish for two or three _ anna _ a fish of several pound weight can easily be purchase they revel on this unwonted fare eat to repletion and very frequently make themselves ill in consequence when jung bahadur come down through chumparun to attend the _ durbar _ of the lament earl mayo cholera break out in he camp bring on simply by the enormous quantity of fish often not very fresh or wholesome which he guard and camp follower consume large quantity of dry fish be send up to nepaul and exchange for rice and other grain or horn hide and blanket the fish-drying be do very simply in the sun it be generally leave till it be half putrid and taint the air for mile the sweltering half-rotting mass pack in filthy bag and sling on pony or bullock be send over the frontier to some village bazaar in nepaul the track of a consignment of this horrible filth can be recognize from very far away the perfume hover on the road and as you be ride up and get the first sniff of the putrid odour you know at once that the nepaulese market be be recruit by a _ fresh _ accession of very _ stale _ fish if the taste be at all equal to the smell the rankest witch broth ever brew in reek cauldron would probably be preferable over the frontier there seem to be few road merely bullock track most of the transporting of goods be do by bullock and intercommunication must be slow and costly i believe that near katmandoo the capital the road and bridge be good and keep in tolerable repair there be a arsenal where they manufacture modern munitions of war they soldier be well discipline fairly well equip and form excellent fighting material we policy of annexation so far as indium be concern may perhaps be now consider as finally abandon we have no desire to annex nepaul but surely this system of utter isolation of jealous exclusion at all hazard of english enterprise and capital might be break down to a mutual community of interest a full and free exchange of product and a reception by nepaul without fear and distrust of the benefit we capitalist and pioneer could give the country by open out its resource and establish the industry of the west on its fertile slope and plain i be no politician and know nothing of the secret spring of policy that regulate we dealings with nepaul but it do seem somewhat weak and puerile to allow the nepaulese free access to we territory and a unprotected market in we town for all they produce while the british subject be rigorously exclude from the country he production saddle with a heavy protective duty and the representative of we government himself treat more as a prisoner in honorable confinement than as the accredit ambassador of a mighty empire i may be utterly wrong there may be weighty reason of state for this condition of thing but it be a general feeling among englishman in indium that _ we _ have to do all the give and we oriental neighbor do all the take the un-official english mind in indium do not see the necessity for the painfully deferential attitude we invariably take in we dealings with native state the time have surely come when oriental mistrust of we intention should be stoutly battle with there be room in nepaul for hundred of factory for tea-gardens fruit-grove spice-plantation woollen-mill saw-mill and countless other industry mineral product be report of unusual richness in the great central valley the climate approach that of england the establishment of productive industry would be a work of time but so long as this ridiculous policy of isolation be maintain and the exclusion of english tourist sportsman or observer carry out in all its present strictness we can never form a adequate idea of the resource of the country the nepaulese themselves can not progress i be convince that a frank and unconstrained intercourse between european and native would create no jealousy and antagonism but would lead to the development of a country singularly bless by nature and open a wide field for anglo-saxon energy and enterprise it do seem strange with all we vast territory of hindustan accurately map out and know road and railway canal and embankment intersect it in all direction that this interesting corner of the globe lie contiguous to we territory for hundred of mile should be less known than the interior of africa or the barren solitude of the ice-bound arctic region in these rich valley hundred of mile of the finest and most fertile land in asia lie cover by dense jungle wait for labor and capital for the present we have enough to do in we own possession to reclaim the uncultured waste but consider the rapid increase of population the avidity with which land be take up the daily increase use of all modern labour-saving appliance the time must very shortly come when capital and energy will need new outlet and one of the most promising of these be in nepaul the rapid change which have come over the face of rural indium especially in these border district within the last twenty year might well make the most thoughtless pause land have increase in value more than two-fold the price of labor and of produce have keep more than equal pace machinery be whir and clank where a few year ago a steam whistle would have startle the native out of they wit with cheap easy and rapid communication a journey to any of the great city be now think no more of than a trip to a distant village in the same district be think of twenty year ago everywhere be the sign of progress new industry be open up jungle be fast disappear agriculture have wonderfully improve and wherever a indigo factory have be build progress have take the place of stagnation industry and thrift that of listless indolence and shiftless apathy a spirit have move in the valley of dry bone and have clothe with live flesh the gaunt skeleton produce by ignorance disease and want the energy and intelligence of the planter have breathe on the stagnant water of the hindoo intellect the breath of life and the living tide be heave full of activity purge by its resistless ever-moving pulsation the formerly stagnant mass of its impurity and make it a life-giving sea of active industry and progress let any unprejudiced observer see for himself if it be not so let he go to those district where british capital and energy be not employ let he leave the plant district and go up to the waste of oudh or the purely native district of the many year ago there may have be much in the past over which we would willingly draw the veil but at the present moment i firmly believe that the planter of behar -- and i speak as a observant student of what have be go on in indium -- have do more to elevate the peasantry to rouse they into vitality and to improve they in every way than all the other agency that have be at work with the same end in view the indian government to all appearance must always work in extreme it never seem to hit the happy medium the lieutenant-governor for the time be impress every department under he too strongly with he own individuality the planter who be a intelligent and independent body of man have seemingly always be obnoxious to the idea of a perfectly despotic and irresponsible ruler in spite however but for the assistance it give in money or seed or plough bullock and implement of husbandry many a struggle hardworking tenant would inevitably go to the wall or become inextricably entangled in the court the official in the indigo district know this and as a rule be very friendly with the planter but not long since a official be afraid to dine at a planter 's house fear he might be accuse of planter proclivity in no other country in the world would the same jealousy of man who open out and enrich a country and who be loyal intelligent and educate citizen be display but there be high quarter in which the old feeling of the east indium company that all who be not in the service must be adventurer and interloper seem not wholly to have die out that there have be abuse no one deny but for year past the majority of the planter in tirhoot chupra and chumparun and in the indigo district generally not merely the manager but the proprietor and agent have be laudably and loyally stir and manager on indigo estate the rate for payment of indigo to cultivator nearly double although price for the manufactured article remain stationary in well manage factory the forcible seizure of cart and plough and the enforcement of labor which be a old charge against planter be unknown and the payment of tribute common under the old feudal system and style _ furmaish _ have be allow to fall individual instance resort may have be have to unworthy trick to harass the ryot the factory middle-men have often be oppressor and tyrant but as a body the indigo planter of the present day have sternly set they face to put down these oppression and have honestly strive to mete out even-handed justice to they tenant and dependant with the spread of education and intelligence the development rapacity instead of do he business on a equitable commercial basis give every man he due rely on skill energy industry and integrity to promote the best interest of he factory gain the esteem and affection of he people by liberality kindness and strict justice it can never be expect that a ryot can grow indigo at a loss to himself or at a lower rate of profit than that which the cultivation of of weapon with the most daring courage and the most imperturbable coolness i think a man no fair match for a tiger in he native jungle there be man now live who have shoot number of tiger on foot but the numerous fatal accident record every year plainly shew the danger of such a mode of shooting in central indium in the north-west indeed in most district where elephant be not easily procurable it be customary to erect _ mychan _ or bamboo platform on tree a line of beater with tom-tom drum firework and other means for create a din be then send into the jungle to beat the tiger up to the platform on which you sit and wait this be often a successful mode if you secure a advantageous place but accident to the beater be very common and it be at best a weary and vexatious mode of shooting as after all you trouble the tiger may not come near you _ mychan _ or give you the slightest glimpse of he beautiful skin i have only be out after tiger on foot on one occasion it be in the sal jungle in oudh a neighbor of mine a most intimate and dear friend whom i have nickname the ` general ' and a young friend fullerton be with I a tigress and cub be report to be in a dense patch of _ nurkool _ jungle on the bank of the creek which divide the general 's cultivation from mine the nurkool be a tall feathery-looking cane very much relish by elephant it grow in dense brake and generally in damp boggy ground afford complete shade and shelter for wild animal and be a favorite haunt of pig wolf tiger and buffalo we have only one elephant the use of which fullerton have get from a neighbouring baboo it be not a staunch animal so we put one of we man in the howdah with a plentiful supply of bomb a kind of native firework enclose in a clay case which burn like a huge squib and set fire to the jungle along with the elephant we have a line of about one hundred coolie and several man with drum and tom-tom fullerton take the side nearest the river as it be possible the brute might sneak out that way and make she escape along the bank the general 's shekarry remain behind in rear of the line of beater in case the tigress might break the line and try to escape by the rear my _ gomasta _ the general and myself then take up position behind tree all along the side of the glade or dell in which be the bit of nurkool jungle it be a small basin sloping gently down to the creek from the sal jungle which grow up dark and thick all around a margin of close sward as green and level as a billiard-table encircle the glade and in the basin the thick nurkool grow up close dense and high like a rustling barrier of live green in the center be the decaying stump of a mighty forest monarch with its withered arm stretch out they bleached and shattered length far over the wave feathery top of the nurkool below the general and i cut down some branch which we stick in the ground before we i have a fall log in front of I on which i rest my gun i have a naked _ kookree _ ready to hand for we be sure that the tigress be in the swamp and i do not know what might happen i do not half like this style of shooting and wish i be safely seat on the back of ` jorrock ' my faithful old bhaugulpore elephant the general whistle as a sign for the beat to begin the coolie dash into the thicket the stately elephant slowly force he ponderous body through the crash sway brake the rattle of the tom-tom and rumble of the drum mingle with the hoarse shout and cry of the beater the fiery rush of sputter flame and the loud report as each bomb burst with the huge volume of blind smoke and the scent of gunpowder that come on the breeze tell we that the bomb be do they work the jungle be too green to burn but the firework raise a dense sulphurous smoke which penetrate among the tall stem of the nurkool and by the wave and crash of the tall sway cane the heaving of the howdah with the red puggree of the peon and the gleaming of the stave and weapon we could see that the beat be advance as they near the large withered tree in the center of the brake the elephant curl up he trunk and trumpet this be a sure sign there be game afoot we could see the peon in the howdah lean over the front bar and eagerly peer into the recess of the thicket before he he light one of the bomb and hurl it right up against the hole of the tree it hiss and sputter and the smoke come curl over the reed in dense volume a roar follow that make the valley ring again we hear a swift rush the elephant turn tail and flee madly away crash through the matted brake that crackle and tear under he tread the howdah sway wildly and the peon cling tenaciously on to the top bar with all he desperate might the _ mahout _ or elephant-driver try in vain to check the rush of the frightened brute but after repeat sound whack on the head he get she to stop and again turn round meantime the cry and shout have cease and the beater come pour from the jungle by two and three like the frightened coolie and the chicken-hearted elephant vacate the jungle as fast as they leg could carry they this happen twice or thrice the tigress charge every time but would not leave she safe cover the elephant be very characteristic of he whole nature to see he stealthily crouch or crawl silently and sneakingly after a herd of cattle dodge behind every clump of bush or tuft of grass run swiftly along the high bank of a watercourse and sneak under the shadow border of a belt of jungle be to understand he cunning and craftiness he attitude when he be crouch for the final bind be the embodiment of suppleness and strength all he action be graceful and half display and half conceal beneath they symmetry and elegance the tremendous power and deadly ferocity that lurk beneath for a short distance he be possess of great speed and with a few short agile bound he generally manage to overtake he prey if baffle in he first attack he retire growl to lie in wait for a less fortunate victim he onset be so fierce and sudden the animal he select for he prey be generally take at a great disadvantage and be seldom in a position to make any strenuous or availing resistance deliver the numbing blow with he mighty fore paw he fasten on the throat of the animal he have fell and invariably try to tear open the jugular vein this be he practice in nearly every case and it shew a wonderful instinct for select the most deadly spot in the whole body of he luckless prey when he have get hold of he victim by the throat he lie down hold on to the bleeding carcase snarl and growl and fasten and withdraw he claw much as a cat do with a rat or mouse some writer say he then proceed to drink the blood but this be just one of those broad general assertion which require proof in some case he may quench he thirst and gratify he appetite for blood by drink it from the gush vein of he quiver victim but in many case i know from observation that the blood be not drunk if the tiger be very hungry he then begin he feast tear huge fragment of flesh from the dead body and not unusually swallow they whole if he be not particularly hungry he drag the carcase away and hide it in some well-known spot this be to preserve it from the hungry talon and tooth of vulture and jackal he commonly remain on guard near he _ cache _ until he have acquire a appetite if he can not conveniently carry away he quarry because of its bulk or the nature of the ground or from be disturb he return to the place at night and satisfy he appetite tiger can sneak crouchingly along as fast as they can trot and it be wonderful how silently they can steal on they prey they seem to have some stray provident fit and on occasion make provision for future want there be instance on record of a tiger drag a _ kill _ after he for mile over water and through slush and weed and feast on the carcase day after he have kill it it be a fact now establish beyond a doubt that he will eat carrion and putrid flesh but only from necessity and not from choice on one occasion my friend put up a tigress during the rain when there be few cattle in the _ derah _ or plain near the river she have kill a pig and be eagerly devour the carcase when she be disturbed snarl and growl she make off with a leg of pork in she mouth when a bullet end she career they seem to prefer pork and venison to almost any other kind of food and no doubt pig and deer be they natural and usual prey the influx however of vast herd of cattle and the consequent presence of man drive away the wild animal and at all event make they more wary and more difficult to kill finding domestic cattle unsuspicious and not very formidable foe the tiger contents himself at a pinch with beef and judge from he ravages he come to like it get bolder by impunity he venture in some strait to attack man he find he a very easy prey he find the flesh too perhaps not unlike he favorite pig henceforth he become a ` man-eater ' the most dreaded scourge and pestilent plague of the district he sometimes find a old boar a tough customer and never venture to attack a buffalo unless it be grazing alone and away from the rest of the herd when buffalo be ` tabby ' cat they begin they operation in earnest invariably on the buttock a leopard generally eat the inner portion of the thigh first a wolf tear open the belly and eat the intestine first a ' and go on to say : -- ` as a rule the stripe of a royal bengal be single and dark the skull be widely different from that of he brother the hill tiger be low in the crown wider in the jaw rather flat in comparison and the brain-pan longer with a sloping curve at the end the crest of the brain-pan be a concave curve ` the hill tiger be much more massively build squat and thick set heavier in weight and larger in bulk with shorter tail and very large and powerful neck head and shoulder the stripe generally be double and of a more brownish tinge with fawn color between the double stripe the skull be high in the crown and not quite so wide the brain-pan be shorter and the crest slightly convex or nearly straight and the curve at the end of the skull rather abrupt ` they never grow so long 160 skull ` the difference be better mark in tiger than in tigress the bengal variety be not as a rule as ferocious as the hill tiger be more supple and cunning they can easier evade they pursuer by elephant and frequently six or eight howdah these expedition be very pleasant and we live luxuriously for real sport ten elephant and two or three try comrade -- not more -- be much better with a short easily-worked line that can turn and double and follow the tiger quickly and dog he every movement you can get far better sport and bring more to bag than with a long unwieldy line that take a requirement of the table one act as paymaster and keeper of account while the most experienced be unanimously elect captain and take general direction of every movement of the line he decide on the plan of operation for the day give each he place in the line and for the time become a irresponsible autocrat whose word be law and against whose decision there be no appeal scout be send out during the night and bring in report from all part of the jungle in the early morning while we be discuss _ chota baziree _ we early morning meal if tiger be report or a kill have be discover we form line in or do whatever we choose on the flank be generally post the best shot of the party the captain as a rule keep to the center of the line frequently one man and elephant be send on ahead to some opening or dry water-bed to see that no cunning tiger sneak away unseen this vedette be call _ naka _ all experienced sportsman employ a naka and not unfrequently where the ground be difficult two be send ahead the naka be a most important post and the holder will often get a lucky shot at some wary veteran try to sneak off and may perhaps bag the only tiger of the day the mere knowledge that there be a elephant on ahead will often keep tiger from try to get away they prefer to face the known danger of the line behind to the unknown danger in front and in all case where there be a big party a naka should be send on ahead tiger can be and be shoot on the koosee plain all the year round but the big hunt take place in the month of march April and may when the hot west wind be blow and when the jungle have get considerably trample down by the herd of cattle grazing in the tangled wilderness of tall grass innumerable small path shew where the cattle wander backward and forward through the labyrinth of the jungle in the howdah we carry ample supplies of vesuvian we light and drop these as they blaze into the dry grass and withered leaf as we move along and soon a mighty wall of roar flame behind we attest the presence of the destroy element we go diagonally up wind and the flame and smoke thus surge and roar and curl and roll in dense blind volume to the rear and leeward of we line the roaring of the flame sound like the madden surf of a angry sea dashing in thunder against a iron-bound coast the leap flame mount up in fiery column illuminate the fleecy cloud of smoke with a unearthly glare the noise be deafen at times some of the elephant get quite nervous at the fierce roar of the flame behind and try to bolt across country the fire serve two good purpose it burn up the old withered grass make room for the fresh succulent sprout to spring and it keep all the game in front of the line drive the animal before we as they be afraid to break back and face the roaring-wall of flame a seething surge sea of flame several mile long encircle the whole country in its fiery belt sweep along at night with the roar of a storm-tossed sea the flame flicker swell and leap up in the dark night the fiery particle rush along amid cloud of lurid smoke and the glare of the serpent-like line redden the horizon be one of those magnificent spectacles that can only be witness at rare interval among the experience of a sojourn in indium word fail to depict its grandeur and the utmost skill of dor could not render on canvas the weird unearthly magnificence of a jungle fire at the culmination of its force and fury in beat the elephant be several yard apart and stand in the howdah you can see the slightest motion of the grass before you unless indeed it be virgin jungle quite untrodden and perhaps higher than you elephant in such high dense cover tiger will sometimes lie up and allow you to go clean past they in such a case you must fire the jungle and allow the blaze to beat for you it be common for young over eager sportsman to fire at move jungle trust to a lucky chance for hit the move animal this be useless waste of powder they fail to realize the great length of the sway grass and invariably shoot over the game the animal hear the crash of the bullet through the dense thicket overhead and immediately stop and you lose all idea of he whereabouts when you see a animal move before you in long jungle it should be you object to follow he slowly and patiently till you can get a sight of he and see what sort of beast he be firing at the move grass be worse than useless keep as close behind he as you can make sign for the other elephant to close in stick to you quarry never lose sight of he for a instant be ready to seize the first the current and not in indian file the strongest elephant should be up stream as they help to break the force of the current for the weaker and smaller animal down below it be a fine sight to see some border on the morung the herd have be well-nigh exterminate little attention be pay to breed in some district such as the mooteeharree and mudhobunnee division fine cart-bullock be breed carefully handle and tend and fetch high price in kurruchpore beyond the gange in bhaugulpore district cattle of a small breed hardy active staunch and strong be breed in great number and be hold in great estimation for agricultural requirement but in these koosee jungle the bull be often ill-bred weedy brute and the cow be much in excess of a fair proportion of bull a deal of in-breeding take place unmatured young bull roam about with the herd and the result be a crowd of cattle that succumb to the first ailment so that the land be litter with they bone the bullock be indispensable to the indian cultivator bull calf be prize take care of well nurture and well feed the cow calf be pretty much leave to take care of themselves they be thin miserable half-starved brute and the short-sighted ryot through its officer experimental farm etc try to get good sire stock for both horse and cattle but as long as the dam be bad -- mere weed without blood bone muscle or stamen the produce must be bad as a pretty well establish and general rule the ryot look after they bullock -- they recognize they value and appreciate they utility but the cow fare badly and from all i have myself see and from the concurrent testimony of many observant friend in the rural district i should say that the breed have become much deteriorated old planter constantly tell you that such cattle as they use to get be not now procurable for love or money within the last twenty year price have more than double because the demand for good plough-bullock have be more urgent as a consequence of increase cultivation and the supply be not equal to the demand attention to the matter be imperative and planter would be wise in they own interest to devote a little time and trouble to disseminate sound idea about the selection of breed stock and the principle of rearing and raise stock among they ryot and dependant every factory should be able to breed its own cattle and supply its own requirement for plough and cart-bullock it would be cheaper in the end and it would undoubtedly be a blessing to the country to raise the standard of cattle use in agricultural work to return from this digression we plod on and
